THE RED RAGE.
COMEDY.
They parted, with clasps of hands,
And kisses and burning tears.
They met in a foreign land,
After some twenty years:
Met as acquaintances meet,
Smilingly, tranquil-eyed—
Not even the least little beat
Of the heart, upon either side 1
They chatted of this and that,
The nothings that make up life ;
Bhe in a Gainsborough hat,
And he in black for his wife.
Ah, what a comedy this!
Neither was hurt, it appears:
Yet once she had leaned to his kiss,
And once he had known her tears!
THOMAS BAILEY ALDRICH.
The Standpoint of Criticism.
The worst of it is that (as a writer in the Satur-
day Review remarked recently) “an art must be
judged by standards rigorous in the measure of
its exalted claim.” “An art”—and an artist,
and any example of art, in any field. This ex-
plains the disappointment of many a book-maker
who gets scant praise for the collected verses or
articles which earned high praise when they
appeared singly in newspapers. Consciously or
unconsciously, most readers justly apply different
oriteria in different cases. From a newspaper we
only expect good journalism. From a book we
have a right to expect good Literature. And “an
art must be judged by standards rigorous in the
measure of its exalted claim.”
» * * *
So the “Exhibition of Small Works and
Sketches ” by “ The Society of Artists ” (Sydney)
must inevitably receive criticism different from
that due to “A Society of Students of Art ” —the
title which would leave a reasonably accurate im-
pression of the members and their pr rformances.
Of course, every artist remains a student in the
special sense all his life-time, seeing that Man is
finite and Art practically infinite. But there
is nevertheless a time when an artist may
in the general sense be said to cease being
& student, a stage of hi 3 development when he
may fairly be assumed to merge the student in the
artist. And that time, that stage, it is obvious
many of the members of “The Society of
Artists ” have not yet reached. They are still
essentially students, absorbed in a struggle with
the rudiments of technique, and quite unable to
give effective expression to artistic ideas—even if
they possess them, which, in some cases, seems a
little doubtful.
* • * *
Now, the technique of Painting, for example, is
ft thing difficult to acquire, and a student natur-
ally considers each conquest in the field of
technique in relation to himself, and estimates its
value in proportion to the pains it has cost him to
make it. But his standpoint, right in the limited
and personal sense, is in the wider sense artisti-
cally wrong. His work must be critically judged,
not in relation to himself, but in relation to the
work of others, in relation to the whole sum of
artistic achievement, and even of conceivable
artistic achievement. To the student, it is a great
matter when he has learned to draw an arm inside
a sleeve, or to make a red light out of red paint—
and some of the members of “The Society of
Artists ” will never succeed thus far. But these
results are very small indeed compared with the
total results attained by Painting. The thought
of what little has been done should always be
sobered by the thought of how much remains
to do.
• » • *
It is a little strange that the beginner in Paint-
ing should so constantly adopt the wrong stan-
dard, the personal standard, of acquirement. The
beginner in Music does this hardly as much ; the
beginner in Literature does so much less. There
are a score of bright young Australian writers
with at least as great a capacity for artistically
impressing other minds as any score of “The
Society of Artists’ ” exhibitors ; but, though their
heads may swell a little now and then, they do not
call themselves Men of Letters, or Women of
Letters, and the best of them are thoroughly dis-
satisfied with their performances. They look up
a stage higher— to Kipling, say; and Kipling in
turn is dissatisfied and looks up to Hardy or
Meredith. And the Australian writer will listen
to depreciation of his work—though he doesn’t
always pretend to look pleased ; but would it be
safe to take aside , or , or , of
the Society of Artists, and point out that this
picture is destitute of perspective, and that one
of expression, and that other is the crude perform-
ance of a tyro just learning to handle his brushes
—and all three are wanting in “fundamental
brain-power ” and artistic stimulus ?
* • » »
These cavils, to be sure, rest largely upon one’s
definition of the word artist. But it is impossible
for anyone who considers that an artist is, and
•hould be, a creator of beautiful ideals, to visit
without disappointment “The Society of Artists’”
exhibition. There is not one beautiful ideal in
the catalogue. There are some examples of
olever workmanship ; many transcripts of natural
objects and effects—good, bad, and indiffer-
ent; a few pictures which have sufficient
value to serve as illustrations—one could see
them and recollect them with pleasure and
pass from them without regret ; but hardly
half-a-dozen things fine enough to take home and
live with—to hang on a wall or stand on a
bracket and be seen every day, and every day be
a source of inspiration, of consolation, er of
delight. Yet this exhibition has a particular end
and aim—it is held “ by way of an experiment ”
to settle an alleged controversy between the
artists and the public. The former are said to
complain that there is a “ want of desire shown
by those well able to afford to possess themselves
of examples of local talent ” (the “ Artists ” are
quite sure about the “talent”); the latter, that
high prices have hitherto deterred them from
purchasing. And here is the writer, one of the
public, declaring that as works of art merely,
apart from technical, local, or personal considera-
tions, there are only some half-dozen of the 128
exhibits which he would count better than decora-
tive lumber ! So that, so far as one of the public
is concerned, “ the Society of Artists’ ” plaint
begs the question. The issue is not whether the
public will purchase artistic work at its own price,
but whether it will purchase inartistic work at
any price. If, instead of calling themselves
“ Artists,” the exhibitors ranked themselves as
‘ Students of Art’’—which most of them really
are —the standard of criticism shifts at once and
agreeably. Though no amount of gilding by
Governor Hampden will make it a satisfactory
sovereign, a shilling is excellent value for twelve
pence. One could praise the admirable efforts of
“Students” where the poor achievements of
“ Artists ” leave no option but to blame and re-
gret.
‘ Yellowbush’ writes—
How much longer are we to go on counting immigrants
and Australians, indiscriminately, as Australian authors?
It is important that a distinction should be made if we
wish to see Australia as it is. The immigrant’s point of
view is naturally quite different from the nativ e’s. This
is why the average writing-man thinks it necessary to
dwell on the melancholy aspect of Australian scenery.
Melancholy is mainly subjective. What is strange and
gloomy to the ‘ new-chum ’ is cheerful and homelike to
the native. But the 1 new-chum ’ got the first say, and his
view of things has colored much of our later literature.
In a recent ‘Red Page’ discussion Clarke and Gordon
and Domett were singled out as being more or less typical
of Australasian literature. Undoubtedly both Gordon
and Clarke were deeply influenced by their life on this
side of the Line, but they remained English for all that.
Gordon’s melancholy is deepened by the memories of
his Engli- life. After all he is an alien, weaving an
English past and an Australian present into Swinburnian
rhymes. Much of his work has no relation to Australia,
and is a mere echo of Swinburne. For instance, the most
striking lines in the ‘ Rhyme of Joyous Guard ’ (‘ If
ever I fought as a man should fight . . . ’) are plagiarised
from ‘ Les Noyades’ in the first series of the ‘Poems
and Ballads.’ ‘How we beat the Favorite’ is certainly
not Swinburnian, but is it poetry ?
Again, in what sense is Domett a poet of Maoriland?
Born in London, a student at Cambridge, a writer for
Blackwood, an intimate friend of Browning, he had pub-
lished two volumes of poetry before he emigrated at the
age of 31. The fact that he lived twenty years in M.L.,
and wrote poetry there, no more makes him a M.L. poet
than it makes him a Maori. One might as well claim
Henry Kingsley as an Australian novelist, because he
spent five years on the gold diggings and wrote ‘Geoffrey
Hamlvn.’ The true Australian poet must be born and
bred in Australia. All others are counterfeits.
* * » •
Writes ‘ Splodger ’ —
‘ Kipling is distinguished by the local color of his
writings. By turns he is a rookie or a color-ser-
geant, a Paythan or a potentate—“ Ung a scriber of
bone,” a “ gray Gothavn ’speckshioner,” or a fleet-
engineer. In the concluding poem of his “ Seven Seas ”
he deals with artists, and reading his verses from a
splodger’s point of view I am compelled to wonder if his
fluent remarks about “thrust blocks,” ‘‘crank-pins,”
"gander-heads,” and “ scuttlebutt,” are as ohaky to the
real M'Andrews as his ethics of art seem to me. This
is how a painter would have written the second and last
stanzas of the poem, interpolating a third to make a
total of four. Stanza first is perfect—
L’ENVOI.
When Earth’s last picture is painted and the tubes are
twisted and dried,
When the oldest colors have faded, and the youngest
critic has died,
We shall rest, and, faith, we shall need it—lie down for
an aeon or two,
Till the Master of All Good Workmen shall put us to work
anew.
(This is Kipling’s second stanza—)
And those that were good shall be happy: they shall sit
in a golden chair ;
They shall splash at a ten-league canvas with brushes of
comets’ hair ;
They shall find real saints to draw from—Magdalene,
Peter, and Paul ;
They shall work for an age at a sitting and never be tired
at all.
(And this is mine—)
And those that were good shall be happy, and sit in a
golden chair,
And splash on a ten-league canvas with brushes of
comets’ hair,
And they shall have saints for models— Magdalene, Mary,
and Paul—
And work for an age at a sitting, and never be weary
all.
(My third verse—new —)
Neither shall subjects be lacking—landscape, tigure or
sea—
Sunrise, grey day, or sunset, just as their fancies be ;
And brushes shall not need washing, and tubes shall be
always full,
Effects shall be fixed by the artist on pressing a knob in
his stool.
(Kipling’s third—)
And only the Master shall praise us, and only the Master
shall blame ;
And no one shall work for money, and no one shall work
for fame.
But each for the joy of the working, and each, in his
separate star,
Shall draw the Thing as he sees It for the God of Things
as They Are!
(And my fourth—)
And the Master shall smile in approval, calling each
painter by name,
And though no one shall work for money, yet sales shall
be all the same;
And each for the joy of the working, in his own parti*
cular star,
Shall paint as he dam well pleases and laugh with tbeg Ms
that are.
Gibbon and his History.
Among the visionary persons who presented
themselves to William Blake was the man who
built the Pyramids. In the volumes of Gibbon’s
Autobiography and Letters, admirably edited by
Mr. Murray and Mr. Prothero, we make acquaint-
ance with the life of his English eighteenth-
century descendant. They enhance our wonder
in presence of the builder and his stupendous
erection. Compared with the “Decline and
Fall,” Carlyle’s “Frederick” looks a slender
piece of architecture ; yet in the latter what
clatter of bricks, what creaking of cranes, what
piling of scaffolds, what din of hammers, what
ring of chisels, what groans of hodmen, what
shouts of the overseer! Gibbon’s stately structure
rises not like an Eastern enchanted palace in a
single night: nearly 16 years were spent in its con-
struction; butitrisesin a silence and a solitude that
impress us with a sense of the sublime. Steadily
and noiselessly the great blocks are quarried and
shaped and moved into their several places. Year
after year the vast monument ascends with no
clamor of workmen, and at length the topmost
stone is laid. It is designed and accomplished by
a single brain and a solitary artificer. And the
man who built the Pyramid is a little, obese,
gouty gentleman, who can saunter into Almack’s
to learn the news, give his afternoons to Madame
Necker, run over to Paris and see the spectacles,
sit up all night in the House —“the general Pan-
demonium to give a vote about which he does
not greatly care, or at Lausanne can entertain his
friends at early dinners, read some amusing book,
play at chess, look into the coffee-house, appear at
assemblies, indulge in his half-crown rubber of
whist, and be comfortably in bed about eleven.
Such was the man who built the Pyramid. . . . ’
* * » *
The mass of additions to the previously published
letters of Gibbon is large. Our conception of the
man remains unaltered ; but the outline is filled in
with pleasant detail. We had known that Gibbon
was amiable ; how otherwise could his ironv have
been so tranquil in its superiority? But we see
him in his letters constant and kind through all
the incidents of pleasure and of physical or
financial discomfort. His father had squandered
the resources on which his own ease and leisure
depended, and at'the same time was ready to re-
proach a son whose youthful extravagances were
petty. Gibbon is always respectful and reason-
able in his self-justification. He was for a moment
disposed to regard his stepmother as “a personal
and domestic enemy ”; not that he quite appre-
hended “ the bowl or the dagger,” but he “well
knew that the odium, noverraie was proverbial in
the language of antiquity ” ; and his stepmother
became the friend of all his life. His friendship
with Lord Sheffield was fraternal in its warmth
and its ease. For one who would carry through
long toil, a fortunate substitute for passion is
affection, without agitated hours, exaltations or
declensions.
• * * *
Gibbon, indeed, was happily framed for intel-
lectual effort and achievement. His bodily con-
stitution made sedentary habits sit 'light upon
him. Why should he climb a mountain when he
could enjoy the charming prospect from his terrace
and feel that four thousand agreeable friends were
a few yards off in his library ? His temper was
reasonable. He made no extravagant demands on
others, knew no ecstasies, and endured no de-
spairs. He was never whirled above or depressed
below the plateau on which men lay broad foun-
dations. He wasted none of his force in malignity ;
he had no heart-burnings of literary jealousy!
He did not seek to reform the world ; he looked
on it with an amused interest at the spectacle, and
let the world go its way. He was not a victim of
religious joys and terrors ; the gout was a suffi-
cient penance for any sins of dining not wisely
but too well. The process of his conversion to
the Koman Communion was simply one of argu-
ment. He fell by a noble hand—that of the
author of the ' A ariations." There was no moral
crisis accompanying his change of faith. Bayle,
with whom he compares himself, had the
pagandist zeal of a convert, and wore out P h°'
religious ardour m a short-lived enfchuria«
Gibbon retraced his steps gently and with!
noise. In addition to these advantages for ° U ”
tinuity of tranquil labour, he had the
of moderate wealth, as far removed from m ?
as from the servitude of riches. Many f fT y
letters are concerned with financial affairs •hi a
never really knew the cruel inroads of anx, t
which commonly beset the career of letters T V
a 'vise master-builder, he accumulated his material
before he began to lay stone on
Finally, the limitations of his mind aided V
in his progress. No problems of metaphysics ®
embarrassing questions of doubtful psycho]’ ,, 0
delayed his advance He was not perplexed b? a
multitude of sympathies ; he did not hesitate ova,
the complexities of human character. He wish h
to unrol a magnificent panorama, and his gen’
as an historical artist enabled him to determine
the large lines and lay on the colors without th
uncertainties of niggling. 119
* * * .
It Gibbon had a passion, it must have been
that of a literary artist. To accomplish a raaie?
tic design—this was his ambition. Cool on alirin«k
every other occasion, he warms with
excitement as his work draws towards a dr,*!
“ Hills began to rise on hills, and Alps on Aim
and I found my journey far more tedious and tod
some than I had imagined.” But the difficulties!
only stimulated him to new and extraordinary in
dustty; he was “straining for the goal
length the last line of the last page wl!
written. And the event was taken with
a quietude of reserved excitement which
was worthy of Gibbon and of the great moment
Fxultation was tempered with regret. Who
that feels a joy in human achievement can
forget his words of dignified pride and gentl«
melancholy? “The air was temperate theTkv
was serene, the silver orb of the moon was reflected
from the water, and all nature was silent. I will
not dissemble the first emotions of joy on tha
recovery of my freedom, and perhaps the e«tab
lishment of my fame. But my pride was soon
humbled, and a sober melancholy was spread over
my mind by the idea that I had taken an ever
lasting leave of an old and agreeable companion
and that whatsoever might b“ the future fate of
my history, the life of the historian must be short
and precarious.”
• • • •
Silence and moonlight, with joy in achievement
and a touch of human regret—these were mow
becoming than a fanfaronnade of trumpets.
—Edward Dovxlen in ‘ The Saturday Reviev.’
NOCTURNE.
Up to her chamber window
A slight wire trellia goes,
And up this Romeo’s ladder
Clambers a bold white rose.
I lounge in the ilex shadows,
I see the lady lean,
Unclasping her silken girdle,
The curtain's folds between.
She smiles on her white rose lover,
She reaches out her hand
And helps him in at the window—
I see it where I stand!
To her scarlet lips she holds nim,
And kisses him many a time—
Ah, me! it was he that won her
Because he dared to climb !
THOMAS BILEY ALDRICH.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“LORD, THINK OF THE LAMBS!”
The sun was hot and the box-trees drooped,
And the hills and the ranges hazed,
And the stones in the creek were afire with
drought,
And the tin roofs crackled and blazed.
There was little grass on the river flats,
The dams were like shaking bogs,
When the parson came with a prayer for rain
To the station at Cedar Logs.
The parson came and the parson prayed,
And he patted his God on the head,
He spoke of famine and seven kine,
Of Egypt and waters red ;
And the service closed with a little hymn
That was sung through the nose,
And the boss got up with a visage grim,
And the congregation rose.
They followed him down to the wool-shed door,
And the parson walked behind ;
“Mac” stopped, and he stood and he turned
about,
And he spoke from his inner mind.
He opened the door that was eight feet wide,
Let in the streaming sun ;
He beckoned the parson to come beside,
And the parson heard and was dumb.
“ Do ye see yon flat beyont the crick ?
Do ye smell the beastly scent
O’ the rotting yows and the fetid cows ?
Was this what yer Goddy meant,
When He closed on Adam for breaking laws,
By ‘ sweat o’ the brow and graft ’ ?
Did He burn my draftin’ yairds for fun
When He saw there’d be nocht to draft ?
“ Is it right for an * evolution ’ man
To blether o’ ‘ walls ’ and ‘ the weak ’ ?
I’m strong ; I’ve brains, and a level head ;
I’ve toiled ; I’ve sweated ; I, weak !
Hear me, whiles I pray to my ain true God
For my land and my life and spul ;
Then go and pray to your little Lord
To pluck me from hot hell-hole.
“ O God of the men that are Men,
Look round on the land !
See the dryness and drouth and sore sorrow !
Stretch forth thy strong hand !
Look, Lord, at the sheep in the dams
Boggit deep in the mud, and their eyes
Taken alive by the crows !
Lord ! think o’ the lambs !
“ Almighty ! I’ve sinned, I’ve no sorrow ;
I’d live just again as I’ve lived ;
But please send thy rain on the morrow —
Or soon. Not for me. I’m broke as it is.
Consider thy beasties !
“ Think o’ the anguish of mothers
With no milk for their bairns !
Hear, Lord, the bleat of the mothers,
The woe of the dying !
Just one big cloud, God, you’ve so many,
To burst on the ranges !
Think of fresh grass, roaring gullies,
Think on the changes !
“ O send rain ere the frost conies ;
Be merciful, pitiful, kind ;
Visit not the sins of such ones
As me on thy beasties.
“ Tak’ thy horse, God, and ride by the rivers,
Smell the stinks in the creek ;
By scorched plains where hot sunlight shivers ;
Mark fat crows on dead trees !
God, think of thy cattle ! ”
The parson mounted his weary nag,
That was galled, and raw on the back ;
He spoke no word as he rode away
Alone, down the dusty track.
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
THE CALL OF THE OCEAN.
’Twas last night I heard the gulls screaming,
“ O traitor ! O dastard ! thou art;
And I woke in a fright from my dreaming,
With the strokes of their beaks in my heart.
I long for a sight of the ocean,
I long for the splash of the spray ;
I long for the laughter and motion
Of sunlight and water at play.
I long for the breeze and the creaking
Of cordage, and canvas, and spar ;
I long for the booming voice, speaking
Of danger and death on the bar.
I long for a sense of the throbbing
Great heart in the breast of the world ;
I long for the wailing and sobbing,
When the storm-god his legions hath hurled.
I long to be far away forward,
Where the stem cleaves a way through the
night,
And a glow like a demon’s eyes lurid
Bathes the bows in a phosphorent light.
To Westward, where sky and plain’s mi eting
Wraps Day in a bright crimson shroud ;
Night’s shadows are bearing a greeting
As I murmur my longing aloud.
O, God ! from these plains dry and dreary
To the sea let me wander once more,
Where the surge, in a tone sad and weary,
Sings a dirge for the dead on the shore.
For deep in my heart there are voices
That are calling, aye calling for me ;
And the Soul of the Ocean rejoices
As a sailor returns to the sea.
G.G.

SATURDAY, MAY 15, 1897.
Joseph Chamberlain’s Scheme.
In a despatch recently received from Mr. Chamberlain,
it is suggested that during the time the colonial Premiers
will be in London they might discuss such matters as Im-
perial commercial union, colonial defence, the representa-
tion of the colonies in the British Parliament, legislation
with regard to Asiatic and other alien immigrants, the
annexation of the Solomon Islands by New Guinea, uni-
form commercial law throughout the Empire, and other
subjects of concern to the different branches of the Em-
pire. The Premiers will no doubt be prepared to meet
Mr. Chamberlain and talk over those matters. Such a
discussion would be highly valuable, as showing how far
the colonies and the mother county' are in accord on
questions of vital importance to the well-being of the
Empire, and, without pledging the British Government on
the one hand or the colonial Parliaments on the other, a
basis of understanding might be arrived at which might
form the nucleus of a great scheme of Imperial union.—
Daily Paper item.
The above paragraph is the first official sign
that something lies behind the great jubilee
grovel of next J une, and that Australasia is
expected to pay indirectly for the board and
lodging of its seven Premiers at the Hotel
Cecil, London. The jubilee grovel isn’t to
be all grovel; a good part of it is hard busi-
ness. And Joe Chamberlain, of Birming-
ham, has managed the business part of the
arrangements, so far, with the astuteness
which naturally accompanies his impassive
eye-glass and his cold, bony, inscrutable,
bird-of-prey countenance. The invitations
were issued, apparently, for an occasion of
pure festivity, and until they were accepted
there was no hint that anything more than
mere festivity and genial riot and mutually-
agreeable swelled head were intended. If the
political aspect of the matter had been intruded
earlier there would probably have been much
more opposition to the departure of the
Premiers, it having come to be a recognised
fact that the Australian politician who goes
to England, and is knighted by the Queen,
and receives a big cigar from the Heir
Apparent, and is dined and drinked and
danced and advertised, always comes to grief
in the business negotiation that ensues.
Having got all the seven Premiers to-
gether, and dazzled them with a vision of
the great Bull capital in its most gorgeous
state of revelry, and inspired a feeling of
gratitude by free board and lodging, decora-
tions and flattery ; oud having ' t ”iaahed
them into humble in significance by the sight
of more people in one city than are con-
tained in all the seven provinces, the wily
Chamberlain will probably find the seven
surprised and staggered politicians in a mood
to promise anything. And when he gets
them in this mood he proposes, as it now
appears, to lead them, if possible, into pro-
mising a great deal. it begins to
seem that that bill for accommodation at the
Hotel Cecil may be—for Australia—a very
long bill indeed.
♦ ♦ * *
Saying nothing of the “ other subjects of
concern to the different branches of the
Empire,” which may mean anything or
everything or nothing, Joseph Chamberlain
has a stupendous order on hand. The matters
which he asks the Premiers to consider
through the bottom of a glass darkly amount
to the absolute bartering away of the semi-
independence of these provinces. The pro-
posal that the seven perambulating Govern-
ments, while in a genially-obfuscated state,
shall commit themselves, and through them
their Ministries and, as far as possible, their
Parliamentary majorities, to the “represen-
tation of the colonies in the British Parlia-
ment,” simply means an attempt to revive
the idea of Imperial Federation. It means
the creation of a new Imperial Legislature
with direct governing powers over the whole
empire. In this Legislature Britain and the
other sections of the empire would naturally
be represented according to their white
population, therefore, Britain would have
500 members to Australia’s 50, or some simi-
lar proportion. The beautifully equitable
theory thereby implied would be that Aus-
tralasia would gain as much governing power
over Britain as Britain would over Australa-
sia, and each man in each country would
have exactly the same share of political autho-
rity. The hopelessly inequitable practice
would be that the Australian would have
one-tenth share in the government of both
countries, which is a hopeless minority and
worth nothing, while Britain would have
nine-tenths of the control of both countries,
which is an overwhelming majority and
amounts to everything. It is impossible to
suppose that Britain would start an Imperial
Federation on any except a population basis,
and on that basis it would practically reduce
Australasia to its old level as a crown colony.
Therefore, no Australasian politician ven-
tures to advocate it seriously in his sober
moments, but apparently Joseph Chamber-
lain has hopes that the seven Premiers,
when they are intoxicated by board-and-
lodging, may be induced to commit them-
selves even to this insane policy.
* * * *
The representation of the provinces in the
British Parliament would practically mean
committing Australasia to bear any share in
the cost of the empire’s military and naval
defence which the British majority in that
Parliament cared to impose. The suggestion
that the Premiers, while slightly off their
base, should take the opportunity to con-
sider the question of defence, is only another
way of saying that they should consider the
advisability of keeping up big armaments at
their country’s own cost to help in Britain’s
wars. That is the only light in which any
British politician ever views the defence
problem. The further proposal that they
should consider the question of Asiatic
immigration is still more interesting.
Britain is too remote from Asia to suffer
by Asiatic immigration to any appreciable
extent, therefore it doesn’t care in the
least about Asiatic exclusion itself. Also it
can get valuable trade privileges from Asiatic
states if it gives their subjects, in return,
free access to the British territories. And,
as the only important British territory that
is within easy reach of Asiatic immigration
is Australasia, this means that J. Bull
would get the commercial advantages for
himself, while Australasia would pay for
them by being eaten up with the swarming
brown and black and yellow millions. Con-
sidering which way J. Bull’s interest lies,
the spirit in which "he proposes to discuss the
Asiatic question can be easily imagined. He
certamiy doesn’t propose to debate upon
ways and means of keeping the Asiatics out,
partly because he has nothing to gain by
keeping them out, partly because this country
is already attending to that matter in a toler-
ably comprehensive spirit, and debate upon
it is unnecessary. Therefore, the reasonable
assumption is that he wants to debate about
letting Asiatics in, and that is a matter much
too sober and momentous to be safely en-
trusted to any festive and newly-bebaubled
politician.
As regards the matter of Imperial com-
mercial union, the only form of commercial
union which Britain ever wants to consider
is one by which the “colonies” will open
their ports as widely as possible to the
British exporter. Under Freetrade Britain
has, to a great extent, lost control of her own
markets to the German and Frenchman and
American, and has, therefore, to strain
every nerve to find foreign markets in their
stead. Her grievance against the great
majority of her dependencies is that Britain
admits their produce free, while they heavily
tax British produce per medium of protec-
tive tariffs. The British view of the matter,
and the one echoed by the Freetrade press
of Australia, is that these provinces should
give open markets to Britain as a fair ex-
change for the open markets which she offers
to them ; and this is doubtless the view which
Chamberlain proposes to impress on the
vieifing Promi«re to th* V>«et of bi» ability
The other view, which he will assuredly not
impress upon them is : Britain, following
what she considers to be her own interests',
offers Freetrade to everybody, her dependen-
cies included, therefore she gives her depen-
dencies no preference, and treats them no
better or worse than anybody else, and
there is no obligation on either side. Six
of the seven Australias, following what they
consider their own interests, levy protective
duties on their imports, British or other-
wise, and as they give Britain no preference,
and treat her no better or worse than any-
body else, there is again no obligation either
way. If Britain would agree to levy, say,
40 per cent, duties on all imports from
beyond the empire, and let imperial goods in
free, thus giving the outside provinces a
valuable preference, it might possibly be
worth their, while in return to levy similar
duties on foreign imports, while letting
British goods in free. This would simply
mean giving Britain special advantages in
Australian markets in return for special
advantages in British markets. But J.
Bull’s idea that the provinces which follow
a protective policy should let British goods
in at reduced rates as compared with those
of other countries, thus giving him a vast
advantage over all other competitors, while
their goods should have no special advantage
in Britain, but should have to hustle on
equal terms against all the competitors in the
world, is too lop-sided to be worth a
moment’s consideration. The only fair basis
for a commercial union is by giving prefer-
ence in return for preference. And until
Britain adopts a Protectionist policy it has
no preference to offer. All it can do is to
give Australia the same chance in its markets
as it gives to every other outsider, and even
the most protectionist of the Australias gives
Britain exactly the same treatment now.
Therefore, there is already all the commercial
union and reciprocity that circumstances
admit of.
* * * *
The sagacious Chamberlain, however,
has a unique opportunity, and he has seized
it with both hands. His proposal that the
Premiers, while in London, should confer
upon matters which practically amount to a
complete surrender of the autonomy of Aus-
tralasia was not made until they had com-
pleted their arrangements for departure. If
it had been made earlier some of the Parlia-
ments might have expressly stipulated that
the head of the Government should keep
clear of political entanglements, and specified
what he might do and what he must leave
undone. As it is, each Premier was sent on
his holiday with no awkward restrictions
whatever, the idea being that he was going
on a holiday and a non-committal grovel, and
nothing else. Experience has shown that
there are few things so foolish that an Aus-
tralian Premier in London may not assent to
them if he is approached in the proper spirit
—said spirit being carefully decanted out of
the bottle—and with due flattery, and
a title judiciously applied. If the Pre-
miers had to come back and lay the matter
before seven more or less unsympathetic
Parliaments, then the mischief, whatever it
is, would probably go no further, and there
would be little harm done. But in the pre-
sent case they need not, so far as Australia is
concerned, lay the matter before any Parlia-
ment. If the Australian Premiers can be
bought and induced to commit themselves to
Chamberlain’s ideas, and sent back full of
pleasant recollections and new-born loyalty,
and with a handsome new title apiece ; and
if each can win over, or buy over, five of his
colleagues on the Convention to his own way
of thinking, they will form a solid majority
of the Federal delegates. Then that body
may gaily insert the commercial-reciprocity
and the Imperial Federation ideas in the
amended Federal Constitution. The Con-
stitution, having already gone through Parlia-
ment, will thereafter be remitted straight to
a popular vote. The mass of the community
is slow to understand, trustful, and blankly
ignorant of details, and it is capable of vot-
ing “ Tea ” to almost any kind of constitu-
tion if it is not allowed time enough to
realise the situation. Consequently “there
are great possibilities before Joseph of Bir-
mingham, if things only go right.
* * * *
Ihe fact that the Federal Convention and
the great Record Reign grovel come together
gives the astute Birmingham screw-maker
the only chance that might have come in
1000 years to fix the iron yoke of imperialism
upon Australia without his scheme having to
run the gauntlet of a long and searching dis-
cussion in a whole lot of Legislatures. It
gives him his one chance to nobble all Aus-
tralia and Tasmania in a single act. When
Queensland comes in, the Convention will
consist of 60 members, and for once and only
once there is just a possibility that this whole
continent may be bought and sold by anyone
who can command JI votes. The Bulletin
opines that the price of Australian Premiers
will be high in London this year, provided
Chamberlain really sees the greatness of his
opportunity. And, if he doesn’t see it, then
tne cold eye which looks out through Bir-
mingham Joseph’s eye-glass has Tost its
cunning.
The Last Eleven N.S.W. Deficits.
iHE last N.S.W. Treasurer who had a surplus
large enough to be worth calling by that name
was James Watson, who carried the bag lor
the Parkes- Rob e rtso n Ministry of 1878-1883.

Since then the course of things has hll
follows, the disastrous figures being taken from

Ihe figures up to and including 1896 are taken
rom Coghlan’s Statistics. The estimate fo“
the year ending June, 1897, is tiiat supplied •
few days ago by Premier Reid in his financial
statement.
* * * ♦
The Government Statistician is the only re-
liable official authority whose figures are ac-
cessible to the public, and whose statements are
free from all party bias. .Every new Premier
when he takes office and begins to wallow in
the miry politics of New South Wales starts
lus career by stating that his predecessor’s
figures were a lie, that he was, in effect a
thief and a perjurer, that his alleged surplus
was a deficit, or that his deficit was twice as
large as he said it was. The Bulletin has
long since ceased to have faith in the state-
ments of any newly-arrived Premier in New
South Wales. But as the Statistician has no
political ends to serve, as he isn’t responsible
for anything save the cast-iron accuracy of his
statements, and as his accuracy lias never been
called in question by either party, this paper
accepts his conclusions as beyond all question.
The above is the result.
* * * *
The said result shows that for 15 years the
politicians of New South Wales have hardly
made one decent effort to keep the national
finances solvent. There have been eleven
deficits in that period and four surpluses. If
the shortages are labelled according to the Pre-
mier in office, then Alexander Stuart was
the worst spendthrift and incapable of the
crowd ; Jennings was the next worst; Reid is
a good third ; Parkes comes nex-t; and the late
Dibbs Ministry was the one respectable com-
bination of the crowd, inasmuch as it only went
to the bad by .£176,000, while Parkes made a
shortage of .£700,000, and Reid of over a
million. An excuse may be offered for Stuart
on the ground that he was avowedly spending
an accumulated surplus; and a further and
much better excuse on the ground that he spent
most of it on public works which his successors
have paid for largely out of loan-funds. The
surplus lasted out the days of Stuart and his
Treasurer, Dibbs, and though they used up
most of it religiously they at least left no accu-
mulated deficit behind them. Jennings, as
Premier and Treasurer, finished old James
Watson’s pile, and heaped up that first historic
shortage which the Freetrade press has ever
since persistently labelled the “Dibbs deficit,”
though in reality the only Dibbs deficit that
was ever handed down to posterity was the
small shortage of ,£176,000 which his Ministry
left behind it in 1894. But by dint of persis-
tent, hard, unscrupulous mendacity on the part
of the Sydney importers’ organs the Jennings
shortage of £1,484,000, and the £716,000
deficit accumulated by the Parkes-Burns and
Parkes-M ‘Millan Cabinets, and Reid’s deficit
of £1,059,000, and Dibbs’ own deficit of
£176,000 are all lumped together and described
as the “Dibbs deficit” to this day. This is
the more remarkable, as by the Statistician’s re-
turns, that long and blighted financier was the
only man in the crowd who had practically no-
thing to do with the accumulated shortage
which exists to-day.
* * * *
Every Ministry, since the days when the
surplus began to fade, has started business
with a fearsome blast about the extravagance
of its predecessors, and about its own beautiful
economy. How their economy has gone the
following table of each year’s expenditure will
show. Three items have been purposely omitted
from the expenditures. The cost of railway
management is not under direct Ministerial
control, and the interest payments on the public
debt have to be met by all Ministries alike.
Also the payments made in reduction of the
public debt are not included, for the economy
which reduces payments in this direction isn’t
worth mentioning, and the extravagance which
pays large sums off the country’s old liabilities
is a vice that deserves all encouragement.
Without these three items the net expenditures
are as follows. There is a certain amount of
doubt as to the figures for the first two years
(1883-84) as in these days gross and net ex-
penditures were not very clearly distinguished ;
otherwise the figures are mathematically
correct:—
1883 Stuart £5,427,781
1884 ~ 5,778,914
1885 „ 5,325,239
1886 Jennings 5,569,228
1887 Parkes 5,615,125
1888 „ 4,932,487
1889 5,387,618
1890 ~ 5,274,730
1891 „ 5,722,558
1892 Dibbs 5,944,448
1893 „ 5,478,088
1894 ~ 5,068,014
1895 Reid (f.year) 2,807,562
1896 - 5,266,051

* * » ♦
The moral which principally sticks out here
is that the last half-dozen Treasurers were all
very much alike, and that the chief difference
between a bad Treasurer who wrecks the
country and a brilliant financier who saves it
lies in his success or otherwise in securing the
support of the Sydney daily newspaper
Ananias. The surest way not to secure
that support, as a general thing, is to put
a duty on paper and printing-material. Alex-
ander Stuart ran New South Wales on an
average outlay of £5,510,644 per annum;
Jennings cost £5,569,228 ; Parkes averaged
£5,386,503 a year; Dibbs cost £5,496,850;
Reid’s average, so far, is £5,376,055. What
differenoe I horp ip among them is almost wholly


represented by differences in the amount
spent on public works. Dibbs paid, on an
average, ,£1,084,000 per annum of the works
expenditure out of revenue ; Reid paid only
£905,000 per annum, and economised by
charging the balance to loans, which just
accounts for the discrepancy between the
average annual cost of the two Governments.
The great original surplus—that of Treasurer
Watson, in the Parkes-Robertson coalition
days—was piled up by disposing of the public
lands at the rate of £2,700,000 per annum.
'Fhe first great series of deficiencies—those of
.Stuart and Jennings —was created by cutting
down land sales to about £1,500,000 per annum,
in an attempt to conserve the public estate.
Then Parkes put things partly right again by
immediately running land sales up as high as
£2,300,000 per annum. The difference, in fact,
between the wickedly-extravagant Treasurer
who has a large deficit and the wise and
economical one who has a small deficit is mainly
that the latter sells more land and sells it
faster. Also, it arises to some extent out of
the fact that the economical Treasurer charges
more public works to loan account, and lets
country roads get into disrepair.
* * * *
Among the N.S. W. Treasurers of the last 15
years there isn’t one who kept the accounts
straight, or honestly faced the bad situation.
They all spent money to very much the same
extent; even poor, well-meaning old Jennings
wasn’t noticeably worse than the rest. So far
as the figures show, however, Dibbs came
nearest to making ends meet without resorting
to the expedient of enormous land sales to fill
the state coffers. Also, they show that there
is really no “Dibbs deficit;” there never was
any surplus at all except through tremend-
ous land-sales ; there never was any great finan-
cial genius who rushed in and “saved the
country ” —except by selling more land; and
there never was any special reason why any one
of the crowd should be a small tin god much
more than another. One thing, at all events,
sticks out conspicuously ; with 11 deficits in
15 years, New South Wales can’t afford to
sacrifice a great part of its customs revenue as
it is doing—not even for the blessed privilege
of getting in line with Britain and Turkey as
one of the three great Foreign-trade countries
of the world. The blessing may be all right,
but it costs a great deal too much as things are
at present.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Commutation.
Last week the “ GoD-fearing Ministry ” of
Botany Bay “commuted” the death sentence
on a prisoner named Primmer, then waiting in
Dubbo gaol to be hanged by the neck until he
was dead. Said commutation consisted in
letting Primmer off with life-long imprison-
ment and 25 lashes. It takes a Gou-fear-
ing Ministry—a Ministry that prays regu-
larly, and goes to church religiously, and
has a good, sound faith in a devil and in a
blazing hell, and in the eternal combustion of
unbaptised infants —to rise to anything like
this. “ Imprisonment for life ” is civil death
and obliteration. It practically means that the
prisoner is never to get a chance to repeat his
crime because die is locked away front his
fellows for the rest of his days. Therefore he
doesn’t require a deterrent ; when the law puts
a man away permanently in a stone box so that
he will never have a chance to repeat his crime,
the idea of taking special measures, or special
horrors, to deter him from repeating it is super-
fluous. So The Bulletin presumes that Prim-
mer isn’t to be flogged to deter him from a re-
petition of his crime, but merely as a fiendish
piece of vengeance ; if the flogging will deter
him effectually then there is no need to lock
him up for life, and if he is locked up for
life it matters very little whether he is
effectually deterred or not. It would be
just about as logical to order him to be
flogged and hanged and imprisoned for life
afterwards ; or to flog him and imprison
him for life, and then hang him as soon
as he developes a latal illness, so as to
prevent him dying naturally. Under these
circumstances, the assumption is that the Hog-
ging is a species of pious frill put on to orna-
ment the sentence. It is the sort of thing that
only a GoD-fearing Ministry of the most
ghastly Lagland pattern would associate its
name with. There is also a cold - blooded
and mathematical accuracy about the thing.
Primmer, it seems, isn’t quite bad enough
to hang, yet he is too bad to be let off
with life-long imprisonment only, so the 25
lashes are added to make things exactly square.
If he were somewhat worse he would probably
get lifelong imprisonment and, say, four times
25 lashes. And if he were a little worse still—
well, how much flogging added to perpetual
imprisonment do Reid and Brunker and A. J.
Gag-Gould reejkon is exactly equal to execution?
At what point in its cold-blooded career does the
GoD-fearing Ministry consider that a commuta-
tion ceases to be such, and becomes an aggrava-
tion instead ? Some politician of a logical turn
of mind should ask Minister Gould (of the
Anglican Synod) to explain how much flogging
added to life-long stone-breaking he would
gladly exchange for a nice, dry, warm hanging
in a salubrious and well-ventilated yard where
there are no dogs or hawkers or hospital-col-
lectors to worry him ? And if he doesn’t
know, it would be a fair thing to ask him how
he knew whether he was commuting Primmer’s
sentence or not?
The Latest Gambling Spasm.
Aiti-arently there is shortly to be a fresh
spasm of indignant virtue over the Sydney
totes. At least, Minister for Justice Gould
seemed to intimate as much when he spoke the
following little piece in the Assembly :
Mr. Gould said, as to the hon. member's charges that
the police were not doing' their duty in connection with
betting, he might say that it was not his province to give
instructions to the police. But since the hon. member
first drew attention to the matter the police had received
instructions to take stringent measures to put down tote-
shops. As a result, there would doubtless be a large
number of prosecutions.
This wakes up the old question that The
Bulletin is always asking, and which never
evokes an answer. The police are supposed to
be taking stringent measures to put down law-
breakers all the time. They are nob supposed
to require special instructions at inter-
vals, and they do not require such instruc-
tions except in relation to this particular brand
of illegality. When the “ cop ” comes upon a
person committing burglary or brutal assault
or murder or highway robbery, or throwing
bricks through a plate-glass window, or passing
bogus bank-notes, he arrests him right there.
When he comes across a man publicly and un-
disguisedly conducting a tote he wanders past
and takes no notice—sometimes, of course, he
takes a bribe—unless he has just received spe-
cial instructions. If he wandered past any of the
other kinds of law-breaker and took no notice of
them on the ground that he hadn’t recently re-
ceived any instructions about arresting bur-
glars, murderers, thieves, forgers, &c., he would
be thrown out of the force for shameful neglect
of duty, but he can let the “ tote ” law-breaker
slide, year in and year out, without being
called in question. The country doesn’t want
to know, every six months or so, that special
orders have been issued to the police to look
after the illegal gambling-dens ; it wants to
know why they don’t look after them every
day without any orders in particular. Also, it
isn’t interested so very much in hearing, once
every nine months or more, that the principal
tote-keepers are to be summoned with great
pomp and fuss and ceremony ; it wants to find
out why they aren’t summoned every day that
their shops are found open, and every night
when the gas is lit, and the big lamp outside
shows the proprietor’s name on it, and the
crowd is conspicuously pouring in to pub its
money on, or pouring out after putting its
money on. Also, it wants to know about the
ignominious discharge of that detective
who went round some time last year
and warned the law - breakers to be
more careful, instead of running them
in. When an officer knows where to find some
two dozen notorious law-breakers, and instead
of capturing them he goes round—apparently
with the concurrence of his superiors—and
“ gives them the tip ” to escape in good time,
and the daily papers report his outrageous con-
duct without comment, the situation is at least
remarkable. So long as gambling is a crime
the law against it should be enforced just as
vigorously as any other law. The police and
detectives who don’t attempt to enforce it
should be discharged for neglect of duty, or
else gaoled for corruption. The GoD-fearing
Ministry which allows the force to openly
neglect its duty without calling it to account
should be called to account itself. At present
the administration of the N.S.W. Gambling
Act is a strange and snufflesome mystery.
What other law is there on the statute-book
which the police are allowed to abolish with
impunity in this fashion ?
Holy Hullabaloo.
Just before his departure from London, Dr.
Clifford, the latest little Nonconformist god
to be fussed over in Australia, delivered upon
the Eastern question what is described as “one
of his great fighting speeches.” Quoth Clifford,
magnificently —“ We have done with Lord
Salisbury’s policy !—we have done with the
concert of the Powers ! Across the dark
eastern sky Greece has shot one brilliant hope !”
—with much more irresponsible blatancy, every
sentence of which, we are told, was greeted
by the audience with ringing cheers.
Almost any kind of hysterical screech, however
—with Blood in it, and the Bible and Eng-
land’s Glory—will set the British Philistine a-
whooping wildly for a fray of which he is too
fat-witted to foresee the probable develop-
ments, and to the chances of which, in any
case, he would take excellent care not to commit
his own comfortable Evangelical carcase. It is
lucky for Britain that the clear-headfed part
of her people—not unmindful of her own
pleasing iittle amenities of conquest —have re-
mained, throughout, unstirred of Stead and
Co. to avenge the Armenian at any cost. Even
if he were worth avenging—which the
other Powers evidently consider he isn’t—
said Powers have not the slightest intention
of allowing Britain to play Special Providence
in the East, and the assumption that J. Bull’s
is in any sort a name to conjure with is nowa-
days confined mostly to the people who punc-
tuate with senseless acclamations the “great
fighting speeches” of smug stay-at-home pul-
piteers. “My voice is still for war !” is the
cry generally of those who have never seen a
bullet-hole or a bayonet thrust. The typical
British jingo is the huckster whose adventures
have been bounded by his yard-stick or his
scales, and who is also, for the most
part, the possessor of the Nonconformist
Conscience. It is, in a word, Napoleon’s
“shopkeeper”—stuffed with a lying legend of
Britisli magnanimity, and insolent with a stale
tradition of British dominance—who is doing
his blindfold best to bring the nation to blows
with Russia or Germany, or both, on behalf of
those Cretan “Christians.” The said Chris-
tians, by the way, would unhesitatingly
reject even the strenuous Clifford as dis-
tinctly outside the fold of the faith, and they
are in any case no more worth the howling
that has been raised about them than are the
Greek heroes who, after forcing their King
into hostilities, have promptly skedaddled back
to Athens to fasten upon said King the blame of
their own fatuousness and poltroonery.

A Victorian House-Breaker.
The law which permits a prisoner to give
evidence on his own behalf isn’t an unmixed
blessing—in Victoria, anyhow. For instance, last
December, Williams and Dunbar were tried
for house-breaking. Dunbar gave evidence
denying his guilt. Williams pleaded guilty,
but also gave evidence denying Dunbar’s guilt.
Williams was sentenced, but two juries failed
to agree about Dunbar, and it was only when
the law hail stuck to him with the dire persist-
ence of a bull-terrier for three successive trials
that it secured a conviction. Then the law
proceeded against them both for perjury
because they swore to Dunbar’s innocence. It
took two trials to get a jury to decide that
Williams had sworn falsely when he said that
Dunbar was innocent, but even then the jury
couldn’t decide that Dunbar had sworn falsely
when he said that he was innocent himself.
Therefore, the situation is that it is quite cer-
tain Williams told a lie, and not at all certain
that Dunbar, who said exactly the same thing,
didn’t tell the truth. Supposing the next jury
ends the trouble by finding Dunbar “ not
guilty,” what sort of a complication will that
make? The position will then be some-
thing as follows : —(1) Dunbar is guilty
of house-breaking, therefore he is doing a
sentence ; (2) he isn’t guilty of house-breaking,
therefore, his denial of that crime is not
perjury ; (3) he is guilty of that crime, there-
fore Williams’ denial on his behalf is perjury.
If a jury finds Dunbar not guilty of perjury,
then the only thing is either to revoke Dun-
bar’s sentence for house-breaking and Wil-
liams’sentence for perjury ; or else to prosecute
Dunbar for further perjury in swearing that
he didn’t commit perjury. Even if it finds him
guilty, the law, to be decently consistent, must
prosecute him again for perjury, seeing that it
is just as much perjury to swear falsely that he
didn’t commit perjury as to swear falsely that
he didn’t commit house-breaking. The com-
bined opinions of all these juries make an in-
teresting total. It took three trials to convince
a jury that Dunbar was guilty of house-break-
ing. It took two more to convince another jury
that Williams swore falsely when he declared
Dunbar didn’t house-break. A sixth trial has
failed to convince still another jury that Dunbar
swore falsely when he said the same thing as
Williams. Therefore, it has taken six trials
to prove that Dunbar did break into that
house, and it isn’t proved to the satisfaction of
the last jury yet. The Victorian law is now
getting ready for a seventh attempt. If it had
only put one-tenth of this energy into the pro-
ceedings against some bankers the public still
remembers
Be Mr. Lyne.
“ P.” writes as follows :
Dear Bulletin, —Politicians, like eels, must get used to
being skinned, and if the| flaying process be not too fine-
drawn and the agony not too protracted, they had better
not complain—use is second nature. As the candid friend
of the Protectionists you help keep our party up to
collar and, as a rule, your criticisms are fair and logical. In
a late issue, I venture to think, in the article, “ Wanted, a
Policy,” you were neither. With your permission I will
state my reasons. The Protectionist leaders are not
“ constantly putting themselves forward as men with
only one idea —Protection.” They support every bona-
fide reform —Mr. Lyne supported the Referendum to wit.
Further, you say in effect that when the land and income
taxes were introduced by Mr. Reid, Mr. Lyne should have
gone one better. You say he should have demanded “ a
heavier impost on large incomes than on small ones, and
a larger tax on incomes derived from property than from
those realised by personal exertion.” At the time those
taxes were introduced Mr. Lyne avowedly declined to
support, assist, amend, or have anything to do
with them on the ground that their introduction
meant the sweeping away of what modicum of
protection the Customs duties afforded us. He op-
posed them on that ground; Hansard shows that
he always held that side by side with a proper Customs
tariff we must have our due proportion of direct taxa-
tion. In 1886 Mr. Lyne was a member of the Government
(Jennings’) that introduced a land-tax of (d. in the £ on
unimproved values with an exemption of £lOOO. He was
a member of the Govt. (Dibbs’) that introduced a care-
fully graduated income-tax, but when Mr. Reid brought
in a crude, unfair and unjust scheme of taxation, in lieu
of moderate protective Customs duties, he avowedly re-
fused to touch it, saying he wished the country to learn
by painful experience exactly what Freetrade under the
Reid rbgime really meant. Nothing but experience
teaches, and the public are now learning the lesson, and
are beginning to buck at the task. Space precludes
quotations, but Hansard shows that Lyne supported old-
age pensions, a minimum wage, regulation of the hours
of labor, irrigation, home-borrowing as against foreign-
borrowing, cessation of land-sales, the exclusion of
aliens, &c., &c.
You think Lyne should publish the details of the
policy he proposes in lieu of the Reid doctrine.
Hardly good enough. You yourself admit that
Reid has “systematically borrowed” the ideas of the
Protectionist party, so why should Lyne supply him
with a lot more just to keep him going now that his fol-
lowing is beginning to clamor and his power to totter’
We are, in all human probability, still 12 months from l
general election, and if Lyne published a detailed mani
festo to-day the party in power would collar nine-tenthi
of the ideas, and Lyne having already announced then)
would have to vote for them. Besides, the details of to-daj
might not suit 12 months hence, and why should on!
party invite attack on mere matter of detail? Reid, wher
in Opposition, entered into no details ; he attacked Pro-
tection, and promised, if successful, to introduce Free-
trade. Gladstone, when in Opposition, never formulated
a detailed policy. To-day Lyne attacks Freetrade, ana
promises, if successful, to introduce Protection. He has
all along said that if the revenue derivable from a scientifi-
cally-constructed Customs tariff is found insufficient then
he will propose a scheme of graduated Land and Income
taxation—but not until then. In the meantime, should
he arrive, the Reid scheme of land and income taxation
will go by the board. When the proper time arrives, i.e.,
on the eve of a general election, Mr. Lynje will speak to
the country through his constituents, at the same tiruo
publishing a manifesto sufficiently liberal, progressive,
and detailed to satisfy the party —the party only, and not
the Freetrade critics, who may go hang themselves. In
the meantime, with fully 12 months’ life left in the pre-
sent Parliament, why furnish Brunker with “ ideas”?
After the smash at Phersala, it may be safely
assumed that all is over with Greece, unless
some strong Power comes to her assistance in a
hurry. The Greeks fought well in the early
part of the campaign, and they seem in most oi
the battles to have had numbers on their side.
But the deadly courage of the Turkish Tommy
Atkins, who never will understand when he is
beaten, and who wades in with a valor that
never fails against any position and any odds,
seems to have scared the Greeks at last, and, in
the retreat to Larissa, their army crumbled to-
gether all in a heap, and their valor evaporated,
and they degenerated into a mass of terror and
hysterics. It will probably be years before that
army that skedaddled to Larissa is its old self
again. So far as the available evidence goes, the
commanders seem to be about equally good or
equally mediocre ; the difference is that the
Greek is a man of ordinary valor, and the Turk
a man whose valor is extraordinary, so he
walked over the Greek every time. It would
be interesting to know if the Greek was ever
the tremendous fighting demi-god he claims to
be. The stories of the terrific things he did
against the ancient Persians are his own stories
almost'exclusively, for no complete Persian
version of the matter has survived. The Greek
wrote practically all the history of those days.
The first time he ran against a nation which
insisted on leaving behind it its own account
of the trouble Greece went down, and never
rose again. The tales of ancient Greek heroism
are Greek tales, and stand on the same footing
as the Belgian’s account of how he alone won
the Battle of Waterloo ; the tales of how thfe
ancient Greek ran away and surrendered and
howled for mercy are the ones which are best
supported by independent records. Possibly
the Greek, as a soldier, was never very much
better than the gallant but ephemeral fried-fish
man that he is to-day. Certainly the ancient
Hellene, if he was the desperate fighting man
he said he was, should have mopped up half
the East, and his domains, even in the alleged
good old days, were never nearly so extensive
as those of many other nations which howl in a
much lower key on the page of history.
Mr. Ashton, M.L.A., in reply:—
Dear Bulletin, —The essence of Mr. Ewing'S remarks
upon my “Equal representation” article appears to be
this ! As the people who elect the senators for the small
states will be the same sort of people, probably, as those
who elect the senators for the big states, the aforesaid
big states may reasonably trust the 'Minorities of the
aforesaid small states to treat them fairly; to which I
answer : As the people who elect the senators for the
large states will be the same sort of people, probably, as
those who elect the senators for the small states, the
aforesaid small states may reasonably trust the majorities
of the aforesaid big states to treat them fairly. That is
to say, if it comes to a matter of trusting someone,
let the majority be trusted in prefererrce to the
minority. That has been my standpoint through-
out. I have not treated the problem as one of big
v. small states, hut as involving the question as to
whether a majority or a minority of Australian people
shall rule. Mr. Ewing evidently misunderstands my
position when he asks me if I can name any Federation
where “ state rights” have not been conceded. I cannot,
nor have I argued that the “ rights” of the states should
not he conserved. My view is that the self-governing
powers of the individual states are sufficiently safe-
guarded by strictly defining the powers of the Federal
Parliament, and making an extension thereof impossible
without the assent of all the states. If Mr. Ewing, how-
ever, uses the phrase “ state rights ”as a synonym for
“ Equal representation of states in the Senate,” then I
answer that that “principle” does not find a place in
either the Canadian or German Federal Constitutions.—
Jas. Ashton.
Vic. Presbyterian Assembly laments aloud be-
cause its mission to the Jews has not been
successful, not one single Cohen having ac-
cepted Christianity at the hands of the braw,
red-headed Calvinists who have been carrying
grace into the Ghetto during the last twelve
months. The Bulletin holds that it is now
the turn of Solomons, and Isaacs, and Mosen-
stein. The children of Israel should go as
missionaries to the Scotchmen of Melbourne.
They might even try to convert the Presby-
terian Assembly to Christianity. Unfortu-
nately, the Jew is not much given to poking
his nose into other people’s business —chiefly
because nobody’s business is half so lucrative as
his own—but it ought not to be hard to convert
a few Scotchmen to Judaism on strictly business
principles; and, anyhow, the spectacle of Cohen,
and Coiin, and Flegeltaub, and Josephs
wrestling in prayer with Sandy Macintosh,
and M'Allister, and M‘Bain, and M'Fadyen
would be a joyous thing in itself, and an in-
spiriting method of celebrating the 60th year of
the reign of our gracious Queen.
Rev. Dr. Marshall, of Melbourne, has found
a reason for the drought s
The losses and privations and humiliations through
which this country has been wading are God’s retributive i
punishment for the national sins of dishonesty and extra-
vagance and folly committed during the land boom.
And what was the boom God’s retributive'
punishment for? And why does He inflict!
retributive punishment upon the farmer, and
the settler, and the other man out back, all of
whom were victims of, but otherwise had
nothing to do with, the boom? The Rev. Dr.
Marshall’s God is most illogical, but, then,
God is nearly always a parson, which accounts
for the fact that when the parson has a boil on
his neck it calls for soap-and sugar merely, but
when the other fellow has a boil it is a visita-
tion of Providence, and demands regular
attendance at church, prompt payment of pew-
rents, and two half-crowns in the plate every
Sabbath till further notice.
A Treat for the London Poor.
From St. Paul’s Cathedral the return journey will he by
way of Cheapside, London-bridge, the Borough, St.
George’s Circus, Westminster-bridge, and back to the
palace. The poorer classes on the south side of the water
will thus have an opportunity of seeing their Sovereign
and witnessing the day’s spectacle.—Melb. Argus.
They will troop in loyal thousands from the
putrid purlieus where
Foul Disease and Crime and Famine have their
pestilential lair ;
They will crawl from fetid alley, they will creep
from courts obscene,
When they hear the joyous tidings of the pass-
ing of their Queen.
She has reigned—-aloft, sublime—
Sixty years—let joy-bells chime !
And these God-forgotten wretches were her
subjects all the time !
They are hungry ; they are ragged ; they are
gaunt and hollow-eyed ;
But their frowsy bosoms palpitate with fine old
British pride ;
And they’ll belt their rags in tighter, and they’ll
hoarsely cry “Hooray ! ”
When their good Queen’s circus passes on
Sexagenary Day.
0 the thunder of the drums,
And the cry of “ Here she comes ! ”
Will be better than a breakfast to the natives
of the slums.
All their woes will be forgotten on that ever-
blessed day
When they see the Kings and Princes and the
Lords in broad array,
Clad in brand-new dazzling uniforms with golden
lace agleam—
-0 their Hunger and their Misery will vanish
like a dream !
Lust of eye, and pride of life,
In that pageant will run rife,
And “The Dials,” drunk with joy, at night
will dance upon its wife.
Sixty years their gracious Queen has reigned a-
holding up the sky,
And a-bringing round the seasons, hot and cold,
and wet and dry ;
And in all that time she’s never done a deed
deserving gaol—-
So let joy-bells ring out madly and Delirium
prevail !
0 her Poor will blessings pour
On their Queen whom they adore ;
When she blinks with puffy eyes at them they’ll
hunger nevermore.
Melb.
C.R.

record. The Edison Vitascope, Kinetoscope, and

The “ Sign of the Cross ” opened at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, in the presence of a very large
audience, and, so far, it appears that the religious
tragedy is going to catch on. It is a story of the
old days of the Caesars and the Colisaeum, when
the people of the new faith were thrown to the
wild beasts, and the fastest lion got the first bite
out of the slow Christian, and the fastest Christian
gave a long race to the slow lion, and the biggest
lion got the most Christian, and the smallest lion
was altogether left. The story itself is simple.
Marcus buperbus, the rich and gorgeous and
cynical prefect, is clanking through Rome in his
gilded armor when he finds the rabble capturing
two Christians—an old man and a girl—and out
of a magnificent contempt for the unwashed he
scatters them and rescues the pair. He gets
mashed on the girl Christian, and from that
moment Marcus starts to slide down hill to the
Colisaeum and the lions. The bleared eye of the
Chancellor, who wants his billet, takes in the
situation. The girl’s address isn’t in the direc-
tory, but by vigorously flogging a very small
Christian—apparently flogging a small Christian
is the same thing as a directory in those days—he
gets the information and arrests her. Then
Marcus starts on a succession of rash acts to save
her, and ends by using insurrectionary language
at the throne of a bilious and cataleptic Caesar.
When all this leads to nothing he makes a
final appeal to his girl to abandon her faith, to
marry him, and to be empress when he
has thrown the bilious, haggard Caesar off his
throne. That fails also, and right there, in a sud-
den blaze of fervor, Marcus turns Christian, and
throws up his splendid billet as second man in the
empire, and steps with his Christian girl into the
arena, where the lion presumably makes an im-
mediate vacancy for another prefect. They depart
with the limelight on them, while the Chancellor,
who has the rest of the stage to himself, smiles in-
scrutably out of his bloated head at his rival’s
downfall.
* * • *
The mounting of this tragic tale is
magnificent. Old Rome is put on regardless of
expense, the Roman ladies are a blaze of
millinery, and the armor of the legionaries clatters
like crates of brass-ware as they go by. Almost
everybody is magnificent save the small Christian
who is flogged to make him give up the address,
ihe two informers who live by running down
Christians for the scalp-money are an interesting
study, and their lament that trade is bad, and
that they haven’t seen a Christian for a fortnight,
appeals strongly to the hearers, most of whom
haven’t seen a Christian, to their certain know-
ledge, for much longer than that. Society in those
days was mostly wine and sin and leg-show ; and
the comic Roman drunk, and the Roman lady
who emerges greatly from her clothes, and the
one who tells dreadful tales about the immorality
of the third lady, helped to lighten the gloom some-
what. Still, ‘ The Sign of the Cross ”is very
gloomy and majestically impressive. All the
Christians are butchered at the last. The
prefect—the noblest heathen of them all-
goes to the lions. Nero is left to his ghosts
and his gibberings and his midnight horrors.
Ihe woman who loved the prefect, and gave him
away because she loved him. is left tear-
mg her hair on the steps of Caesar’s
throne. The principal informer is hauled away
to execution; times get so bad that the other in-
former has to arrest him on a charge of Chris-
tianity to make ends meet. Nearly half the play
is taken up with pious exhortation of one kind
and another—this includes the time spent in flog-
ging the smallest Christian. Some more of it is
occupied by Marcus talking to himself and hang-
ing’ fire between two opinions. Nero and the old
Roman drunk keep things moving more actively
than the others ; when they are not there the
pageant goes with a solemn stride. It would feel
very slow in places but for the outbreaks of
spectacular magnificence, and the constant im-
pression that something tremendous is liable to
happen to a Christian at any moment, and the
novelty of all the good people being burnt or
eaten, while the bad ones are left. The play is
well-constructed in its own vast, sombre, magnifi-
cent way, but though one religious drama may be
a great success two would probably be a failure.
* * * .
At least three-fourths of the co. calls for
severe commendation. Caleb Porter, as Nero
makes his first appearance in Sydney, and his
blotchy, bilious, shuddery Caesar is a notable piece
of work. Nero, with his starts and horrors and
visions and sudden alarms, and the gloating voice
m which he orders the Christians to be combusted
is the finest single figure in the show. Julius
-Knight s prefect is a fine, straightforward, manly
pagan. He wears his mail with dignity, and
ml 6 t S, th - e f - rlgld Chris tian girl like a man of
mi f v' lhe Christian girl wears a long white robe
and her pale, impassive face looks rather pretty in
the limelight. Her part consists mainly of a rapt
expression the aforesaid white frock and a mass
of hair, and Miss Ferrar wears these three things
reasonably well. A rather hurried and uncon-
vincmg miracle connected with a thunderbolt
prevents the prefect really hugging her, and serves
as the turning-point in the drama. Gaston Mer-
vale, Hawtrey (a highly artistic Roman drunk)
Miss Mortyne (as the empress whom Caesar paws
lovingly with a shaky hand while giving orders for
P°. ured , over heroine before she is
lighted), Miss Linda Raymond, and especially
Miss Elliott Page, who fills a difficult part very
cleverly, all deserve to be well spoken of. All
of the Christians, also, can pass muster, barring
the smallest Christian, represented by Miss Marie
Neilson. The smallest Christian is the one com-
plete disappointment of the company, and this
paper recommends his immediate relapse into
paganism.
** * *
The present week is the last of Thornton’s
four-months’ season at Sydney Criterion. About
11 on Friday night the Rev. Robert Spalding will
finish bringing the church into contempt and dis-
repute for this season, and will pack up his weak,
watery eye, and his cold feet and his bun, and
take his mild and harmless departure. Except
that The Bulletin desires to shed a tear weighing
2£cwt. over the absence of the original Uncle
Cattermole, the present version of the “ Private
Secretary’’gives no reasonable cause for objection.
Misses Seabrooke and Bessie Rignold fill the two
girl-parts very prettily ; the two girls haven’t
much to do except look pretty and lean up confi-
dentially against two nice young men, and they
get through this contract without turning a hair.
Guyton Heath, who has an occasional tendency to
be shadowy and indefinite, is rather more definite
than usual, and Herbert Terry, who plays Catter-
mole, junior, is always an agreeable youth who
wears a lovely frock-coat to great advantage. The
idea of putting up the “Private Secretary” on
the same night as Williamson’s stupendous “Sign
of the Cross ” has a lot of humor in it, too. The
early Christian preacher at Her Majesty’s and the
recent curate with his bun at the Criterion
represent the two extreme ends of the one
creed. Most creeds which ever amount to
anything begin somewhat after the fashion of
Williamson’s show, and most of them end apolo-
getically with the milk and the bun and the meek,
cackling little priest whose mother is much alarmed
lest he will catch catarrh in his pale, apologetic
nose. This is a solemn thing to consider, and The
Bulletin bids a regretful farewell to Thornton
with mixed feelings. The “ Secretary ”is a
comedy which has very few equals, and Thornton’s
Secretary in particular is a brilliant shriek of
mirth, but when one thinks of the amount of
valuable tiger that was used up in olden times in
the effort to suppress a creed whose natural end,
when left to itself, is the Rev. Spalding suffering
from cold feet in a city that Nero probably never
heard of
** * *
The Brough Comedy Co., which is steadily
farewelling through Australasia, will arrive at
Sydney Cri. on Saturday in a voice broken with
emotion, and revive “ Sowing the Wind ” for six
nights only. “ A White Elephant ” comes next,
and as this pallid pachyderm has not yet been
seen in Sydney it should be the event of the
season. After that follow “The Solicitor,” “A
Woman of No Importance,” “ A Woman’s
Reason,” “Dandy Dick,” the “Second Mrs.
Tanqueray,” “Niobe,” and possibly—if room can
be found for it inside the five weeks available —
“ Thoroughbred ”as a finale. After that the co.
farewells some more in S.A. and Maoriland.
** * *
Melville Stolfcz’s variety co. is keeping up
a consistent boom at Sydney Royal. No member
of that remarkable combination has yet broken his
or her neck, and the pleasant, motherly person
who does the triple somersault in the air takes her
little excursion lightly, as one who is quite at home
in being upside-down upon nothing. The lady
who dances on the thread up by the dome is like-
wise doing well, and if there is anything in the
Moslem theory that the souls of the departed have
to travel along a hair on their way to Paradise
she should get there, unless some large, coarse soul
shoves her off. Some changes in the programme
are announced for next week.
i** m *
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon”
still skips in a rather depressed frame of mind at
Sydney Palace, and its prospects don’t appear to
amount to much more than they did last week.
The Bulletin begins to be convinced that the
Palace will by-and-bye be hired at a great re-
duction by the Wesleyan denomination, and the
collection-plate will stand in its marble vestibule,
and the squalling infant will be passed up over
what is now the orchestra to be duly christened
Jemima Ann, and the Rev. Peter John Job Crump
will hold forth where Gourlay now skips so impro-
bably by the light of the moon. Which is a
sad reflection, and one that can only be ade-
quately quenched in beer.
