** * *
One consecutive week of the new comic
opera, entitled “ Lelamine and the Lord from
Government House,” was inaugurated at Mel-
bourne Princess’ on Saturday night, with festivi-
ties quite equal to the occasion—equal, in fact to
many such occasions rolled into one. Flags and
garlands made the route to the dress-circle seem
like the path of glory that leads only to the grave
A bombarding contingent, stationed in a stage-
box, fired a salute of 57 floral tributes at the per-
formance, whilst an honorary secretary was
skirmishing round the premises exhorting press-
men in general to calm their critical faculties at
the managerial canteen.
* * * »
The composer of “Lelamine,” Alfred R
Moulton, conducts the orchestra; the libretto
was written by a man who has already gone to
render an account of his crime. He was a good
enough citizen, no doubt, but a shockingly
bad librettist. If he put an intelligible plot into
his story the amateur artists must have forgotten
to mention it on Saturday, and, as regards dia-
logue, The Bulletin believes that the amateurs, as
a rule, refrained from plain speaking out of re-
spect for the deceased author’s memory. They
didn’t wish his jokes, if he had any, to be under-
stood. Composer Moulton’s music shines some-
what by comparison with the literary half of this
reckless undertaking. It contains three or four
graceful little melodies of the Robinson type. A
couple of songs for the tenor, with chorus, and a
sentimental ballad for the contralto, went very
well on Saturday. Indeed, a lot more of the
music might be called pretty if it were not so
wearisomely similar to the airs that went before.
All that the composer could do for “Lelamine”
was accomplished in the first act, after which he
did the same sort of thing over and over again,
His orchestration is trivial, and the finales have
no force or fulness. Chorus-singing is always ex-
pected to be the best feature of an amateur opera
performance, but in this case the 80 voices get
very little chance, and many of them, seemingly,
are not educated warblers, and so the people who
come and go in mobs are only remembered as a
spectacle. The men do credit to the stage-
manager, and the ladies make a bright display of
millinery. Also, the ballet is expensively put
upon the stage, but a fascination dance by Miss
Florrie Green, who has proved herself a high-class
kicker in previous amateur shows, doesn’t satisfy
the admirers of that supple-waisted damsel.
* * * ♦
The principals in “Lelamine,” bar one
exception, are neither remarkably promising nor
ridiculously incompetent. Miss Rose Musgrove
is the exception. She plays a soubrette part with
natural dash and intelligence, making the most of
her words and dancing buoyantly. Miss Annie
Marr (soprano) and Miss ICitty Grindlay (con-
tralto) are satisfactory as vocalists and otherwise,
though the one has a queer lisp and the other
suffers from twang, The baritone in a Greek
pirate kilt contrives to pause on the brink of ab-
surdity. Nicol Turner, lean and nimble,
passes muster as an amateur funny man
when due allowance is made for the
author’s dulness. C. S. Cunningham stands up
and sings like a person who is not quite abashed
by his fancy costume, and the sudden death of one
Edward H. Shakell, in the first act, is not justifi-
able, considering that a couple of minor characters
don’t get buried with him. Touching the Earl of
Shaftesbury, The Bulletin feels baulked of its
prey. Yarmouth’s earl was a peg to hang a beauti-
ful Democratic moral upon, whereas Shaftesbury
walks and talks and wears the gaudiest uniform
imaginable (designed by Barnes) in a pleasing
manner. Looking at Shaftesbury, no one would
suppose that his blood was almost as blue as his
coat. Many tenors of no family pretensions what-
ever are inferior to him for general stage purposes.
Shaftesbury’s warble is only a gentle murmur,
which soon gets lost in a duet, but it is sweet and
sympathetic as far as it goes. An alarming truth
for the parents of sassiety girls to reflect upon.
A titled tenor, who carries a good stage appear-
ance ! No wonder that they turn pale when he
reaches for a top note. “ Matsa,” reconstructed in
the cast, will follow “ Lelamine ” on Saturday, and
run for a week or so prior to the production of
“A Night Off.”
* * * *
The demand for B racy’s company, at the
Bijou, became much weaker after “ Nemesis ” ap-
peared on the scene, and the end of the season is
due this week. For the last nights Bracy has re-
vived “The Sultan of Mocha,” an opera which
represents a large outlay of catchy music in a
hopeless cause. The fate of this revival is never
in doubt from the moment Knight Aston, the
name-part potentate, sings his awful introductory
notice, “Sultanaml,notabitshy. LookatmyEye.”
After a few repetitions of the aforesaid the public
mind is made up. Bracy has not made half
a million of money during his month in Mel-
bourne, yet there is clearly a disposition to sup-
port comic opera at reduced prices. If the youth-
ful veteran can get hold of something that is up-
to-date, and not burdened with low-comedy busi-
ness, he may call again at the same theatre and
expect to reap his reward.
* * * *
“In Sight of St. Paul’s” blazes away
cheerfully at the Royal, in spite of sundry indig-
nant snorts from the moral playgoer concerning
gilded vice and the dissolute behaviour of the
Panther’s back-hair. There is really nothing to
complain about. The baked Panther dies in a
hospital, and the bad young man gets shot, like-
wise Albert Norman is hanged for shooting him.
Provided that vice comes properly to grief at the
finish, it is all the better for being gilded in its
early stages.

Bois : “ It is not inappropriate for Francillon’s husband
to read the Petit Journal. There is a sly knock in
volved, in the innocence of the only newspaper that
ranges up and down the whole strata, from the Presi-
dent’s to the blanchisseuse’8.”
Boy-Politician ’ Caulfield, in his new character of
temperance-lecturer, advertises himself with quotations
from an old Bulletin notice, which hailed him as the
Great Apostle of Rum. He seems terribly in earnest this
time. The Drink Fiend had hold of him for five years he
says, “ but since he got enfreed from its clutches' he feels
like a fellow just liberated after doing a long term of
penal servitude with constant whippings,” &c.,~&c The
strange thing is that Caulfield, who has lived among many
men, should appear to feel himself unique in this state of
exaltation. He talks as though he were the first person
who had ever been “cured ” of the drink habit, whereas
he differs only from thousands of other intermittent con-
verts in the desire to pour forth unkind remarks about
the Fiend (good old D.F. !) in public. But maybe he has
one claim to originality. “I am not aware of any total-
abstainer ever before lecturing in aid of charitable-institu-
tions,” says Caulfield.
Coincidence : Ten years ago there were two young
jewellers named “ Jack ” Edwards and “ Dick ” Sceats in
business together in Auckland (M.L.). They were both
fine singers, sang in amateur opera together, went on the
stage professionally at the same time, and a month or so
back they both died on the same day.
Henry Irving lately cabled to New York denying that
there was an atom of truth in the ’Murkan story of his
having quarrelled with Ellen Terry. But, according to
unkind critics, it is quite time that Henry and Ellen did
quarrel.
Fred Neebe died a month or two since. The son of a
clergyman, and a good fellow in quite a different way to
his father’s, Neebe was an unctuous impersonator of
parsondom on the stage. He came to Australia with
Jennie Lee some years ago, to play the oily shepherd in
“ Jo,” and his method of calling down a blessing on
Snagsby’s back-parlor gave great satisfaction to the
irreverent.
Irving to Supe : “Confound me, sir, can’t you do as I
do ? ” “No, sir ; if I could, I’d get £‘30 a night instead of
30s. a week.”
Chirgwin, the white-eyed, is quite a family man, and
daily is seen walking his children about St. Hilda. But
he has more virtues than this. He has cultivated a
friendly knack of giving gold medals—real gold, not the
hollow mockeries of. amateur sports meeting—to those in
whom he gets especially interested. One or two cyclists,
including Charlie Kellow, have benefited by this delight-
ful habit, and see another plank between themselves and
a rainy day.. The medals have Chirgwin and his fiddle
and his hat in bold relief. The,y were struck before he
came to Australia, and, although, like Bonaparte, they
once seemed a little previous, latter receptions from the
great unwashed of Rickards’ have justified the amiable
Kaffir in bringing them along.

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
Coghlan, in the N.S.W. Statistical Register
for the present year (Part 111., page 329) gives an interest-
ing history of that great overdraft which is called by the
N.S.W. Foreign-trade press the “ Dibbs deficit.” The
figures don’t wholly agree with the annual revenue and
expenditure returns, because of the weird fashion in
which N.S.W. Treasurers charge expenses back to each
other ; it is simply a statement of the amount of the
overdraft at the end of each financial year without regard
to any after explanations. The figures show that Treas.
Dibbs, when he resigned office at the end of 1885, left a
balance to the good of £2901. In 1896—Jennings’ year as
Premier and Treasurer—an overdraft of £1,286,581 was
piled up. Parkes held office then till the end of 1891,
barring an interlude of 48 days, and added £719,269 to
the deficit; he left it at £2,005,850, Then Dibbs took
office for three years and added £211,749, making the
total shortage, so far, £2,217,599. After that Reid charged
over £400,000 of it to the permanent debt, reducing
it to about £1,800,000, and next he raised it to
£2,301,054 (in June, 1896). These figures are a wildly
hilarious commentary on the aggressive untruthful-
ness of the Foreign-trade press and politicians who
■have so much to say about the “ Dibbs’ deficit.” On the
facts and figures of the case, the deficit
belongs to everybody except the lengthy person whose
name is so persistently affixed to it. No less than £299,000
of it seems to lie at the door of that Parkes Cabinet of
•which William M'Millan was Treasurer. What’s the
matter with a “ M'Millan deficit” ?
* » » *
Westralia is floating another <£1,000,000 loan at
3 per cent. When its last 3 per cent, loan was offered the
minimum price was fixed at £9B, and it actually fetched
£lOO 16s. Bd. This was at the top of the boom, hast
February £1,100,000 Westralian 3 per cents, sold in Lon-
don with difficulty at about £97, and it then became
apparent that things were not quite what they had been.
The present loan is offered at a minimum of £95. This
will make £2,100,000 borrowed by Westralia this year,
•equal to about £l6 per head added to the province’s in-
debtedness inside five months. If the loan floats, W. A.
•will owe nearly £7,000,000, and will be making a vigorous
■sprint for a front seat among the heayilv-indebted pro-
vinces of Australia. At present Westralia has the.small-
■ est debt per head, but the new loan should put it well
ahead of Victoria and not very far behind N.S.W. ip this
■respect.
* * # #
The half-yearly report of the Bank of Austra-
lasia up to 12th ‘October, ’96, shows a net profit of
£40,399 compared with £40,568 at April, ’96, £41,158 at
October, ’95, and £40,854 and £45,735 for two previous
half-years. The 6 per cent, dividend swallows up £40,000,
-so there is a margin of £399 only, and the Australasia has
added just £715 to its accumulated profits in 2J years.
The fact that all the capital and reserve fund and
.£1,500,000 of the deposits are kept lying idle in the cellar,
out of a conservative yearning after enormous cash re-
serves, fully accounts for the comparative smallness pf
the profits. The Australasia owes the public £16,135,000,
and against this it holds £3,902,000 in cash and £2,050,000
in easily realisable securities, which is not only an im-
mensely strong line of defence but one which seems a
good deal stronger than is really necessary. The business
shows a small increase, but the Australasia takes very
few .risks, and looks for absolute security more than a
large turnover these times.
* ■* ♦ * ■*
An agitation is beginning to be heard of for the
amalgamation of the Colonial Bank with either the Vic-
toria or the Commercial. The union of the National
Mutual and Mutual of Victoria life offices broke the ice ;
and the combination of the Perpetual Executors and the
Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. was another move in
the right direction. Now that the .idea is started it is
probable that some more superfluous institutions will
follow suit, and thus save much needless expense and
staff and premises and brass plate. If the Colonial does
combine it will be a case rather of .selling the business
than of amalgamation. The Colonial ordinary share-
holders have no capital invested in their own alleged hank
now, and though a £2 call has been-made it will probably
require a hard struggle to collect it. At present they are
in the unique position of owing their-institution £42,000
to square losses. , * *
A creditor of the A. J.S. Bank put in a very
justifiable wail when the second reconstruction scheme
came before Judge Manning last Monday. He had a
fixed deposit, which had been locked up since at
per cent., and was now to be further locked up at 3 per
cent., or a little more if profits admitted. He also had
an overdraft on which he was paying the “ usual rate,
which presumably means something like 7 per cent. He
couldn’t pay off' the overdraft in cash, and the bank
'wouldn’t let him square it with his fixed deposit, valuing
£he latter at 20s. in the £. At present market rates it
would take £BOO worth of fixed deposit to pay off £5OO ot
overdraft. The Judge refused to consider the position of
the applicant; still, his grievance seems a very genuine
one. When the bank owed a depositor, at the time of the
smash, say, £BOO, and he owed the bank £5OO, the reason-
able proceeding, in the opinion of most people, would
have been to set one against the other, and simply recon-
struct the £3OO balance. To seize the £BOO and claim the
right to harry and sell up the customer for the £5OO looks
like creating 1 a very large amount of moral stench for a
very small profit.
* * *
It is reported by cable that the British deposi-
tors of the A. J.S. Bank require the retirement of Manager
Adams and three of the directors, and only assented to
the latest reconstruction on these terms. This is the dif-
ference between the British and the Australian depositor.
In all the reconstructions the Australian depositor has
never insisted on anything. He has never organised com-
mittees of inspection as the British person did, or dictated
terms to the management. In fact, he has never shown
that he has a single kick in him. Every time he has
accepted practically anything that the arrogant bank-
director liked to offer him, and accepted it thankfully, by
a big majority, without inquiry, and sometimes he has
passed an enthusiastic vote of thanks to the defaulting
bank for letting him have it.
* * -1
The animal election of directors of the Colonial
Mutual Life Assurance Society was accompanied by a
certain amount of disturbance. The two retiring
directors got in again by 1310 and 1306 votes, against an
indignant new man who received only 783. Under the
Colonial Mutual’s articles of association, it was alleged, a
Droxv is not only good for the election for which it is
Tret given, but can be used at any number of after
elections so long as it isn’t definitely revoked, and so long
as the paper will hold together. This seems to be a
beautiful device for keeping the old orowd continuously
in office. On the present occasion it was complained that
496 of the votes which put the old directors back into
office were old proxies—mostly from eight to ten years
old—which had apparently been accumulated in the office
for use whenever required. A number of others
were objected to as being irregular, and one of
the scrutineers supported the defeated candidate’s
objection. If the allegations are well-founded then the
alleged control of affairs by the policy-holders doesn’t
amount to very much, and it is a lovely plan for keeping
any new men from getting in to see what the old men
have been doing. If they are not true, they should be
promptly disproved. In last week’s issue The Bulletin
§ave its reasons for believing that the Colonial Mutual
adlv requires some new blood in it and a whole lot of
careful overhaul. It was bossed almost all its life by one
strong-minded manager, Jaques Martin, the Man that’s
Dead. It has had the same directors at head office arid
the same auditors so far back as the writer’s information
goes. And too much of the Happy Family arrangement
isn’t good for any institution.
• • * *
The Australian Mutual Fire Insurance Society,
the most consistently prosperous concern of its kind in
Australia, shows another good year’s work. The net
premiums (£29,334) are about £BOO more than in ’95 and
a trifle less than in '94, and, after paying all losses and
expenses, and writing-off over £2200 for depreciation, the
profit on insurance business is £lO,llB. (The losses in
this remarkable co. are only £4687). Interest on invest-
ments yields £5733, against £6059 and £5797 for the two
previous years —equal to about 4)4 per cent. Including
£922 brought forward, there is £16,773 available. An 8
per cent, dividend to shareholders absorbs £2OOO ; bonus
of 20 per cent, to policy-holders takes £7302; £4OOO is
added to reserve (making it £94,000), and £3OOO to con-
tingency-fund (making £6000). These additions make
£lOO,OOO of reserves accumulated in 25 years —the
largest fire-insurance reserve save one in Australia, and
the largest, bar three, in the seven provinces. The Aus-
tralian Mutual does business in N.S.W. only, and is a
valuable proof of the advantages of the mutual system, and
of keeping the business closely concentrated, so that it is
under the managerial eye.
* » * .*
The balance-sheet of the Equitable Life As-
surance Society of the United States shows that the
premium income for 1896 was £7,797,325 ; interest on
investments, £1,860,317 ; total income, £9,657,642. This
is about £400,000 more than the figure for 1895, and
near!}' £600,000 more than for 1894. Of this, £3,245,496
is added to the accumulated funds, making them
£44,821,558. Expenses swallo.w up £1,679,163, which is
equal to 21£ per cent, on premiums, or very pearly 18 per
cent, oh total income. For an office of the Equitable’s
size and age this is a ver3 r heavy percentage, much
heavier than that of the A-M.P. Society or the Maori-
land Government Department. Also, the rate is
higher than it looks on the surface. The Equitable,
when a surrender value is applied tP purchase a paid-
up policy, includes it among pew premiums, which
it certainly isn’t, and thus makes the premium income
look larger and the percentage of expenses look corres-
pondingly smaller than they really are.
The Equitable has the splendid .surplus of
£8,995,090-the biggest insurance surplus on earth. But
it still bases its calculations on the assumption that its
-funds will continue to earn 4 per cent, indefinitely, arid
as the interest actually earned was £4 6s. 2d. per cent,
in 1894, £4 3s. lOd. per' cent, in 1895 arid £4 6s. per
cent, in 1896, the margin is a dangerously small
one— too small, considering the steady fall in interest-
rates all over the world, to justify the assumption
of a permanent 4 per cent. rate. If the Equitable
assumed only per cent, instead of 4, as some of the
Australian offices are beginning to do, it would still have
a large surplus, but it wouldn’t be near so overwhelming
a surplus as it is now. This same remark applies to many
pther American offices. A 4 per cent, rate and an immense
surplus are just as secure as a 3)4 per cent, rate and a much
smallqr surplus, but they are not any mote secure, and
the public should make a mental note of this fact.

Thompson and Sons’ (Sydney) share- report
for May, ’97, still includes the Bank of North Queens-
land’s reconstructed deposits ariiong current securities,
quite regardless of the fact that they were all repaid
nearly two years ago. It quotes Commercial of Australia
deposits as carrying 4J per cent, interest and repayable in
five yqars from the original due date, whereas the Com-
mercial has fixed up a new agreement and altered both
these things. It evidently hasn’t heard of the Q.N.
Bank’s new arrangement yet, and it quotes National Bank
deposits as repayable in 1898, 1899 and 1900, regardless of
the fact that all the 1898 deposits are repaid, and the last
of, the 1899 lot will be cleared off in a few months. Nothing
fike being thoroughly up to date.
Some puzzles of N.S.W. fruitgrowing: One
grower sent 26 cases of cherries to one Sydney agent
and 26 cases to another, the cherries being of
equal quality. One agent returned 6s. 6d. per case, the
other 2s. 6d. Another grower sent a large lot of fine
plums. The agent, when dunned for a return, replied
that the sale only met expenses, but that he was willing
to receive another lot. Another grower came to Sydney,
and, being personally unknown, bought his own fruit
from the agent at 9s. 6d., and, when he went back, the
agent wrote stating the price he sold at was 6s. 6d. One
grower has sent fruit to several agents, and has been un-
able to get any returns from any of them. Another nar-
rates ; “ I got a friend in Sydney to call on my agent one
Saturday and ask for a few cases of my fruit. The agent
replied, ‘ All sold.’ Next week, I received a letter from
that agent saying, ‘ I couldn’t sell till Monday, when I
had to almost sacrifice your consignment.’ ” The narrator
adds, “ Then I gave up fruitgrowing.” This is pathetic ;
but where is the remedy ? Perhaps, if law were a little
more sure and less expensive, a dose of it might do some
of these gentry good. But it wouldn’t cure all. Some
growers advocate handing over their sales to the Chinese
That, however, looks like the blindness of desperation.
Co-operation in distribution is the keyword. It may be
difficult to compass, but co-operate—co-operate.
* * * *
Block 14 (which pays a dividend this month) is
now relying almost entirely upon its own ores; the
extraction of sulphides during the past six months was 50
per cent, greater than during the preceding term. Never-
theless almost the whole of it is taken from above the
300 ft level; below that the sulphides are, so far as
exposed, generally hard. It is, however, thought best to
treat the more friable ores while the machinery is being
perfected : as the treatment of slimes, &c., advances, it
becomes possible to more economically treat those ores
which require finer grinding. Additions to these slime-
treating implements continue to be made, and still more
are contemplated.
* * * *
Block 14 persists |in the excellent policy, ini-
tiated by Zeb. Lane, of closely filling up the worked-out
stopes with refuse. , * *
Mr. Gluyas, general manager of the South B.H.
mine, has resigned, and goes to Westralia, after bringing
the mine to the dividend stage. Latterly, Mr. T. J.
Greenwav was appointed consulting engineer, and this is
at the bottom of the resignation. Green way thinks that a
great deal of money has been mis spent, and has advised
that preparationsforstripping ore in large quantities ought
to be more advanced. Recent prospecting work has given
disappointing results so far as quality of ore is concerned,
and it has become necessary to arrange for treating more
of the very large reserves of the poorer stuff. To do this
extra plant will be needed. Generally, the immediate
prospects for shareholders are not bright. For the
present a general manager, in succession to Gluyas, is not
to be appointed. The system adopted at Block 14 is to be
followed ; this will give the consulting engineer and the
Adelaide people, with whom he is moatly associated, a
freer hand. Certainly they have not done badly with
Block 14.

B.H. North concentrating machinery is settling
down to better work.
* * * *
B.H. Junction has placed an order for new con-
centrating plant, to be added to the existing works, with
a Kapunda (S.A.) firm, which has also in hand the addi-
tions for Block 10.
• * * *
Though the lead-market has firmed very much,
the exceptionally low price of silver makes silver-lead
mining where a railway does not run to the shaft ex-
tremely precarious. Thackaringa, only five miles from
the Silverton line, has some of the best small lead
“shows” on the Barrier; yet, notwithstanding the “re-
vival,” 495 tons of crude, picked ore constituted the
entire export from that district for the 12 months ended
April 30. The figures are not consoling to shareholders
in some of the recently-floated Junction Extendeds and
Broken Hill Extendeds similarly placed.
* * « •
A beginning has been made with the erection of
the large new concentrating plant at the Junction. Simul-
taneously King shaft is being equipped for the hauling of
ore, &c. —possibly to help supply this new and large plant,
possibly in view of the danger of a further strain upon
the other shafts affected by the recent creep. B.H. Pro-
prietary experience is that no shaft in the neighborhood
of a subsidence can ever be depended upon.
* * » »
Sulphide Corporation’s works at Cockle Creek,
Newcastle, were put into full work at the beginning of
April, and have since been running smoothly. J. S. Reid,
Australian chairman of directors, says that during the
past 12 months the mine has yielded, besides ore stored,
55,300 tons which has been sold. The lode in the mine,
as developed, is 850 ft. long, and from 60 to 150 ft. wide.
* * * *
The much-talked-of Hillgrove and Armidale
Water-power Electric Co., which was to supply all
the mines and townships in the County of Sandon
(N.S. W.) with electric-light and power, and build a tram
from Armidale to Hillgrove and sundry other works,
seems to have fallen on evil days. When its works were
erected, at a cost of over £20,000, the wooden fluming was
found defective, much of the water to be conveyed to the
pelton wheels being lost en route. Then drought came
along, and the alleged “never-failing streams” dried
up. The original people are mostly out of the co., now
known as the New England Electric Co., and it is practi-
cally in a comatose state.

“ Pending negotiations for placing the Big Hill
G.M. Co. (N.S.W.) under offer to an English buyer, with
the object of providing additional working capital, it has

Sabbath-tipped mine !
• * • *
Nope but a fool would attempt to jump Bull’s
leases in Westralia. There was a time when the wardens
and Govt, lent much encouragement to jumping. The
finders of the Londonderry failed to get their block leases
back. The reason is too well known. Things have much
changed. Several attempts have been made to jump the
Hainault mine, worked by a Bull co. The warden, with
the aid of the pqlice, recently removed all persons from
the lease and allowed the co. to re-peg, and, says the
local paper—“ It is to be hoped this will be the last effort
made to dispossess the co. of its mine, as the news of
Such moves is bound to have a bad effect on the London
investors in our mines.” Every consideration for the
.London investor, who, having been robbed and otherwise
victimised in the boom, is not taking any at present.
• * • *
Norseman, W.A. : “An important develop-
ment has taken place in the Three Colonies Extended.
Very rich specimens have been obtained, and the gold is
very coarse. The future of the mine is assured. Locally
there is considerable excitement, and contributing shares
have changed hands at Is. 3d.” If that is not enough to
make any mining-man sick, what would ? Rich speci-
mens, coarse gold, future of mine assured, local excite-
ment, and scares fifteen-pence. O Boom, where art thou?
* * * *
A Victim ” writes to Sydney D.T. on Queens-
land Menzies mine, W.A. It was boomed on two or
three 6oz. crushings, the reefs were big, plenty of 6oz.
ore in sight, and divs., regular and good, were fore-
shadowed. Shares ran up to 335. After the first crush-
ing and div. they came down, and have been coming
down, down ever since. The manager resigned, and th«.
new man reports that the tons have been too big, the
gold is of poor quality, the reef much disturbed in places ;
also he only talks of “ fair’’gold. This gives “ Victim ”
the blues, and raises his bile. Three years the writer has
been indicating, as far as he dare, that this sort- of busi-
ness was going on in nine-tenths of the W.A. mines. It
is the Liars’ Land, where truth can barely exist. Even
the good mines of the country, as far as public respect is
concerned, have been killed by gross exaggeration. Com-
pare, for instance, the wires appearing several times a
week in papers like the S.M.H. and Argus of the veins of
almost pure gold in the Great Boulder, the immense size
and number of reefs, etc., with the information given of
the developments of the Mt. Morgan mine, or any other
mine of this category outside of Westralia. Compare
them with the report of the Boulder itself by the manager,
Hamilton, who shows that the reefs run from one foot
thick to eight, and average less than four. Of course,
some day when the mischief is done, the papers will
awake to the fact that these crams are already received by
the public with pitying contempt. A later report from
the manager of the Q.M. mine says the lode is made up
of blocks or shoots of stone with blanks between, and is
otherwise erratic and hard to follow.
« * » *
Still later, a rumor that a wire had been received
from the manager that “ the mine never looked better ”
stopped the slump in the shares. Well, if the mine never
looked better than when the reef runs from a foot to two
feet thick, and only in shoots at that, and is broken and
disturbed and hard to follow, how must it have looked
when the public were made to believe it was twice as
thick, wonderfully regular and rich, and that an average
of 6oz. could easily be kept up?
* * * *
Big Blow, Coolgardie. Cable sent to London
office : “Cleaning up, but water at present supplied much
too salt; gold can be seen floating into the tailings.”
Later : “ Crushed 90 tons, yielding 16oz. gold ; average
sample of tailings, 12dwt. per ton.” Note by secretary :
“ This return indicates that the quartz treated was of an
average value of 15dwt. 13gr.” The cables also indicate
that the mine is well-named. The gold would need to be
much richer than 15dwt. to be seen floating away in
tailings. Will the co.’s balance-sheet oredit value of
tailings ?
* * * *
If the returns of crushings from Peak Hill
mines, W.A., furnished by Frank Reed, of Cue, are cor-
rect, that camp is proving itself one of the finest of W.A.
fields. Coming to the front after the boom, the mines,
not Bull, have to be worked for all they are worth.
Crushed from all mines up to the 14th March, 1918 tons,
for 77700 z. gold ; average per ton, a little over 4oz. Prin-
cipal producers : Daisy Bell, 112 tons, 11930 z.; Peak Hill
reef, 259 tons, 12910 z.; Golden Chimes, 167 tons, 815 oz.;
small parcels, 144 tons, 17730 z. The ore crushed consists
chiefly of quartz, kaolin, and cement, and it is claimed
that, owing to the slimes created by the kaolin, from one
to four ounces have been lost in the tailings.

“ Professor Krause’s report, which will be ready
in a day or two, will, it is said, controvert many pessi-
mistic rumors concerning' the value of the (General
Gordon) mine.” This was published three weeks back.
Had it been of this character the chances are the public
would have heard all about it. Having heard next to
nothing, it may be taken that the Pro. has told the cold-
blooded truth about this over-boomed show, of which more
lies have been told than perhaps of any other mine in the
Liars' Land, and that is saying something. It was said to
have started crushing five weeks ago. When the shares
were standing at 265. one absurd tipster advised their
purchase, and has been doing so ever since. They are
now down to 3s. 6d., and he still says,.“on the most re-
liable information, that the mine is as good as ever."
Which may mean that it is just as bad as ever.
'** » «
The West Australian Venture Syndicate is a big
advertiser in W. A. It gives a gorgeous list of the explora-
tion co.’s it is associated with, the names of the mining
engineers who are retained to inspect, and adds : “ Mining
properties bought, floated or financed. Notice to mine
buyers—The W.A. Venture Syndicate has always on hand,
for sale, developed and partially-developed mining pro-
perties at Hannan’s and other districts, particulars of
which will be forwarded on application.” No doubt. All
the exploration co.’s have mines on hand, and, now the
boom is dead, the weekly turn-over in mines is not tqp
numerous. Anyhow, the advt. is so attractive that the
writer contemplates asking if they have a not too well
developed mine at about 30 bob. If so, he may become a
buyer.

things talking of another boom.
* * * •
For impudence in anticipation, the Kalgoorlie
wire-hummer to the Argus and S.M.H. wears the belt.
The public have become so tired that the market budges
not an inch, and the news is only read with pitying con-
tempt for the papers that publish it. The latest : “No
returns have yet been received for the Associated Gold
Mines from the Australian group since the first parcel,
but it is estimated that the mine is turning out about
8000 oz. of gold monthly, 400 tons being sent away every
week.” This co., it is known, has got two or three fine
leases amongst a great number of purely prospecting
blocks, the working of which runs away with a barrel of
money. In spite of the grand developments in the Aus-
tralia and Australia East Blocks, the shares are lower
now than they were a year ago. In a dead boom, London
is not taking any “ pups ”of a cat-like kind. Simple little
truths like these never come out in the newspapers which
have played a big part in Westralian boodleism.

last year. East Coolgardie— i.e., Kalgoorlie—l4,222oz.,

cially produced loz.

Mt. Bischoff tin-mine, Tas., has declared a 7s.
6d. div., the 241st, making to date £1,456,500. Doubtful
if all the other tin-mines of Australasia lumped would
equal this.
« * * •
Active operations, says a correspondent, are to
be immediately begun at the Liberator (BJue Tier, Tag.)
mine. The original owner was quite satisfied to effect a
good sale of this show on shallow indications, and, the
necessary buyer happening along, there was no need for
fooling about in the bowels of the earth. The new pro-
prietors may do all the fooling they like, and if, instead
of knocking the bottom out of the mine, they happen to
strike it extravagantly rich, the wily Bakhap will pro-
bably be the first to congratulate them.
• * * «
The chairman and manager of the South Star,
Vic., have circularised the shareholders, with the view of
easing their minds —“ so many rumors and false state-
ments having recently been circulated about the mine.”
It is denied that the payable ore in sight will only last 12
months ; the Age and Argus are wrong in saying a reduc-
tion of hands would soon have to be seriously considered ;
the working of the tailings by cyanide has not been a
failure. Reference is made to the quantity of gold still
left in the solution and charcoal, and attention is drawn
to the fact that “the cost of carting and crushing, prior
to the completion of our own battery, was 6s. per ton,
while the present cost of crushing and cyaniding together
only reaches 3s. B£d. per ton.” Which seems too cheap to
be effective.

SOCIETY
Melb. Benevolent Asylum, judging from
the wail of an unhappy inmate who writes to The
Bulletin, is a very pathetic establishment on
Tuesdays and Fridays. There is no meat included
in the bill of fare these two days per week, but
merely potatoes and bread served upon bare
tables. Little pyramids of salt adorn the festive
board at intervals, and some fifty or sixty blind
old men may >be observed wiping the table with
their respective ’taters as they grope feebly for the
nearest salt-heap. The “ Bbnevolents ” don’t
seem to wallow in luxury at any time. Mention
of butter melts them to tears. Lately they were
favored with donations of butter from outside
philanthropists, whereupon the management
rushed into print with an announcement that such
dairy produce was scarcely required in an asylum
where treacle and dripping gave pleasure to all.
Yet the dripping supply is painfully limited. A
few of the more active lodgers dodder into the
kitchen twice a week to collect fat from the cook,
whilst the large majority stop away and gnash
their toothless gums in despair of being able to
get fat when there is none left to scramble for.
As for treacle, the allowance is only Jib. every
alternate week, and the “ Benevolent ” who has
secured his dose of treacle is burdened with the
difficulty of findinsr a place to keep it to himself.
As a rule, he plants it in the garden, if nobody is
looking, and recovers the tin by stealth— always
presuming that he possesses a tin, and isn’t obliged
to devour the sticky allowance at one sitting. In
which case he dies from over-fet-ding, and gets
buried without military honors.

saw offered at auction a silver casket wliich had
cost £BO, and which had been placed with an en-

Just read the last sentence of the Address
to Queen Victoria adopted unanimously by both
N.S.W. Houses of Parliament last Thursday :
We humbly trust that your life may long be preserved ;
and we dutifully assure your Most Gracious Majestj- that
in no portion of your vast dominions are the sentiments of
loyalty and love for your Throne and Person more warmly
cherished than by. the inhabitants of New South Wales.
Who is responsible for this masterpiece of prose
the Chancellor of the University or the Minister
for Education ?
* * * *
Sydney Hospital-Saturday collection was
admirably stage-managed, the prettiest and best-
dressed collectors being put in the front row about
town, while out in the suburbs was equivalent to
the third row of a ballet. The collectors say that
women don’t give, though one lady walked from
King-street to the Quay with a shilling in her
hand waiting in vain to be a«ked for it. Why not
try a men’s collection ? Julius Superbus Kuight,
Burns-Walker, Scot Inglis, and other idols would
have garnered in whips of coin, for even frisky
maids who only come to cough would have re-
mained to pay.
* * * *
The Victorian mounted rifles, who have
gone ’Ome, had all their baggage and their note-
paper (!) marked with the words “Queen’s Es-
cort.’’ Now none of the colonial troops will form
any part of Queen Victoria’s escort at the June
flare-up. The old lady’s escort is always, on State
occasions, composed of the Life Guards, and it
will be so this time. The colonial troops will pro-
bably not be within a mile of H.M.’s carriage.
As a matter of fact, the Vic. mounted rifles will
act as escort to a stout, stubbly-bearded, spec-
tacled, and shabby little man by the name of
Turner, and his matronly spouse.' What fuu will
be poked at that cockily pretentious “Queen’s
Escort ” label on the other side !

• * *
There is something pathetic
in the way the dying man holds
on to his quack. In Melb. some
time ago a “cancer specialist”
was convicted and sentenced for
killing one of his patients ; his
procedure was to daub the cancer-
ous place with arsenic, and the
sufferer died either of cancer or
the arsenious agony, or both. On
that occasion he nearly got off
through the impassioned support of
a female witness, who told how he
had “ cured ” her of cancer. Then
the cured patient went home and
died of the same old cancer before
ttie specialist had fairly got out of
his trouble. When he emerged he
proceeded to cure another patient
of the same complaint, and the re-
sult was a verdict of manslaughter
by the coroner’s jury and another
committal for trial. Whereupon
an agricultural person got up and
explained that the specialist was
treating him for cancer, and that
his faith was still so solid that lie
was prepared to bail him out there
and then. And he did bail him
put, presumably for fear he should
lose the advantage of his services.
Still, when you have an incurable
disease, and only one greasy old
man in all the world can save you
by putting a sticky rag on you,
and that man has an inveterate
habit of being run in on charges of
killing other people by the same
treatment, and you know you will
die for want of him while he is in
gaol—well, what can you do but
bail him out ?
The Australian Star :
Deceased was not seen alive again until 10 hours later
when his dead body was found with the horses and
vehicle intact some 200 yards away.
Sure, we’re all Irish.

Master Dave Riddle,
whose portrait appears in
this issue, mounted on his
father’s horse, Streak o’
Light, and facing the jump
cleared by him on Bathurst
(N.S.W.) showground on the
previous day, is probably
the only 12-year-old lad yet
sired who has taken a horse
over a jump six feet and half
an inch in height. Young
Riddle has the credit in
the Gowra district, where
his father, Mr. Walter
Riddle, is well-known as a
breeder of good horses, of
being able to sit anything
lapped in hide. In addition
to his sticking ability, he
has “ hands ” possessed by
few matured horsemen, and
the confidence and skill
with which he takes Lis
mount up to the jumps
and lifts him over is a
treat to witness. After the
high jump the saddle was
taken off, and young Riddle
took Streak over the show-
jumps and log fence at
steeplechasing pace bare-
backed.

In a recent Melbourne. maintenance-case
one item of evidence for plaintiff was a pillow-
case full of hair which she alleged defendant had
torn out of her head during 20 years of married
life. Defendant alleged that the hair had come
out naturally; also, that it had come out faster
than usual owing to continued dissipation, as he
had been bailing the plaintiff out ever sinci 1871 ;
likewise, that if he had pulled any of it out it was
done accidentally while tryingto haul the lady home
at night; further, that if he pulled it out intention-
Miss CHARLOTTE HEMMING, the young Victorian reciter.
Photo., Falk Studios.
ally it was only a reasonable amount of hair to
pull out, considering the provocation and the 2C>
years allowed for haulage. Finally, he alleged
that she had borrowed hair from the woman next
door, and other persons, to make up the collection
and excite sympathy, and that her hair was of an
inferior variety which came out easily. Case
adjourned just here.

Mount Morgan (Q.) state school was built
some six years ago to accommodate 300 children
It now holds 800. Doctor’s official reports sav
there is not breathing room for half the number-
the space allowed being only 3ft. sq. as against
Bft. allotted by law. Reports also state that the
germs of consumption, etc., are being dealt out in
large quantities. And all this ’cause why ’
Because the Education Dept, is centralised in
Brisbane—and the Mt. Morgan wail is a very
feeble noise in the ears of Southern officialdom
300 miles away.
* * * *
The Dowager Duchess of Bedford’s ashes
should help along the cause of the projected crema-
torium at St. Kilda (Melb.) cemetery, concerning
which there is just now a furious outcry in the
neighborhood. A complaint that the St. Kilda
boneyard district is rather thickly populated
conies with astounding impudence from certain
angry howlers whose land was originally included
in the cemetery reserve. When these influential per-
sons lodge their protest with the Government as
they probably will, they had better be called upon
to explain how they, or their ancestors, happen to
be living upon property which was long ago set
apart for the mouldering dead. Anyway, their
objection to the possible aroma of crematorium
cookery can be met, it is said, with the argument
that their freeholds have a much nastier stink
than the smoke from a burning sinner.

Ed. Bulletin,— Re de-
mand for shorthand clerks and
typists. There is a demand
here (Adelaide), at all events—
and I am under the impres-
sion that it is the same else-
where—nt a salary. Business
men expect to get capable sten-
ographers (the proper term) at
about 10s. a week—and they
do get them. One of our lads
writing 150 words a minute,
and capable in every respect,
applied for a position and was
offered the usual 10s. a week.
When he stated that he ex-
pected £l, the mountain of fat
requiring his services erupted.
The real causes of the trouble
are want of organisation among
stenographers, and the fatness
of fat. Phono. (P.S. —As
showing that the lad referred
to was capable, I may say he
now engaged as correspond-
ing clerk with a big firm at ill
A week —a record for Adelaide.)

* * *
The sweater is much ab-
road in Brisbane. A seam-
stress makes 71 d. on a pair of
trousers ; machinist 4d. : finish-
er 3Jd. Coat, waistcoat and
trousers are built up at a total
cost of 4s. 6d. (public buys at
£2). Kanaka shirts are made
at ljd. apiece ; Dungarees cost
a penny for making and 2d. for
finishing threepence in all;
kanaka coats fetch in sixpence
to the maker. The term kanaka
will yet be a synonym for the
products of sweated labor.

Henry Gyles Turner, of the Commercial
Bank (Melb.), speaking as chairman of St. Kilda
cemetery directors, considers that Collins-street is
a proper place for a crematorium. And Turner
knows Collins-street is full of busted securities
and cancelled bank “ capital ” which ought to be
burned with solemn funeral rites.
Re the onslaught upon the proposal to build
a crematorium at St. Kilda, one of the at-
tacking forces is led by a tombstone-maker.
Immediately behind this aggressive warrior
the great regiment of undertakers is mar-
shalled in battle array, the rest of the army
being mainly composed of snuffling pietists and
worms, for “Christian burial’’ is a matter of

proved the bane of Australian communities, has
attracted the attention of a Melb. firm of special-
ists (Messrs. Holdsworth and Co.), who invite the
public, by announcement elsewhere, to consult
them on the subject of a definite remedy.*

H.R.H. the Princess of Wales has appealed to the public
to contribute to a fund towards providing a week’s
dinner for the poorest residents of the London slums in
connection with the celebration of the 60th vear of the
reign of Her Majesty the Queen.
Tire same evening Her Royal Highness was informed
by cable from Melbourne that “Australia is
20,000 fat sheep and 500 fat bullocks to the Princess of
Wales for her dinners to the poor.”— (Argus.)
N. : “A girl, having a magnificently
healthy illegitimate infant, was sentenced for
perjury in a Melbourne court the other fore-
noon. Spite of her awful distress, the Judge
refused to let her have her child ; then he went
home to dinner. Observe the Grundyite moral.
That infant’s business is to Die. ‘lt will be well
taken care of,’ said the Judge. Of course. The
Judge did not say the mother ought to have killed
it herself. Society does. ”

At Melb. police court a woman, charged
with driving at night without a light, pleaded
that she had a lamp, but had omitted to put a
candle in it. “Ah,” chipped in Panton, P.M.,
“ you are like the foolish virgin who forgot the
oil.” The weak one fired up instantly: “I am
not a foolish virgin ; I am married and have six
children.” Fined 2s. 6d.

Kleptomania
is a craze which
attaoks some
people periodi-
cally ; with
others it is chro-
nic. A man
started a restau-
rant in a Wes-
tralian town and
began thieving
in a business-
like way. At
night he went
round and stole
water for domes-
tic purposes;
from different
local camps he
grabbed fire-
wood ; also, he
stole horsefeed
from stables,rob-
bing the horse of
its -well - earned
supper. At last
he was caught,
and got six
months’ hard in
a sea-side gaol.
Taken away
with others in a
conveyance to
the nearest rail-
way-station, at
each camping-
place on the road
he 'stole every-
thing he could
lay his hands on
—spoons, knives
and forks, pan-
nikins, etc., and
stowed them in
the horsefeed
which they car-
ried. The plun-
der was all dis-
covered by the
policeman in
charge, who was surprised to find one of the
horses with a silver spoon in its mouth.

At Bairnsdale (Vic.) Assizes, the other
week, a youth convicted of a capital offence was
released on his own recognisances. Capital joke !
THE FIVE FLYING JORDANS.
Photo., Marceau, San Francisco.
All last Thursday morning Collins-street
East was dotted with groups of red-headed, black-
coated men with shaven upper-lips and aggressive
cheek-bones, who gathered closer and closer to the
Presbyterian Assembly Hall as 11 o’clock ap-
proached. It was to be a great day; for at 11 a
converted Hebrew was expected to come forward
and publicly announce his acceptance of Presby-
terianism, porridge, and pork. More than usual
interest hinged on this, for only a few weeks

back the son pf a certain rabbi was, it was
reported, to have been publicly received, but
the Presbytery was “ slipped uj) at the last
moment.” This time better preparations had
been made. The hall was packed, and, as the
hand slowly approached the fateful hour,
the strain was intense. At last, just at the
tick of 11, a short, dark young man crushed
through the standing crowd at the doors and
walked boldly down the aisle. A thousand
heads turned, and a hum of applause rose in in-
tensity to a roar as he marched past the assembled
parsons in the front row and seated himself at a
table in front of the moderator. Unable to stand
it any longer, three cheers were given, and then
tlie congregation calmed itself to hear the convert
leitify. But the short, dark young man seemed
serenely ignorant of the ovation, and coolly pro-
ducing a roll of copy-paper from his pocket
awaited developments. The Presbyterians had
been wasting their religious fervor on an unre-
generate reporter —a heathen who had come for
news, not salvation. As for the alleged convert,
he never arrived, after all, and the rumor circu-
lating among the remnants of the congregation
about 2 p.m. was that he had struck for better
terms.
* * * *
Detective O’Donnell, who travelled from
Melb. to London and back, per Oceana, with a
vague hope of “ clearing up ” the gold-robbery
case, confesses himself baffled. O’Donnell and the
authorities who despatched him on his picnic,
might as well explain the working of their gigantic
intellects in this matter. Did they think it pos-
sible that the robbers of 5000 sovereigns from the
Oceana’s strong room would not be smart enough
to get away with the booty before the ship left
port? Surely their suspicions must have fallen
upon some ex-members of the detective force.
One wonders where the admirable artist is
now, and how he is spending the money so
cleverly acquired. Is lie living iridescently
in gay Paris, drinking “the foaming wine of
Eastern Prance,” and caressed by fair women
—a Sardanapalus for six glittering months, or
(with economy) a year? Or has Fie, peradven-
ture, entered a monastery, and endowed it with the
remains of his spoil ? Or is he living quietly in
Melbourne —within hail of the Detective-office—as
a gentleman of easy means ? He may have been a
Mahatma (to which solution of the problem Detec-
ti veO’Donnell seems to seriously incline) who carried
the swag away on his astral back to use it as the
nucleus of a fund to establish an Esoteric Nun-
nery, with Annie Besant as Abbess, in far Thibet.
Or he may have been an alchemist who badly
needed the gold for his crucible, or, perhaps, a
missionary with a mania to procure, at any cost,
coin wherewith to purchase (ra a distant country)
Bibles and patent-leather boots and scented soap
for the pore, benighted heathen. He may, in fact,
have been anything but a mere vulgar burglar. If
he had been the latter, Detective O’Donnell would
have brought him back ignominiously by his
thiok-lobed ear. It is a pretty problem as it
stands. Couldn’t Conan Doyle resurrect Sher-
lock Holmes —for one occasion only—to tackle it?

MEATING THE DIFFICOLTY.
The Queen has been graciously reigning
Full sixty long years for our good,
Yet the moon, ever waxing and waning,
Still beams upon subjects complaining
That fate doesn’t find ’em in food.
They groan ’midst a tumult of grovel ;
But Charity, smiling and sleek,
Will call them from fever-gerined hovel
To take part in a festival novel
Of Meat—in the Jubilee week.
Sing hey ! for Her Highness befriending
The pauper who starves at her door.
How thoughtful her project for tending
The wants of a world without ending
Where people seem born to be poor. °
Her wishes were loyally noted,
And promptly was laid, at her feet
By .numerous servants devoted
(Their names in the papers are quoted)
A tribute of Jubilee meat.
Sing hey 1 for the multitude sighting
Fair visions of mutton and beef ;
In dreams they are greedily fighting
For chances of chewing and biting
The “ grub ” that gives solace to grief.
Meantime they get thinner and thinner :
Their faces are pallid with pain—
With pain in the regions called “ inner ” ;
But soon they will wallow in dinner,
Then—back to starvation again.
One week of sublime indigestion
Should make them contented and gay ;
Her Highness’s simple suggestion
Has settled the Socialist question
■ At last in a practical way.
Let each man be carefully treated
To morsels of fat with the lean,
And the demagogue’s cause is defeated :
For the masses, persuasively meated,
Ask only for “ God Save the Queen.”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 11, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
A Melbourne girl, Miss Phoebe Howitt (a grand-
daughter, by the way, of William and Mary
Howitt), has carried off the ladies’ lawn-tennis
championship at this year’s matches, and Vic.
folk, of whom there are many in Sydney just
now, are jubilating much thereat. The finals
were played at the S.C.G. on Saturday in a detest-
able wind which gave the prowling snap-shotters
present excellent chances for sensational repro-
ductions of flying skirts and spread-eagle atti-
tudes. The Maoriland player, Miss Nunneley,
from whom the championship has been wrested,
went at her work with grim determination, and,
despite her vigorous heavy strokes and her ener-
getic vertiginous leaps into the air, never lost her
pale, intense earnestness. The two Dransfield
girls, and, in fact, all the local cracks, gave jerky,
uncertain dives at the ball and wore a nervous,
worried look.
Miss Howitt was almost the only girl who
seemed to be playing for the fun of the thing, and
had a pleased smile as she gave her steady, dexter-
ous drives. At the prize-giving she practically
devastated the glittering stand. She has now
enough table bric-a-brac to make her quite inde-
pendent of wedding-presents when she marries,
and a whole phalanx of silver shields.
Some of the quarantined Himalaya people are
quite sorry their detention is over. They liked
tne scenery, the company, the commissariat, and
the long moonlight walks—minus the moon—the
“sausage parties,” and “potato picnics,” and,
above all, they admired the P. & O. Co.’s kindness
in footing the little bill. One young man, whose
vaccination didn’t take, was suspected of privately
rubbing off the lymph, so that he could remain
longer with an angel similarly spotted with
ineffective tattoo-marks. Evening strolls after
the concerts were immensely popular, but as each
strolling couple armed itself before starting with
an extra-warm rug, the promenading theory was
felt to be a hollow pretext. One gallant salt, who
used to sally forth into the cold night air osten-
tatiously swagless, had his “ bluey ” ingeniously
planted in a sheltered nook, so the girl didn’t
catch a chill, while everybody thought the pair
really went out for a “good, brisk walk.” Love
rules—alike.in court, camp, grove, and quaran-
tine.
The pink-clad girl who nightly walks the silver
wire at the Flying Jordans’ performance at the
Theatre Royal, has introduced an entirely new and
original smile, differing completely from the worn,
thin-lipped grin of the usual woman acrobat.
Conventional acknowledgements for applause after
very difficult monkey-tricks on trapeze or tight-
rope are as humbly deprecating as if the performer
would say, “ A mere nothing, ladies and gentle-
men, any of you could do the same,” but Virginia
Aragon, standing at about the level of the gallery
boy’s eyes, flashes into their faces a brilliant and
triumphant dazzle of dark eyes and white teeth
calculated to make every boy of them feel his
heart thumping violently against his ribs in
sudden admiration. On her entry she trails
behind her an enormous white overall of expensive
satin with a whole farm of ostrich-plumes upon
it. Discarding this, her garments are seen to be
a very much abridged edition, and, after her
pocket-handkerchief trick, she disposes of the
cambric morsel in a way which causes a titter by
tucking it up inside her abbreviated pink trunks,
the only thing in the nature of a pocket she has
about her.
There were frocks at the Admiralty House
garden-party last Thursday. Taken all round, it
was the best-gowned show of this season to date.
Commonplace wearing-apparel was weeded out,
and every woman was resplendent in velvet and
fur. A social leader, whose clothes are usually
copied, had on a symphony in dark-green velvet
with an upstanding, much-gilt braided and be-
furred collar ; many matrons wore important-
looking, black velvet mantles; the party from
Govt. House was small and unremarkable in ves-
ture beyond a fairly bright purple frock; some
women shivered in silks. The party huddled
itself together from the biting wind on the lawn
in front of the house, and for amusement listened
to the Orlando band, discussed tea and light
refreshments and each other’s dresses, and watched
Fort Denison turning as many tints in the sunset
as a dying dolphin. Blue coats and brass buttons
were numerous. Many of the Nicest women like
to appear with a uniformed admirer attached ; the
rule seems to be the older the siren the younger
the lieutenant. Juvenile damsels generally avoid
their naval contemporaries and promenade with
elderly captains. A girl who came out at one of
the recent balls was describing her experiences to
a friend next day: “I got on very well,” she
declared, “till somebody introduced a mere boy,
a middy; and when I found I had got down to
middies I came home.”
Presbyterians in session are presided over by the
Moderator, who can choose for himself how he will
be garbed. The new N.S.W. Moderator appears
in the assembly in a Court dress consisting of a
fine black-cloth dress-coat trimmed with braid-
froggings and ruffles of white lace on the breast
and at the wrists, black cassock and knee-breeches,
and shoes with silver buckles; over this, a robe,
bands and D.D. hood of purple and white silk.
Solomon in all his glory !
The first of numerous “afternoons ” arranged to
increase the attractions of the Society of Artists’
picture-show was given last week. Mrs. French
and Mrs. Mackenzie supervised the pro-
gramme, and the upper room in George-street,
where the exhibition is held, was crowded with
military people and others. On Saturday an ar-
tistic afternoon, managed by Miss Ethel Stephens,
was billed. For Tuesday the legal folk took up
the task of entertaining all-comers, under the
direction of Mrs. Cohen. On Saturday evening,
“9th May, the journalists of Sydney intend to cele-
brate the end of the show in a blaze of glory by a
programme coruscating with talent. His Ex.
visited the gallery one day last week, and on his
departure, as he was backing politely out of the
narrow doorway, contrived to carry away one of
the works of art on his coat, by rubbing against
the yellow-ochre man on the passage wall.
A new barmaid arrived at the principal hotel
of a Yic. township, and all the local ladies were
agitated as to whether or not Hebe dyed her hair,
which was very plain to see. One day the wife of
the bank manager next door noticed a wisp of
golden hair blowing about her backyard. It was
the same color as the barmaid’s, and presumably
one of her stray combings. The lady eagerly
pounced upon the wisp and washed it. It was
dyed after all, and at every subsequent gossip
meeting the discovery was announced to thrill-
ingly-interested listeners. Of such large-minded
women is the sassiety of country towns usually
composed.
The stern Romans in “The Sign of the Cross ”
look as natural as if they had wandered out of
Smith’s Classical Dictionary. Such a classical
atmosphere envelopes the play that you feel like
being at school again, and grope in your mind for
the principal parts of “aino,” which is freely con-
jugated on the stage in all its moods and tenses.
The revels are crowned with roses and rapture,
and bald-headed patricians are garlanded in a
giddy manner as well as the dancing girls.
Glabrio, the bald antique, has many bright things
to say against women—a habit which passes for
cynical brilliancy in all ages. His most cutting
jibe, “ I can get a woman to say she’ll love me as
long as I have any money,” is vociferously ap-
plauded by the millionaires in the shilling gallery.
Julius Knight, as Marcus Superbus, has several
very pretty frocks, the most striking being a cos-
tume of pale-grey cloth, studded with gems and
embroidered overmantle with faint-blue border ;
his ball-dress is of sky-blue over white-and-gold
tunic and silk fleshings ; the armor is chiefly white
and gold. ’ He makes a splendid figure, and
entirely eclipses the women in glory. Miss Elliott
Page has a patrician bearing, and one sumptuous
robe. There are multitudes of slaves in fine
raiment and guests in ditto, who form flowery
stage pictures ; and Nero, in his imperial-purple,
is apparently on his last legs, and very tottery.
Gaston Mervale is grim as Tigellinus in a red
toga, with a cart-wheel pattern. The Christians
are all very shabby, and their midnight service
near the Tiber recalls a torchlight Harmy meet-
ing. Linda Raymond, as the giddy Dacia, bundles
that Roman matron’s blue skirt around her legs
and shuffles &la Yum Yum. Mrs. M. Morris is
too modern in style to appear at her best in the
severely classic.
At the official opening of Brisbane Exhibition
(or “Jubbert”) most of the exhibits of import-
ance were still on the sea, and there were large
yawning spaces which were satisfactorily filled by
crowds, mostly consisting of dowds and bald-heads.
The speeches were of the dreariest. The chief
revel of the week was the Governess’s Home Ball,
at which many girls “came out.” On Monday
Bananaland society was “received” by Lady
Lamington.
Portable jokes are the latest importation from
the land of dollars. The new American comedian
at the Royal carries on his light humor in the
form of a pick—among other things—and this
enables him to remark that he’s going out to the
races to “ pick ” a winner. Then he makes jests
with a bell and a spade. Anybody can get jokes
now by buying them at the ironmonger's. The
dialogue sandwiched in between the acrobatic
feats of the Jordans and other spine-twisters can
hardly be said to be funny without being vulgar,
though sometimes it is vulgar without being
funny, and sometimes it is both funny and vulgar.
But it is never neither funny nor vulgar.
A vice-regal tour was recently undertaken to
St, Helen’s (Tas.) by the Gov. and suite. The
whole town —population about 200—turned out to
greet the noble tourists, who were presented with
the usual loyal address, and the whole party was
then driven to the police buildings to see the
grapes grown by the local constable. This sort of
thing is regarded as a high old time in Tasmania.
The Sydney Philharmonic Society has been hard
at work rehearsing for the first concert season,
due this (Thursday) evening. Miss Josephine
Deakin, Messrs. Simonsen, Burns-Walker and W.
T. Colyer will be the principals in Berlioz’s
“Faust.” The chorus has been enlarged, and the
orchestra much strengthened with a view to com-
ing out with a strong roar in the Pandemonium
scene.
The French ball will take place next Monday
night, 17th, in Paddington Town Hall. One of
the features of the party will be a French cotillon
which the performers are now engaged in busily
rehearsing. The Town Hall meeting re the pro-
posed Hospital for Consumptives will be on the
same evening, and His Ex. and party have pro-
mised to be present at both.
Mrs. Gore-Gillon, now petitioning for a separa-
tion from her husband, is a slim, fair woman, with
what dressmakers term an “ elegant ’’figure. She
is usually tailor-made, and much addicted to large,
overshadowing hats ; also she often carries a silver-
headed cane in public. In M.L., Gov. Onslow was
a friend of the doctor’s family, and vice-regal
acquaintanceship elevated Mrs. G.-G. to the ranks
of the Nicest. The book, “Napoleon and the
Fair Sex,” which has been boomed so much during
the case, isn’t such a terribly moral-corrupting
work |as many people hoped' from the shocked
manner in which it was referred to. I have seen
it lying about in most correct and proper houses,
and no domestic peace has been wrecked thereby.
It is a collection of old and rather weary tattle
and gossip about the First Empire—and the
author’s idea of thrill is to print Josephine’s
washing-list in full.
( A man in the Cri. stalls, on the first night of the
“ Private Secretary,” was as good a claque as Yic.
Chief Sec. Peacock. He had a boisterous laugh,
and was evidently interviewing the Rev. Robert
for the first time. Whenever old Cattermole
shook and worried the mild curate, the man in
front howled and screamed ; when the limp and
bewildered sec. was hauled out of the box looking
like a thing the cat would bring into the room on
a wet night, the spectator crowed and yelled with
mirth; and when, for the thousandth time,
Thornton took his flying leap over the settee, the
house heard one last exploding guffaw, and the
rest was silence. The natural inference was that
the enthusiast had burst.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“After the sassiety papers had announced the
dates of Guvment House balls for May and June
a small mob of rather nice ones, who had shown a
Spartan dilatoriness in calling, rolled up hastily
to inscribe their names in The Holy Book. Their
horror was intense when they found the visiting
volume removed from its accustomed place in the
cold mortuary of a vestibule, and a silence there
as of the dead. No explanations were forthcom-
ing, All the callers could do was to leave their
‘ Yery-sorry-came-too-late ’ cards on the V.R.
door-mat, and turn to praying to a Higher Power
for a card.
“Sentiment is not quite extinct in the silver-
coated heart of the Australian husband, to judge
by recent demonstrations attending the arrival of
a certain lady, who, after a trip ’Ome ‘ for
repairs,’had been detained a week in quarantine
on the Himalaya. The devoted spouse (by pro-
fession a financier) had two magnificent floral
designs perpetrated, at a Collins-street florist’s.
One design represented a ship, in Neapolitan
violets (favorites of the loved one), with masts and
rigging complete, from one of which fluttered rib-
bons with such tender turtle-dove devices as
‘Welcome to my darling wife.’ The second
design, on the lines of a lyre, was wreathed in
orchids and fern, with a nest at the base, in which
four little birds (representing their progeny
numerically) chirped forth a welcome to mammie.
Furthermore, their home-roost at Brighton had
been fresh painted and upholstered, a boudoir re-
ceiving special attention, freshly decorated with
pale or sea-green fittings, as a never-to-be-for-
gotten symbol of that sea-trip.
“ There are whispers of a coming flare-up in
Ji.C. circles on the question of church music.
Archbishop Carr, through the instrumentality of
his right-hand councillor, Dean McKenna, has in-
troduced the Gregorian Chant at St. Patrick’s
Cathedral, to the dire dissatisfaction of the common
or garden kind of worshipper. All the goody-
goody priests who seek episcopal favor have fol-
lowed the potentate’s lead, made a clean sweep of
the expensive soloists in the choir, and fallen back
on the reserved stock of ‘ Catholic young men and
children of Mary.’ Other pastors of a more inde-
pendent order stick to the florid operatic masses
of Mozart and Gounod, and await the conse-
quences.
“ What an irreligious institution is the church
choir ! A well-known bass singer was recently
asked to contribute a solo at St. .Tames’, Little
Collins-street, Melbourne. He promised and was
advertised. He turned up to time, and was
knocked out of all conceit to find eleven people in
the body of the tabernacle, and in the gallery
about 30 dead-beats (who muster strong every
Sunday on the strength of a free lunch after-
wards). The singer refused point-blank to con-
tribute his solo, even ‘ for the honor and glory of
the Lord,’ and so the advt. represented a dead
loss to Christianity.
“ Melbourne is already in a state of Jubilee
measles, shop after shop beginning to break out in
electric rashes and transparencies, beseeching
Providence to be tender in its dealings with Her
Gracious. In Swanston-street a shop for some
time past has sparkled with a gigantic V.R. every
night. Last Saturday evening a neighboring
establishment hung forth on a long pole a beer-
bottle with the bottom cut out of it. A candle in-
side the bottle supplied the Jubilee illumination.
“ The Diamond Jubilee shivoo attracts all sorts
and conditions of people from Melbourne to Eng-
land’s bloated metropolis. One of the latest ex-
portations is Miss Sutherland—a good friend to
Melb. street boys and fallen lydies—who has gone
for a four months’ trip to repair her health, see
the procession, and study the beneficent influence
of Queen Victoria’s reign upon the submerged
tenth.
“An insurrection among the nurses at Melb.
Hospital, the other week, was averted by a prompt
disappearing of the grievance. The grievance was
a ‘ sister,’ of only 20 months’experience, whom the
matron had procured from Adelaide, without
authority, to act as superior officer to the local
talent. The local talent, being in most cases
better qualified than the ‘ sister,’ and the introduc-
tion of a foreign ‘ sister ’ being contrary to the rules
anyhow, 36 nurses marched to the matron’s room
in 36 caps and pinafores, only to find that the
secretary was there before them, with ‘ sister’s ’
resignation in his strong right hand. Whereupon,
the deputation withdrew, wreathed in smiles, yet
not quite happy. It had missed the excite-
ment of tearing off its 36 caps and pinnies, and
going forth on strike. And when a nurse has a
best boy ready to take her out for the day, she
wants a strike badly.

“Lady-collectors for the Queen’s Shilling Fund
have not too rosy a time. The rule is that every
house, large or small, must be visited. Sometimes
the inmates are so palpably poverty-stricken that
it seems a shame to ask them. They think so
themselves, and say it, too, with a good deal of
strong language thrown in. Of course, one is
delayed by time-wasters of all sorts. Shrill little
women, in default of a donation, scold humanity
in general for misplaced charity, and lay bare the
peccadilloes of neighbors, no better than they
should be, said to fatten persistently on the funds
of the local Benevolent Association. Then there
is the fatherly man, contributing, along with his
shilling, pathetic anecdotes of suffering ‘ virgins ’
(as he calls them every two seconds) subjected to
the professional scrutiny of irreverent male meds.
“Collectors, comparing notes, condemn tiny
houses and huge ones as equally unprofitable.
Bet wi xt-and-between villas are havens where
tired askers.scoop in shillings, sweetened by civility
and invitations to rest and afternoon tea Man-
sion doors are barred by ‘ uppish ’ maids, who an-
nounce haughtily that ‘ the lady has given else-
where ! Probably the philanthropist referred to
prefers doing good on the housetops. After all a
shilling should bring in a shilling’s-worth, though
the latter may mean nothing more substantial
than an impressive thank you ’ from Lady This
or gushing post-card from Mrs. T’other.

Last week’s rainfall in Victoria was sent
bv the Lord as a special favor to James Balfour
late of the Freehold Investment Co. St. James
in question bosses the Council of the Churches
and it was the C. of C. that persuaded the Govern-
ment to fix a Sunday for prayer and humiliation
Now, when the prayers reached Heaven the Lord
enquired : “ Who is connected with this move-
ment ? ” and Angel Gabriel said, “ Well, Balfour
is in it.” Consequently some rain was sent down
as requested.
At a Koroit (Vic.) tabernacle, last Sunday week
the vicar found himself very awkwardly placed!
He had to offer up prayer for rain, notwithstand-
ing that Koroit was already as wet as it wanted
to be. So the good man, after praying for rain in
other parts of the province, offered up thanks for
the local deluge, by way of reminding the Almighty
that Koroit worshippers didn’t wish to get caught
in a shower whilst returning home from church.
Which recalls to mind a very old Bulletin story
of the two evangelists who were holding a drought
service one Sunday on Sydney Domain. “0
Lord, send down thy plenteous rain upon our
parched land,” snuffled the tall, thin preacher.
Whereupon his short, fat brother, clasping his
hands and gazing up to Heaven with a conciliatory
smirk, added, “ But not, O Lord, until our little
meeting is over.”
F. R. Godfrey, chairman of Melb. Hospital
Committee, signalises the completion of the term
of Drs. Alfreda Hilda Gamble and Janet Greig as
medical officers to the institution with an outburst
of eloquence, in which he falls foul of the “ medi-
cal gentlemen ” attached to the hospital for not
treating the ladies with that fine old-fashioned
courtesy due to them as “sich.” Mr. Godfrey
suddenly discovers that the age of chivalry is past
because the male doctors refused to relieve the
female doctors of all the unpleasant duties, leaving
them to wrestle with barking “ drunks,” and
persons afflicted with blue devils, and various
“horrid cases” too numerous and too delicate to
mention. When their attention was drawn to the
situation, the male M.D.’s remarked coarsely that
the doctresses would have to take the thick with the
thin. This, of course, is not conduct becoming an
officer and a gentleman in his attitude towards a
defenceless maiden in distress. But, when we re-
member that the woman is a fighting rival, and
that she is rushing the he-sawbones for place and
preferment, for his shekels, and beef and bread,
for his dogcart, and his tall horse, and his pretty
villa, it is only reasonable to expect the latter to
regard the former merely as a man and a brother,
who has casually mislaid his breeches, and has
undertaken to compete in petticoats, but cannot
expect to be allowed under-grip and the privilege
of hitting and kicking below the belt on that
account. If the age of chivalry had not passed
long before it existed, it would hardly have sur-
vived the fair damsel armed with the stomach-
pump.

that a man now under sentence of death for

Queen Rosey, of Illawarra (N.S.W.), has
presented her ebony husband, King Mickey, with
a daughter—the 10th child born unto the pair, 14
of the number being boys.

Some people say that the London Lady’s
Letter to S.M. Herald, allegedly contributed by
the wife of Rev. Haweis, is, in reality, written by
the musical little bore himself. The sex of a par-
son is often doubtful.
If the Turner Government wishes to do a
good stroke of policy it will appoint Wrixon to the
Supreme Court when a vacancy arises. He is the
best available man and his appointment would be
acceptable to all parties.
Thomas Garton died suddenly on the floor
of his study in London, a mail or so ago, at the
age of 75. 'He was the first man to bring news to
England of the discovery of gold in Australia. He
had gathered a stack of wealth in Australia, and
spent the balance of his life in philanthropy.
S.A. Agent-General Playford, who aban-
doned radishes for diplomacy, is credited with
being “one of the best-dressed men in London.”
“ Honest Tom ” has, however, refused bebauble-
ment so far, despite the advice of his old friend
Kingston. And now the problem is, what about
both of them in June ?
Queen Victoria is to be fitted with works
to enable her adequately to do the Gracious
Sovereign business in the record reign procession.
Her carriage will be provided with “ a sort of
rocking-chair, which will enable her automatically
to appear to return the salutes of her subjects.
The monarchical sham !
“Princess T. Kroubetskoi, of Russia,”
arrived in Australia per Polynesien. Some people
misread the lady’s name for Troubetskoi, thinking
that Australia was favored by a visit from Amelie
Rives, the authoress of “The Quick and the
Dead,” who lately married a Russian prince.
But the newcomer turns out to be a mere princess
and nothing more.
Rumors that Vic. Justice Williams—now
laid up by a severe accident —will not return to
the Bench may be contradicted on family
authority. The Judge has had little heart in his
judicial work since Madden was pitchforked into
the Chief Justiceship over his head, but he does
not want to be out of harness altogether. Law-
yers regard Williams as one of the soundest of
Judges, and would be sorry if he did retire.
Writes “Bures” re Oscar Wilde and Wain-
wright. In his “Dorian Gray,” Oscar Wilde
says :
“ Harry,” said Basil Hallward, looking him straight in
the face, “ every portrait that is painted with feeling is a
portrait of the artist, not of the sitter. The sitter is
merely the accident, the occasion. It is not he who is
revealed by the painter ; it is rather the painter who, on
the colored canvas, reveals himself. The reason I will
not exhibit this picture is that I am afraid that I have
shown in it the secret of my own soul.’’
The appeal of John Higginson, of Noumea,
in a complicated company case involving the in-
terests of his old partner, J. C. Morgan, was dis-
missed by N.S.W. Full Court last week. J. C.
Morgan was Higginson’s right hand, away off in
the beginning of their joint career. Together
they fought the established moneyed power of
New Caledonia, beating both it and the Credit
Foncier forces called in to assist it. J. C. Morgan
died some years age, and the present litigation is
with his brother, W. E. Morgan.
“ We humbly beg Your Majesty’s gracious
acceptance of our loyal and hearty congratulations
upon the completion of the sixtieth year of Your
Majesty’s unprecedentedly long and incomparably
beneficent sovereignty.” (Extract from the illumi-
nated address of Fitz Gibbon and the Smellbourne
Board of Works). “ Loyalty,” it may be observed,
is composed of adjectives and adverbs.
Capt. Burrows, governor of Melb. gaol,
was one day in the prison exercise-yard, talking
with a man under committal for murderously
assaulting another. He spoke to the fellow about
the cruelty of the crime, and said: “Supposing,
for instance, that one of the men yonder were to
assault me in the same way?” “Bless yer, sir,”
said the man, “ they wouldn’t do it. Why, in this
yere gaol yer bear the best o’ characters.”

Premier Kingston wired triumphantly that
he hadn’t been ill at all on the voyage from Adelaide
to Albany. Apropos : when Kingston was travel-
ling awhile ago from Albany a certain
“ loud ” passenger was bragging that he
had never been seasick. The man be-
came so arrogant that at last Kingston
and one or two kindred spirits bribed
a steward to doctor the boastful one’s
soup, with the result
that he succumbed to
what he fervently be-
lieved was mal-de-
mcr, and didn’t re-
appear till next morn-
ing. Thenceforward
all was peace.
