Treas. Holder (S.A.), who in Kingston’s
absence acts as Premier, will be 47 on Wednesday,
May 12. He first entered Parliament in ’B7, and
during’ the subsequent 10 years has served nearly
six years as a Minister. He has been in turns a
school-master, a country newspaper editor, and a
general agent.
Green Box : “ Who would have thought of
demised Gov. William Robinson going before his
brother Hercules ? He was an undervalued man
by the public, though not by Downing-street,
which found him a success in seven or eight pretty
difficult governorships. He might be likened to
Leopold Lewis, who lost a grand legal practice
through his brilliant success in adapting the play
of ‘ The Bells ’ for Irving, and died a pensioner on
the tragedian. Thus Robinson discounted him-
self as a Governor by dabbling in music. Hercules
cowed him somewhat unduly. It is possible his
ability was equal to that of his rumbustious big
brother, and one would have little hesitation in
saying it equalled Bowen’s, but tonguey qualities
go for so much. Born for bachelorhood, Robinson
failed to attain it. Wherefore his malaise.”

The general opinion in S.A. is that, apart
from Premier Reid, Kingston would prove more
than a match for any man in the Convention.
Reid’s calmness and the humor of his interjections,
it is felt, would drive the fiery Charlie into mad-
ness if they were leaders of opposing political
parties in the same House.
Melb. Argus chuckles over the fact that
labor-member Trenwith was about the most care-
fully and expensively apparelled person at Ade-
laide Convention. Quite natural: the clothes
with which Trenwith did “credit to the colony ”
whilst he was figuring as a Victorian politician
in London have not yet lost their freshness.

My good man, the Prince of Wales is on sale very cheaj
to-day ; any ruffian who possesses a fairly round sum may
buy his dismal acquaintance at a comparatively moderate
price. Why, even Hirsch contrived to purchase, a bosom
friendship, when no foreigner of decent birth would a®-

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Those who sympathise with the afflicted will
rejoice with Station-master I. Ludwick, of Talbot
railway station, Vic., whose recovery from lum-
bago by the use of .Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure has
created such extensive and favorable comment.
Me explains in a letter dated Feb. 12, 1897 : “For
many years I had very bad attacks of lumbago,
suffering terrible agony, each lasting three or four
days, rendering me unable to do any work. I
tried every known internal and external remedv,
but received no benefit until, about six years ago,
1 tried Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure, and lam pleased to
say tour bottles cured me, and I have never been
troubled with this painful disease since. I have
also recommended this remedy to others, and I
am pleased to say, with good results.”*
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific for Liveb
and Kidney Disease.
William Fleming,

POLITICAL
One member of We.stralia’s Leg. Council
was returned to the “ Old Men’s Home ” with 13
votes.
The Westralian spurification of Rolls Act
worked sad havoc in the t’othersider votes at
Westralian elections.
The return of the two Labor men (Guthrie
and Hutchinson) for Adelaide district of S.A.
Leg. Council now looks certain.
The President of Yic. Leg. Council states
that Sargood is “necessary to the advancement of
the colony.” “ The colony ” must be a crab.
Moans Zeal, M.L.C., Vic: “A director’s
life under the new Companies Act is the life of a
galley slave.” Humph ! and the lives ci many
under the old act ought to have been.
A Westralian election question : Is the
candidate in favor of granting woman’s suffrage ?
Heavy voice from the back, “ Yes, give ’em any-
thing to come over from t’otherside.”
Not a single official of either House of the
A. legislature wears a uniform, the only robe
, office donned being a gown. There is no usher
of the-black, or any other, rod, and the assistant-
clerk.m either House has the title and performs
the duties, when necessary, of Sergeant-at-Arms.
The idea of bundling hundreds, thousands,
if iposjdble, of Sydney’s hungry and ragged unem-
ployed on to the space in front of Redfern railway-
etation, to yell Premier Reid a malediction on his
departure for ’Ome, ranks among the most trucu-
lent party moves of the generation. And the idea
hqd abase.
A GREAT DISPLAY.
“ Veil, veil! Cohen must he mad to put up that big dransparency for the
Diamond Jubilee: vat exdravagance, to be sure!”
W IFE: “ Veil, I dinks you should make some disblay.”

Premier Kingston’s departure for England
leaves his Ministry without a lawyer in its ranks.
Holder will have to act as grand jury, and dodge
legal problems in Parliament until his chief returns.
Frank Harris, the Saturday Review
editor, who is excellently informed on South
Africa, avers that English war on the Boers
would hoop up and cooper all South Africa into a
Republic.
Political appointments are still possible
in Victoria, despite Public Service and Rail-
way Acts passed to prevent them. One
potentate in particular, it is averred, works
his opportunities for all they are worth.
Within a short space he has smuggled in
five youths—two were his own sons, and the
rest sons of intimate friends.
“We may presume,” writes Rev. Dr.
Geikie to Sydney D.T., “ that the members of
the Federal Convention (who refused to em-
body anything about God in the Federal Con-
stitution) are neither agnostics nor atheists.”
Well, the atheist-affirmative is a very rare
bird ; but, as regards agnostics, since the ma-
jority of intelligent men are nowadays agnostic,
in esse, if not in nomine, it is only reasonable
to suppose that a majority of the Convention-
members are of opinions which —while prevent-
ing them, on the one hand, from asserting
God —prevent them equally, on the other, from
taking what Dr. Geikie calls the manlier
course of denying Him altogether. There is
no special manliness in being, like his rever-
ence, cocksure about the unknowable.
Brunker’s appointment as N.S.W. Acting-
Premier is the necessary consequence of the order
in which the members of the Reid Ministry were
sworn in and gazetted. In the event of death or
disability of the Prime Minister, the members of a
Ministry succeed to the duties of Premier in their
originally gazetted order. In the U.S. constitu-
tion this principle is expressly laid down.
Another half-hundred of the “Empress’s ’
subjects—“ the most miserable objects yet intro-
duced ” —are reported as en route from Sydney to
“New India” at Tucki, N.S.W. Their prede-
cessors on the sugar fields, however, are fast
putting on flesh and impudence, and the Richmond
Herald sends forth a wail over a prospect of soon
having in the district more colored man than plain.
At Croydon, recently, John Hoolan con-
fessed that he had seen the error of his Ways and
lived to repent. He had hunted away capital, and
instanced the present state of the Etheridge as
caused by this hunting of capital. He wanted in
future to do all he could to attract capital, and
hoped some day to be a capitalist. . . . And this
is the fire-eater of yesterday. Beware of the Jerry
Simpsons ! Whenever they brag about never
wearing socks they’re on the high road to a plug-
hat.

gregor hires out his

Maurice O’Rorke
is the only survivor of
the Maoriland House
of Representatives of
1861 who sits in the
House of 1897. Last
year there were two
survivors, but the
other one, the patri-
archal Saunders,
dropped out at the
’96 election, and
O’Rorke is now a
lonesome Methuselah
all “on his own.”
South Australia
is the only Australian
province whose post
and telegraph depart-
ment shows a sub-
stantial profit. Also,
it is the only province
where an expert per-
manent official runs
the department in-
stead of it being en-
trusted to the fool of
the Ministry who
has generally to make
way for the fool of
the next Ministry
before he has begun
to learn anything.
There maybe no con-
nection between these
circumstances, but it
certainly looks as if
there was.
Copeland’s pro-
posal to have N.S.W.
Parliament meet at 3
p.m. and rise at 11
p.m., “whoever was
speaking,” is the old
eight-hour-system of
the workshops. When
the clock strikes,
“down tools.”
N.S.W. Single-Tax latest manifesto de-
clares for the abolition of income-tax, abolition of
exemptions under land-tax, and increase of that
tax to 3d. in the £,. What chance has such a pro-
gramme ! And where, now, has gone the vaunted
tax on stimulants and narcotics ? Has the League
thrown the Wise-party over, or the Wise-party
the League ?
Premier Reid, at his farewell banquet in
Sydney Town Hall, enthused much about the
Australians being “one people.” Yet at the
Federal Convention he fought to the bitter end to
keep the railways under six hostile provincial
managements, and to keep the debts separate, and
generally to make Australia as far as possible six
peoples instead of one. If half the after-dinner
Federal enthusiasm were only available when it is
really wanted !
DIPLOMATIC WEAKNESS, ,
SYMPATHISER: “ You are not looking too well."
SICK ONE: “ Oh, I don’t want to. If I did, I’d be expected to do some housework!"
Tt is reported now that Nelson, as soon as
he has secured whatever title happens to be going,
will cable his resignation of Queensland Premier-
ship. Then Horace Tozer, as Acting-Premier,
will seize the billet before Byrnes can get back.
But the air is full of rumors about the Happy
Family at Brisbane, and the only point on which
they resemble each other is that each rumor
represents somebody as surreptitiously throwing
someone else overboard.
While Colonial Secretary Brunker has been
made nominal acting-Premier of N.S.W., the real
work is to fall on Minister Carruthers, who will
engineer the Federal Bill through Parliament. A
very clear admission of the very patent and well-
known fact that Brunker knows nothing about
Federation, and a direct smack in the face for the
voters who supported him in his voiceless candi-
dature for a great National Convention, where he
figured merely as a smug dummy with a vote.
Old John M‘lntyre, leader of Vie. Opposi-
tion, enthusiastically assured Premier Turner that
he would not countenance any attack on the Govt,
in Turner’s absence. It transpires, however, that
John is himself going England wards to look for
some favorable opportunity for bumping his old
white head in the dust before the throne, and his
substitute isn’t bound by John’s promise, so if
the Tories see a chance to Upset the Government
while Turner is away it will be upset promptly.
There are grumblings out West (N.S.W.)
at the free exercise of the power conferred by the
’95 Land Act to attach resumed areas to lease-
holds ; and indignation meetings are being held.
The complaint is made more especially as to river-
frontage holdings. The Act certainly provides
that any resumed areas so attached may be again
resumed, and so thrown open for settlement upon
six months’notice being given by the Governor,
which, in practice, means the Minister for Lands ;
but confidence in Lands Ministers’ friendship for
selectors is not unlimited out back.
The present Dutch succession-difficulty in-
volves a choice chapter of “ Royal ” history. Of
the late King’s two sons by his first wife, one went
mad, the other raked himself to death ; and, when
the father in turn had debauched himself into a
miserable wreck, the present Duchess of Albany
was chosen in hot haste to provide the crown, if
possible, with an heir at the last moment. But an
elder sister became the victim ; result, daughter—
the present girl-queen, upon whose marriage and
possible issue depends the preservation of the
rotten House of Orange from extinction.
The Vic. Ministry lost an honorary mem-
ber last week, but the business of the country
wasn’t interrupted by the melancholy event. In
fact, everybody had forgotten that David Coutts,
M.L.C., had been anyway connected with the
Turner Government until the matter cropped
up casually in his deat i notices. Coutts was a
grazier in a way, and he died of dropsy, resulting
from the drought. Alas ! even Property has its
drawbacks. Thousands of older and unhealthier
men than the late M.L.C. have lived through this
drought-time without feeling a penny the worse
for it.
Jawbone Neild, M.L.A., in the old age
pension scheme debate: “I do not see why We
should give the person who has an income of .£5O
a year a pension of 10s. per week and settle the
same sum on those who have nothing at all.”
Perhaps the best reason is that every citizen will
contribute to the pension-fund through the general
taxation, therefore every citizen has the same
right to benefit by the fund if he wants to. It
isn’t a question of “giving” the money, in any
case ; it is a matter of the whole community in-
suring its life and collecting the insurance when it
comes due. Neild in the attitude of a national
benefactor talking of himself as the “ We ” who
“ give the money ” is grotesque.
Writes “T.” to The Bulletin :
The Victorian Minister of Mines ‘is carrying* out his
track-cutting policy under a system very much like
sweating. A 30-mile track at Buchan has just been com-
pleted for 9d. a chain ; and another in East Gippsland has
recently been let at the same price. This latter track is
28 miles long, across mountainous and otherwise tough
country, and is to be cleared Bft. wide. For a distance of
sa Pl in K s are so thick that a horseman can
with difficulty ride through them. The track also includes
four miles of cutting, to be cut sft. wide, and much of
that is rock. When the job is completed each man will
have earned about 10s. per week These contracts are
tendered for by one of a party of mates, who share alike
in the profits. The reason why they are taken so cheaply
is the same as with the sweated factory employes thev
must obtain a living somehow. It would surely be judi-
cious for the track inspector—the one who surveyed this
has bossed Government jobs for years—to put on the
work an estimate, below which tenders should not be
accepted. Mr. Foster, having such evidence of Victorian
prosperity before him, must be joking when he says he
can t understand why so many Vies, migrate to other
lands.
“Can we never extract this tapeworm ol
Europe from the brain of our countrymen?”—
Emerson.
Writes “ A Voter : ”
Dear Bulletin,— How to make elections a pront instead
of a loss to the commonwealth. Every district to keep a
book with voters’ signatures recorded. At election-time
each voter to post his vote, securely sealed, enclosing a
separate slip containing- his signature for comparision
The district signature-book to be sent to the Federal City"
prior to the election, and the signatures to be compared
by experts. The votes to be opened and counted by the
usual officers appointed for that purpose. The advantages
of this system would be : A large increase of revenue to
the post office ; saving of time and money to the voters
who now mostly lose one day visiting the polling booth l
saving the country a large amount of the usual election
expenses. Fraud would be almost impossible under this
system. If cheques are honored by the banks, why should
not written votes be honored by returning officers? Sig-
natures to be renewed every year.
Sydney Telegraph leading article : “ One
of the counts in Mr. Lyne’s indictment against
the (N.S.W.) Government is that the roads and
bridges vote has been starved in order to make the
public balance-sheet presentable. This is a stock
charge.” It is. For instance, Dibbs, in his last
year of office, spent .£450,000 on roads and bridges.
And Reid, when he came into power, not only
accused him of “ starving ” the road vote, but in
1895 he charged £332,000 of his own road-and-

Presented to the Annual Meeting of the Society, held on Wednesday, 28th day of

INCOME.
The annual income arising from policies issued during the year amounts to
£26,471 19s. 3d.
CLAIMS.
Claims have matured by death under 337 policies, on 306 lives, amounting to
£112,222 Is. 10d., including bonuses.
A sum of £37,677 ss. 6d. has been paid for endowments matured under 181 policies.
One annuity of £2l ss. 6d. has ceased to become payable owing to the death of the
annuitant.
ACCOUNTS. .
The addition to the funds during the year was £117,136 13s. 6d., bringing the total
funds up to £2,040,972 7s. 2d.
REDUCTION IN EXPENSES.
The ratio of expenses to premium revenue has again been diminished notwithstanding
the increased volume of new business.
INTEREST.
The average rate of interest yielded throughout the year by the funds (after deduction
of the amount in the Investment Fluctuation Fund) had exceeded 4J per cent.
THE GENERAL MANAGER.
It is with sincere regret that they have to report the death, on 26th June last, of Mr.
Thomas Jaques Martin, the general manager and managing director of the society since its
establishment. They acknowledge that it has been largely owing to his great organising
powers and thorough grasp of the business that the society possesses its present extensive
organisation and large volume of transactions.
The General Committee of Management, in whose hands lay the appointment of a
successor, unanimously elected the Secretary, Mr. Leslie John Park, to the chief executive
position of General Manager of the Sydney office.
The General Committee of Management also appointed the Assistant Secretary, Mr.
A. F. Crombie, to be Assistant General Manager and Secretary to the Principal Office Board
of Directors.
DIRECTORS.
In accordance wdth the articles, it will be necessary for the meeting to elect two direc-
tors to fill the places of the retiring directors, viz., the Hon. Agar Wynne, M.L.C., and
Mr. George Withers. These gentlemen being eligible offer themselves for re-election.
Mr. Phillip Blashki, J.P., and Mr. Alfred Webster have also given notice of candidature.
AUDITORS.
Mr. Louis Charles Wilkinson and Mr. B. D. Smith retire at this meeting, and offer
themselves for re-election.
HENRY HENTY, Chairman.
Melbourne, 15th April, 1897. LESLIE J. PARK, General Manager.

We have audited the balance-sheet and accounts of the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society, Limited, to the 31st December, 1896,. together with the books and vouchers, of
which we have made a monthly examination in terms of the articles of association, and
have found the same correct ; and we consider that the balance-sheet is a full and
fair balance-sheet, containing the particulars required by the regulations of “ The Com-
panies Statute, 1864 ” (“ The Companies Act, 1890”) and is properly drawn up so as to
exhibit a true and correct view of the affairs of the society.

A Newtown Sensation.
SUFFERED FOR NINETEEN YEARS.
A SUPPOSED INCURABLE CURED IN
TWO MONTHS.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS AGAIN
VICTORIOUS AFTER HOSPITALS
PRONOUNCED A LADY INCURABLE
—THE AWFUL EFFECTS OF RHEU-
MATIC FEVER—A HUNDRED AND
FIFTY PEOPLE WATCHING HER—
A BRIGHT AND CHERRY PERSON-
ALITY—WHAT SHE SAYS ABOUT
THE PINK PILLS.
From the Australian Star.
The truest of axioms remarks that there is no
smoke without fire, and whenever the public talk
in warm and appreciative terms of the benefit
they have derived from the use of some specific,
there is always certain to be clear and honest
evidence behind to prove these words.
Hearing in especial of a local cure which had,
amongst numberless others, been effected by the
use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, a
reporter was despatched to the residence of the
patient in question to personally investigate the
case.
Mrs. Evans is the lady’s name, and her house is
situated at No. 105 Mary-street, Newtown, neat
St. Peters.
When we entered we were met by a bright and
cheerful woman, past middle age, but still wear-
ing on her face the signs of recent illness and
departed pain.
We explained our mission.
“Oh,” she said, “ sit down by the window there.
I’ll tell yon all you want to know—and more
besides ” —she added, cheerily, “ about the mar-
vellous change that has taken place in my con-
dition entirely since I began taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills.”
“ You had previously been an invalid, had you
not ? ” we asked.
“Yes,” she replied. “lam a widow, and the
mother of twelve children, and for nineteen years
I have suffered intensely from the after effects of
rheumatic fever.”
“ And now ? ”
“ Ah ! ” she said, shaking her head and smiling,
“ I am much better now.”
“ How did you first hear of the Pills, then ? ”
“ Well, a man left a pamphlet at my door issued
by the Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills Company, and I
read it, and was struck by a case in it similar to
my own in which a cure had been effected.”
“ Did you get a box ? ”
“ Yes, I got a box, and just took the Pills about
the end of November or the beginning of Decem-
ber. ”
“ And the improvement ? ”
“It dated gradually from that date, and as I
took each box I could feel myself perceptibly
growing better, until to-day I’m a changed woman
to what I was two months ago.”
“ Were you very bad, then ? ”
“ Yes. Before I began taking Dr. Willliams’
Pink Pills I couldn’t move my shoulder, neck,
arms, or hands. I couldn’t think of using the
sewing machine. Now I turn the wheel quite
easily with my hand ; but before that I couldn’t
lift it from my side. Look what I’ve done with
the machine, thanks to the Pills.” And she
showed us a number of ladies’ articles profusely
ornamented with fancy stitching and frills of deft
embroidery. Each stich was white and regular,
and each curve cut with the nicest attention to
detail.
“You know I earn my living by this,” she said,
looking up, “ and I’d be thankful for more cus-
tomers. But before I took Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I could no more think of using the machine
than I could of flying.”
“ Did you try much medicine before you bought
the Pills ? ”
“Yes, continually for 19 years; and I could
swim,” she added, reminiscently, “in all the lina-
ments and stuff I’ve taken. Bottle after bottle-
one thing followed another —and there seemed to
be no end to it. ”
“Didn’t you try the hospitals?”
“Yes ; I tried them all. But after I’d been at
each place a while they all declared me incurable.
Why, I walked into the Sydney Infirmary in
Macquarie-street, but it did me no good, and I
had to be carried out.”
“ And you have had private doctors, I suppose?”
“ Yes, I have. Plenty of them—and too many,”
she remarked, with a laugh. “ But it was no
good. After they’d treated me a bit they all told
me the same : ‘ It’s no use,’ they said. ‘You’ll
never get better. We can’t do any more for you.’
So I wasted my money year after year. One doc-
tor recommended me to go into the hospital, and
have my sinews cut. But I wouldn’t agree to
that.”
“ Then you ascribe the improvement in your
condition absolutely to the use of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills?”
“ Yes, absolutely,” she replied. “ I have taken
seven boxes by now, and I’m using my eighth.
And I intend to keep on using them. They’re
the only thing I’ve ever had that have done me
any good, and I can’t to be too thankful to them.
I believe absolutely in them.”
“ Why, look,” she added, quickly, as she raised
her arms, and pinned her collar with the greatest
ease. “I could not raise my hands from my
waist before I took the Pills, and was thoroughly
useless. Now I can earn my own living. And
the awful pains from my neck and chest and
shoulders have all gone away. I never feel them
a bit now. Though in the old days,” and she gave
a little shiver at the recollection, “if I made the
slightest move sharp pains would dart all through
my neck and shoulders.
“ Your case is exceedingly interesting.”
“Do you know that there are about 150 people
who have heard of me and are watching my case?
I am a great object of interest to the neighborhood,
and as I get well others who’re also suffering are
taking the Pills, and I recommend them strongly
to all my friends. I bless the day,” she added
conclusively, “ when I first bought a box of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. Many people know of me,”
she added, “ through my late husband, who was
for several years a leading officer in the Sons and
Daughters of Temperance.”
Then the reporter read the interview over to her,
and she signified its correctness and signed her
name, “ Honor Evans,” below. “ Honor by
name” she said—“ And Honor by nature,” we
added, looking into her earnest face as we bade
her good-bye.
As we left we felt that here, under our personal
observation, was a case, similar to many, in which
the efficacy and marvellous effects of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills had been honestly and exhaustively
demonstrated. *

Military music is, seemingly, not up to much in Vic-
toria. Of 11 brass bands competing for prizes at the
Druids’ Carnival the two lowest scorers (they were judged
by points) were the bands of the Vic. Permanent Artil-
lery, and 2nd Battalion Infantry. The three boss bands
came from Sydney—the Railway Military, Newtown
Brass, and Glebe Borough.
Miss Eva Mylott, the most promising Sydney contralto,
resembles Myra Kemble strikingly, especially when ani-
mated. Miss Mylott’s voice was trained and developed by
Signor Riccardi, Sydney’s retired Mephistopheles.
At Melb. Athenseum Hall a Mrs. C. T. Rickarby has
started to deliver a course of four Friday evening lectures
on the Queens of England, with limelight illustrations.
Queen Victoria is mercifully omitted from the list of sub-
jects. The lecturess restricts herself to the 30 queens
(mostly by marriage only) who wore a crown between a.d.
1067 and 1837, and didn’t live to have a diamond jubilee.
They were mostly uninteresting persons apart from their
exalted billets, but their little histories offer a few oppor-
tunities for eloquent grovel, and Mrs. Rickarby, in record-
ing their alleged virtues, fills a long-felt want by distract-
ing public attention, for an hour or two, from the
unprecedented goodness of Albert Edward’s mother. Re-
marks about any queen who isn’t our Gracious Sovereign
Lady ought to be thankfully received just now.
Haresfoot: “It needed Mrs. Potter’s Francillon to con-
vince me she is an actress. Charming 1' Yet I wager the
business is the invention of a Parisienne, like many other
things that la belle Cora does in plays adapted from the
French.”
Dan. Barry’s facetious advertising devices in the back-
blocks of this fine young country have earned the dis-
pleasure of British M.P. Hogan. Speaking at the Play-
goers’ Club, a few Sundays ago, Hogan informed the
company that in the course of his Australian experience
he had regretted to find three blood-and-thunder dramas
billed as the work of Spurgeon, Parnell, and W. E. Glad-
stone respectively. Whereat the company, no doubt, did
beat their white shirt-fronts, and beseech Heaven to
blight the theatrical manager who had taken those famous
names in vain. And yet our old friend Daniel remains
capable of even greater atrocities. In his time he has
attributed some of his awfullest plays to W. G. Grace,
Queen Victoria, the Archbishop of Canterbury, and L. L.
Smith.
Flautist Lemmone gave his first public performance in
Australia (i.e., since his return) at Point Nepean quaran-
tine station. Lemmone had the bad luck to arrive per
s.s. Himalaya, and was doomed to vaccination and isola-
tion along with the rest of the passengers.
Poor Nellie Farren is selling her jewels, either from
necessity or because she has wearied of this world’s pomps
and vanities. Anyhow, her “ necklace, formed of 45 fine
graduated brilliant collets, and pendant en suite,” and a
host of other glittering souvenirs, were about to be auc-
tioned when last mail left.
N. : “ Observe the subtle oourtesanic fashion of doing
up the hair by the Panther and her cohort, in ‘ln Sight
of St. Paul’s. ’ ”
S.S. : “Have just seen ‘The Lady of Lyons’ for the
first time, and find myself familiar with much of it
through the medium of modern farce and burlesque.
‘The Lady of Lyons,’ more than any other classic, lends
itself to broad travesty. How stilted it is, how artificial;
one comes from the theatre with a guilty feeling, as if,
after reaching years of discretion, one had been caught
reading ‘ Pamela, or Virtue Rewarded.’ Bellew, a most
wary actor, as Melnotte, shivers on the brink of the
ridiculous. Intuitively arises the odious comparison:
‘ Good, but not as funny as Whitburn.’ ”
“The Firm” did not break Adelaide records with
“ Matsa.” It is hard to run a show in Adelaide just now,
as nothing is to be gained from the present vice-regal set,
and the “bong tong” follow the Buxtonian example of
staying away.
Mrs. Boothman writes: “ The Bulletin has stated that
Alma Gray is Ada Baker’s daughter. Also that she is a
copyist of Alice Leamar, and a singer of objectionable
songs, such as ‘ Johnny Jones.’ I am her mother, the
wife of Alfred W. Boothman, myself better known by my
theatrical name of Ruth Gray. Alma did not sing
‘ Johnny Jones ’ and has no coarse and vulgar songs in
her repertoire. She imitates no one—she has sufficient
natural talent to obviate the necessity.”
“ Old Tom Haydon, in all his verdant juvenility, ap-
peared at Mackennal’s ‘ Show Sunday,’ accompanied by
ma Haydon, and Ethel, ‘ The Circus Girl,’ looking pale
and tired from overwork,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “ Ethel’s one ambition is to ‘ rest ’ Marie
Tempest and play ‘The Geisha,’ still the best draw in
London. The ex-Melb. amateur has pluck, talent and
confidence, and wisely handled should go further. Dark
Mabel Lane looked bright and lively, despite her nightly
appearance in the depressing, drawling curtain-raiser at
the Globe. Next week she is promoted to the ‘ piece ’
‘ The MacHaggis,’ by Jerome and Phillpotts, where
Weedon Grossmith gets the ‘ fat.’ Mrs. Phil. May and
Nellie Veitch were also present. The latter trips to the
native heath of her yearning and then returns to Victoria,
preliminary to a long and last farewell of the South.
Gorlitz, Hugh Glass, Leslie Norman and Frank Madden
were there with other Australian peripatetics, and cabbed
and chaperoned their fellow-exiles to Hilda Spong’s as
eve fell.”
Yosper, the long-haired newspaper-man
who was gaoled for so-called “ sedition ” at
Charters Towers by the Griffilwraith gang, is one
of the elected at Westralian polls. Yosper is a
man of no particular brains, but with imposing
appearance and splendid energy. He looks good
enough to be a democratic Napoleon, and has a
fine gift-o’-gab for the multitude. His past hasn’t
been too creditable —not altogether by his own
fault; but if he puts ice on his head and walketh
slowly and uprightly, W.A. holds for him a fine
political future.
The well-rigged Westralian elections—
with three-fourths of adult males disfranchised —
put the Forrest crowd in office again by a big
majority. The figures are—Ministerialists, 29 ;
Opposition, 8; Independent, B—with one result
to come. Politics on t’otherside no longer consist
wholly of Six-Family lickspittles : only half nowa-
days is lickspittling and half {mining swindle ;
but the great sandy province stews in its mournful
gravy just the same.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Totalisator dividends on two concluding days of
S.A.J.C. meeting:—£2 175.; £l3 75.; £9 7s. ; £l4 35.;
£2 10s.; £9 15s. ; £2 ss. ; £5 10s.; £8 10s. ; £2 Is. ;
£4 10s. ;£8 15s. Very fair prices considering that the
majority of the winners were “ dead birds.”
* * * *
The two last days of S.A.J.C. meeting at
Adelaide were even more successful than the opening
one, and on Cup-day no less than £15,167 passed through
the tote. Mora, the Cup-winner, is a three-year-old filly
by the lately-defunct Malua from White Star, a mare with
the best of running blood. Mora has been mercifully
treated as a two-year-old, and so is likely to train into
a sterling four-year-old. The erstwhile champion, Loyalty,
started first favorite for the Cup, but finished down the
course ; however, on Saturday last he won the Fisher
Stakes, beating a moderate field. The unreliable Staffa
wound up the meeting by winning the Totalisator Handi-
cap of 200sovs., carrying Bst. 81b. to victory in less time
than the Goodwood Handicap was run in, when he had
61b. less on his back and only managed to get third.
» * * *
Adelaide Cup was a comparative boil-over. Not
one of the heavily-backed cracks secured even a place,
and the trophy once more went to Victoria. Mora, a
nicely-bred mare, like Warpaint in ’96, was a comparative
outsider, the heavily-backed division being Acton (who
was interfered with), Surefoot, and Loyalty. Birksgate,
the second horse, nearly brought off a big coup for
John Crozier, an Adelaide owner, who bought it the
day before, and would have made a heavy win with the
tote odds at 20 to 1. £15,167 went through the machine.
* * * ♦
When days look darkest for pony-racing, new
devotees invariably turn up to keep the game alive.
Sundry doctors have now taken to the game in earnest,
both as owners and punters, and there is joy once more
amongst the gang of “ guns,” who were becoming quite
despondent and wondering where the next lot of “mugs”
was to come from. Some of the best ponies now running
about Sydney are owned by local medicos.
• * * *
Apropos M‘Elhone on the “tote.” The writer
saw a Sydney Stipendiary Magistrate, accompanied by a
lady, e n teflon e of the chief betting offices in Pitt-street,
last week, while the bobby on the pavement smole a broad
«mile.
* * * *
Mummell Boy, who has been on scratch for
some time past at the Brighton (Sydney) trots, was let
loose last week, scoring an easy win and a twelve-months’
“ send-up ” for “ reversal of form.” As Thb Bulletin has
so often pointed out, this sort of thing is more the fault
of the handicapper than the owner. No horse with a re-
cord like Mummell Boy should ever be let up on scratch,
no matter what his latter-day performances are like. Any
trotting man will tell you it is never safe to take liberties
with a horse who hold's a good record, however ancient.
• * • *
“Newhaven’s contradictory performances at
Randwick were easily explainable,” says the horse-repor-
ter for Melb. Age. Then he unwisely went on to explain :
“ The fact that the ground was different on each day
simply absolved those who were interested in the horse
from any charge of inconsistency ; and, besides, it is well-
known that Newhaven when raced on soft ground does
not perform as well as usual.” It is also well-known that
the ground wasn’t soft when the unsatisfactory animal got
badly beaten for the V.R.O. St. Leger; in fact it was
harder than when he won the Champion race, five days
later. The in-and-out running of Newhaven has been a
turf scandal, but not so great a scandal as the unwisdom
of the V.R.O. andA.J.C. stewards in allowing it to pass
without enquiry. Horsey men by the thousand have
discussed the Newhaven freaks, and found them inex-
plicable. The Age reporter’s nonsense is, however, easily
explained. He is clearly a foolish person, endeavoring to
soothe trainer Higinbotham’s feelings.
• • * •
Melb. : “ To-day I saw a Chinaman in charge
of two racehorses, with clothes on, at exercise, and he
was mounted on one. First time I have seen a Chinky in
the pigskin. Soon the rider of the Melb. Cup winner
will be bracketed mandarin blue jacket, gamboge sleeves
and face.”
* * * •
Semiramis, by the Grandmaster horse Gibraltar
out of the imported Security, won three races at Tam-
worth (N.S.W.) meeting. In Tam worth Cup she is
returned as having cut out the mile and a quarter in
2min. 9sec.—a big order with Bst. lib. up.
* * * *
Though much whispered about as a likely Birth-
day Cup winner, the imported Positano has not been
entered for that race, Mr. Hangar doubtless reserving him
for higher game. Tho’ but little raced in England, he
showed fair form at Kempton Park, running third in
City of London Breeders’ Foal Plate of 1179sovs. If
leniently treated, he should show up in either Caulfield
or Melb. Cup, provided he be not given “ a thorough
Cup preparation ” — i.e., provided he hasn’t his heart
galloped out on the training-track.
• * • •
Mr. Kelso, after his bad luck at A. J.C. meet,
soon struck form, as last Saturday he won Warwick Farm
Handicap with the Anniversary Handicap winner Tom,
who just managed to beat Rebel Queen after a good
struggle. The Chester horse, Projectile, was third, and
as he is rapidly recovering his former nick, he must
prove dangerous in the Birthday Cup. The N.Q. cham-
pion sprinter, Physician, ran twice at the meeting with-
out success, but he showed he could gallop, and will be
returned a winner ere long.
♦ * * *
“ Paris 111. started at 7 to 2, a good second
favorite, for the Northamptonshire Stakes, to finish fourth
with his 9st. 41b.,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent. “Turf experts prophesy his decline, and it
certainly looks as if his best days in England were over.
Pounamu, Maluma, Merman, Emmalea, Erl King, and
Bombshell 11. show little indication of refunding their
passage-money.”
* * * •
“Manifesto, the Irish ‘fancy,’won a fast-run
Grand National,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ much to the dismay of Gollan, Gibson,
Escott and Co., with whom Norton was the ‘snip.’
Daimio was not sent, and collapsed early in the second
round. He is to be nursed and schooled for the ’9B event,
as at present he can’t go fast enough to keep his jockey
warm. Norton fell over a mile from home, a disaster the
papers overlooked, and Timon, who had made the pace a
clinker, came down at the second-class fence. The time,
9min. 39sec., is not over lOsec. behind record. ’
* * * *
After unshipping Hickey in the English Grand
National, Norton completed the course “on his own,”
fencing in perfect style, tho’ ’tis doubtful if he would
have got so near the front if his rider had sat tight.
* * * *
First Mate, Wolumumbi, San Marco, Milkmaid,
Creme d’Or and Aurea appear a likely sextet for the
winner of the Royal Stakes, at S.T.C. Randwick meeting,
to spring from ; whilst Sam, Projectile, and Hindoo may
be expected to show prominently in the Birthday Handi-
cap. The up-country horse Pat, and Favor should manage
to account for the two Hurdle races, in which event
Cornet may also have a look in.
» * * »
Lacrosse teams from S.A. and N.S. Wales will
visit Melb. during July and play a round of interprovin-
cial matches there. The selection of an Australian team
to visit Canada, the home of the game, and probably Eng-
land, is almost certain to be mooted at the same time.
* * * •
Lacrosse has now caught on in Melbourne and
bids fair to knock out the Republican game of baseball.
There are 12 clubs going strong, and they put 15 teams
(eight senior and seven junior) of 12 each into the field
every Saturday. The fillip has been given by the M.C.C.
taking up the game in preference to baseball. Meanwhile
what is practically an M.C.C. baseball team is doing its
utmost to convince Yankees that the game is flourishing
in Victoria like a dry-grass fire.
The interprovincial eight-oar race, at Melb. on
Saturday last, fell an easy victory to Victoria, followed
home by the Westralian crew, composed chiefly of old
Vic. oarsmen. The N.S.W. Association stood out this
year owing to the inclusion in the Victorian eight of
a cash cyclist. N.S. Wales has given way in every
particular, barring the so-called cash-amateur. Although
it has never yet rowed a manual laborer in its crew, N.S.
Wales at the conference agreed to Victoria rowing a whole
crew of workmen if it felt so inclined, under which cir-
cumstances Victoria, having so much the thicker end of
the stick, behaved foolishly in insisting upon including a
cash cyclist in its crew.
• * • •
Westralia’s first aquatic distinction was gained
on Saturday, when “ Teddy” Powell carried off the ama-
teur sculling championship of Australia on the Lower
Yarra. The race was a sorry procession, the winner being
really the only finisher ; Dennis (Q.) was a quarter of a
mile behind, and Lindgren, the Vic. man, stopped at the
second mile. Powell won the last Australian championship
in Brisbane in ’95, when everyone thought the race a
moral for Slack, of Breakfast Creek. Powell was at
the time a Victorian, and he rowed in several Vic. repre-
sentative eights and was a member of the Albert Park
Club. For the past 18 months he has lived in Perth.
The order of the interprovincial contest — Vic-
toria (1), Westralia (2), and Queensland (3) —was only
what was expected from the day the visiting crews ap-
peared on the Yarra. The Westerners, however, quite
failed to pick up the condition they lost on the voyage
over, and the probabilities are that had they taken the
Yarra as fit as when they left, the finish would have been
a good battle. With a few weeks’ more coaching the
Westralians would be a better crew than many who have
won the interprovincial contest.
Lindgren, who represented Vic. in the sculls,
retains his amateur status in an amusing fashion. Riding
as a cash cyclist last year, he was eligible to continue
rowing as an amateur, provided he retired from the pro-
fessional track on 31st Dec. —vide the interprovincial
agreement. All the same, he started in a bike race for
money at Northcote sports. Called upon to explain,
Lindgren showed that he never entered for the race at
Northcote ; and the secretary of the sports meeting wrote,
saying that Lindgren was virtually a trespasser on the
track on that occasion. The law said “ entered” ; Lind-
gren had not entered, and so he remained a lily-white
amateur.
* * • *
Smith, the Melbourne billiardist who ran rings
round James, in Sydney, the other week, is to take 1000
points in 10,000 from Charlie Memmott, spot and push
barred.
* * * *
The Vic. cycling season proper finished on
Saturday with the Geelong meeting, where all the cracks
turned up. The pacing-wonder Body was the hero of the
day, winning the Five-mile Scratch from Walne and Mid-
dleton, and finishing second from his new mark of scratch
in the big handicap.
In the classic event, Body and Green went out
just after the first mile, and raised such a gap that, when
Walne tried to reduce the break, only A. A. Middleton,
Megson and Green were capable of hanging to the Queens-
lander ; the rest of the big field, including Bilmartin and
Porta, went out when the race was half over. A lap and
a-half from home Body started to move again, and, when
the bell went, he was still in front with Green, Walne,
Middleton and Megson tailing behind. When the last
rush came the Englishman was pocketed, and Walne’s
grand run was made a bit too late, the Maorilander finish-
ing a foot in front.
Body, on the same mark as Walne, Martin, and
Megson, was the only scratch man who qualified for the
final and the big handicap. Relph, on 15yd., helped him
considerably to win his heat, but, in the final, Body lost
ground coming round a dangerous corner, and young
A. C. Middleton, on 80yd., was able to keep ahead in the
run home.
Middleton, like Body, has come to the front
wonderfully during the last month, since the time he so
effectually beat Walne after training at St. Kilda. Three
tolerable handicaps in three weeks is not bad. Young
Middleton, like his big brother, has heaps of pluck and
strength, and he is far the more skilled in his management
of a machine.
The bad turn spoken of put young South Melb.
M‘limes into the second place of the 2nd and 3rd-class
handicap. He was riding splendidly, and had he not been
forced to ease up to save his neck he would have beaten
Cochrane in the run home. In the amateur event the
riders were very dicky at the corner, and one of their
number (Clartson) had a smash involving unconscious-
ness and a badly-chipped face.
• • • •
At St. Kilda, on Saturday, they interlarded
some record-breaking with the races. Relph went out
first for the quarter, but his chain gave way 100yds. from
home. His team saw the mishap and eased off, but they
nevertheless passed the line only 26sec. after the man
started. A. A. Middleton, the Dunolly miner, riding a
107 gear, then brought down the Australian time to 25sec.
dead.
Relph had a cut at the mile, and though he lost
the pace at one sharp turn, he caught his triplet again
and did the distance, so says the timekeeper, in lmin.
48sec. Several private watches, however, made the go
1.50.
It is doubtful whether the League will recognise
these records. They were certainly held under fair con-
ditions, and a quorum of the racing board was present
and gave leave. But, according to League rules, several
days’ notice must be given of any attempt at record-
breaking. So probably the L.V.W. will strike out Satur-
day’s times and let Megson’s 1.48 3-5 stand as Australia’s
best. But the rule is, in one respect, a very awkward
one: no cyclist can tell what the weather will be days
ahead.
* * • *
Tebbutt, who, by the way, teaches in a Baptist
Sunday-school, says he will not race for another two
years.
The first round of the N.S.W. senior football
competition started on Saturday last, when Randwick
beat Pirates, Wallaroos defeated Paddington, and Went-
worth beat University. If Saturday’s play is any cri-
terion, it looks as if Randwicks and Wallaroos will be the
pair to fight out the finals. There are plenty new men in
all the teams recruited from the juniors, but University,
losing as it does Abbott, Colquhoun, Waugh, and others,
looks on paper a very weak team.
* • * *
The opening day of the Vic. football season was
a pathetic travesty of similar occasions in the past, when
for a month before all Melbourne anxiously awaited the
first battles. This year nobody seemed to care a rap
whether the matches were played or not.
One sensible idea adopted by the money-grab-
bing “Football League,” which now possesses all the big
clubs of Melb., is the recognition of the “behind” in the
score. Formerly one club, by a stroke of luck, would
kick one goal and no behinds, while their opponents were
driving the ball about the posts all the afternoon without
being able to get it through. Now the game is decided
on points—six for a goal and one for a behind—which,
without discounting the value of accurate shooting, pre-
vents such miscarriages of sport as frequently took place
in the past.

Among the protected industries of Vic-

of success. This mammoth establishment em-
ploys upwards of 250 operatives, and practically
supplies the Southern province with sweets, to the
almost entire discomfiture of the foreign manufac-
turer. Now, Macßobertson seeks to extend his
domain, and is ambitious to cater for the tastes of
Federal Australia as well as for his native pro-
vince. A notable novelty turned out to perfection
by this firm is chewing-gum, a Yankee notion
which has recently become the vogue in Melb.*

Henssler, the lately-appointed Queensland
immigration lecturer, is unlikely to pull off his trip
to Germany and draw the honorarium after all.
German law prohibits a German from seducing
German subjects from their own country with the
object of settling them under a foreign flag.
They have a gruesome taste in the matter
of festivity at Brunner (M.L.). March 26 was the
anniversary of the mine explosion which caused
such hideous slaughter a year ago, and the Brun-
nerites observed the occasion as a holiday.
A far-back correspondent :
Longreach (W.Q.) is bidding- hard to beat Cairns for the
Chow primacy. Some of the hotels there have Chinese
cooks. Several of them buy their bread exclusively from
the Chinese bakery. Strangely enough, these pubs, are
mainly supported by white workers, but for whose thirst
seeking solace in this direction they would be compelled
to shut. Longreach has a Chinese cabinet-making fac-
tory, and these pretended “ union ” hotels are furnished
almost throughout with Chow furniture. Ilfracombe, in
the same district, has a white baker and a Chinese ditto.
It has only three hotels ; two of these patronise the
Chinaman. Yet the white workers seldom take their beer
at the other pub. Curious animal, the unionist.
At last Melb. Criminal Court Judge
Hodges questioned, as well he might, the wis-
dom of the Crown Law Department in bringing a
charge of manslaughter against a mother who had
“overlain” the baby in a drunken sleep, when
there was no question as to the genuineness of her
drunk. Hundreds of such cases occur in Great
Britain every year, and are disposed of by the
coroners, whereas if the charge of “criminal neg-
ligence ” were permissible the Society for the Pro-
tection of Infant Life would have a shot at the
delinquent every time. There’s no law to punish
a woman for sleeping soundly, whether from drink
or fatigue, and no means of proving that the infant
didn’t roll into a place where the mother was
likely to sleep on top of it in an ordinary sober
kind of way.
Dear Bulletin, —“ T.D.” on Australian
slang is a bit too sure about “barrack” and
“ borak.” The latter, he says, “is undoubtedly a
development” of the former. Briefly, to show a
doubt. “ Barrikin ” (noun) is old English cant for
words hard to follow or comprehend. From this,
mistaken for a participle, it is quite likely that
“barrack” may have been formed. But then
“ barrack,” in its present sense, is of very recent
use in Australia —a thing of 12 or 15 years, while
“to poke borak ” was old slang about Melbourne
40 years ago. Now a suggestion. At the time I
refer to “ borak ” was commonly used and under-
stood to be an aboriginal word for “no.” It may
or may not have been so, but it certainly was so
used both by blacks and whites. It may, of
course, have come there from Tasmania. Old
settlers could settle this. —-T.M.
X.Z. : “Prof. R. Semon, of Jena, in his
lately-published ‘ Australian Bush and Coral-
Sea-Coasts,’'denies that the echidna is an eater of
adult ants. Ants full-grown may be found inside
them, but these have got there by accident, when
the echidna, setting out after epicurean dishes,
starts to break down the hills to get at the
white succulent larvae, and pupse (so-called eggs).
Asserted also by him that if you set an echidna
straight at a teeming ant-track, it won’t look at
it. These assertions contradict past acceptances
considerably. The spectacled German is, how-
ever, commonly a much better scientific observer
than the average bushman, the one being trained
to observe, the other being an impressionist.”

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfalme Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should he no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the comer of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth

Mr. Button writes later:-“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill j his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies."

“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir,—Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered

improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well. —Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,

CXJREO
It transpires that Childers and other re-
maining members of Gladstone’s Cabinet were
really opposed to his scheme of Home Rule. Yet
they spoke for it in Parliament. Such is the uni-
versal political hypocrisy.
A Thames (M.L.) would-be suicide by
poison was pumped out just in time, and woke up
after a while in the hospital to find Policeman
M‘Phee, who had him in custody, at his bedside.
“Am I dead?” he asked. “You ar-r-re not,”
said Policeman M‘Phee ; “ you’re better.” Then
the unsuccessful corpse shed tears and wailed, “I
won’t see the Lord and all the little angels to-
night, after all.” How many people can show a
record like that for solid faith ?
In the return of Yic. railway “deadheads”
is the item “Executive Councillors—36.” These are
members of the Council, who are now neither
Ministers nor M’s.P., since the full strength of
the Executive is about a dozen. Among the 36 are
Frank Stuart, who was an honorary member of the
Munro Ministry for just half a year; “Jimmy”
Munro, now resting in suburban obscurity ; L. L.
Smith ; Matthew Davies ; “ Tommy ” Bent;
Howard Spensley, absentee owner of Royal
Arcade ; “ Tommy ” Loader; John Henry Brooke,
now journalising in Japan ; Edward Langton;
Dow; Townsend M'Dermott; Charles Gavan
Duffy; John Denniston Wood; Archibald
Michie ; and “Fish Creek ” Richardson. Of how
many of these can it be truly said that they have
earned from the country a free pass for life over
its railways ?
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection —
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
London contains a ‘ ‘ purveyor by Royal
Appointment to Her Majesty the Queen and the
Royal Family ”of “ perfumed butter. ” The butter
is made up in small pats and, wrapped in fine
muslin, is placed on a bed of rose-leaves in an
earthen jar and covered with another layer of
roseleaves. Then the jar, filled up with a solid
chunk of ice, is left in a refrigerator for ten hours
before using.
A record. An 8-year-old boy was struck
in the abdomen by a swinging-boat, and thrown
thereby into water 40 feet away. The abdomen
was opened, and the damage found to be such that
eleven feet (!) of the small intestine had to be cut
out. The cut ends were sewn together, and in 5
weeks the boy left the hospital cured. Fifteen
months later he was enjoying splendid health.
The Canadian Practitioner reports the case.
The S.A. authorities are digging vigor-
ously around the neighbourhood of Streaky Bay
for dead bodies, supposed to be planted (a la Glen-
brook corpse) by Joshua Beard. A few months
ago Joshua Beard went out kangaroo-hunting
with Walter Richards, and came back alone,
merely saying that Richards had gone off “ down
the main road. ” About a year earlier the same
individual went out kangaroo-hunting with Joseph
Mario, and then also he returned alone, saying
that Mario had gone to Westralia. This ten-
dency of Beard’s to go out with another man
and to return without him attracted no special
attention, but some of Richards’ property was
found in his possession, and he Was charged with
the familiar crime of “ goods reasonably supposed
to be stolen.” Still, there was no serious suspicion
of any buried corpse, but, at the trial, Beard un-
accountably blasted his chances by announcing
that Richards had accidentally shot himself, and
he had buried the defunct mate in the bush.
He also led the way to the grave, where it
was found that Richards’ wound was in the
back of the head. Then he got maundering about
the missing Mario, who, he alleged, had been
killed by a horse, and ended by showing his grave
also. The Streaky Bay mystery, coming so close
after the Glenbrook affair, sets one wondering
whether the Australian bard who writes about the
bushman’s lonely grave has ever begun to under-
stand how numerous that grave is, or who shunted
the bushman into it. And, considering that
“ Banjo’s ” Clancy hasn’t been heard of for quite
a long time, the writer rises to inquire what sort
of person he was last seen with.


“ So true a Sovereign lady
Ne’er ruled all hearts before 1
Rise up and praise Victoria’s days,
The glorious years three score.”
Purtell?—No. Knapp?—No. Zachariah Sut-
cliffe?—No. Neild?—No. Sir Edwin Arnold,

Gidyea scrubs smell horribly after rain,
as the bushman knows, but the Rev. Coalsofire,
fresh from Hingland, struck Gidyea station just
after a shower and detected what he called “ a
most offensive odah.” After asking everyone,
from the manager down to the cook’s “slushy,”
what the “ odah ” was, and getting the same reply,
“ no strange smell here,” he convinced himself he
was getting cancer in his nose, and cleared back
for the township next day.

A certain North Australian surgeon, so goes
the story, was consulted re a married-female ailment,
and with due solemnity he advised the immediate
performance of an operation. The “ knife ” alone
could cure the case, said the expert, so the deed
was done, and the loving husband paid a substan-
tial fee for the lady’s alleged salvation. Shortly
afterwards the convalescent took a trip abroad for
her health, and, daring her travels, was persuaded
to consult a famous specialist. This authority
promptly told his visitor that the operation she
had suffered in Australia was quite unnecessary,
also was calculated to do more harm than good.
The lady having written home a full account of her
interview with the foreign surgeon, her husband
tore round to the Australian operator, placed
the letter before him, and demanded an explana-
tion. The surgeon remained perfectly cool under
the attack. He read the letter whilst picking his
teeth, then remarked airily that the other surgeon
didn’t understand how the case had stood at the
time when the knife was applied. “If this man
had seen the state of my banking account six
months ago,” said the dauntless Australian prac-
titioner, “ he would have thought that two opera-
tions were necessary.” “ Off with the leg so
much for Buckingham ! ” Not that there was a
leg in question, or that Buckingham was the name
of the operating surgeon in this narrative.

“ COURIER ” BUILDINGS, QUEEN STREET, BRISBANE.
24 QUEEN STREET, MELBOURNE.
A Kangaroo-Hunt front Shingle-
Hut.
[FOR THEBULLETIN.]
We always looked forward to Sunday. It was
our day of sport. Once, I remember, we thought
it would never come. We longed restlessly for it,
and the more we longed the more it seemed to
linger.
A meeting of selectors had been held ; war
declared against the marsupial; complete anni-
hilation of him sought, and a hunt on a grand
scale arranged for this particular Sabbath. Of
course those in the neighborhood hunted the
kangaroo every Sunday, but “on their own,”
and always on foot, which had its fatigues.
This was to be a raid en masse and on horse-
back. The whole country side was to
assemble at Shingle Hut and proceed thence. It
assembled; and what a collection ! Such a
crowd ! such gear ! such a tame lot of horses !
and such a motley swarm of lean, lank, lame
kangaroo-dogs !
• * * *
We were not ready. The crowed sat on their
horses and waited at the slip-rails. Dogs trooped
into the yard by the dozen and proceeded to com-
mit acts of depredation. One pounced on a fowl ;
another lamed the pig; a trio put the cat up a
peach-tree ; one with a thirst mounted the water-
cask and looked down it, while the bulk of the
brutes trotted inside and disputed the right of
access to the safe with mother.
Dad loosed our three, and pleased they were to
feel themselves free. They had been chained up
all the week, with scarcely anything to eat. Dad
didn’t believe in too much feeding. He had had
wide experience in dogs and coursing at “home ”
on his grandfather’s large estates, and always
found them fleetest when empty. Ours ought to
have been fleet as locomotives.
Dave, showing a neat seat, rode out of the yard
on Bess, fresh and fat and fit to run for a king-
dom. They awaited Dad. He was standing
beside his mount, Farmer, the plough-horse, who
was arrayed in winkers with green-hide reins and
an old saddle with only one flap. He was holding
an earnest altercation with Joe. . . . Still they
waited. Still Dad and Joe argued the point. . . .
There was a murmur and a movement and much
merriment. Dad was coming ; so was Joe—
perched behind him, “double bank,” rapidly wip-
ing the tears from his eyes with his knuckles.
’ Hoorray ! They were off. Paddy Maloney and
Dave took the lead, heading for kangaroo country
along the foot of Dead Man’s Mountain and
through Smith’s paddock, where there was a low
wire fenoe to negotiate. Paddy spread his coat
over it and jumped his mare across. He was a
horseman, was Pat. The others twisted a stick in
the wires, and proceeded carefully to lead their
horses over. When it came to Farmer’s turn he
hesitated. Dad coaxed him. Slowly he put one
leg across, as if feeling his way, and paused again.
Joe was on his back behind the saddle. Dad
tugged hard at the winkers. Farmer was inclined
to withdraw his leg. Dad was determined not to
let him. Farmer’s heel got caught against the wire,
and he began to pull back and grunt —so did Dad.
Both pulled hard. Anderson and old Brown
ran to Dad’s assistance. The trio planted their
heels in the ground and leaned back. Joe became
afraid. He clutched at the saddle and cried
“Let me off!” “Stick to him!” said Paddy
Maloney, hopping over the fence, “ stick to him. ”
He kicked Farmer what he-afterwards called
“ a sollicker on the tail. ” Again he kicked him.
Still Farmer strained and hung back. Once more
he let him have it. Then—off flew the winkers
and over went Dad and Anderson and old Brown
and down rolled Joe and Farmer on the other side
°f the fence. The others leant against their
horses and laughed the laugh of their lives,
“ Worse ’n a lot of d—— jackasses,” Dad was heard
to sa Y- They caught Farmer and led him to the
Unee again. He jumped it and rose feet higher
than he had any need to, and had not old Brown
dodged him just when he did he would be a dead
n ? an now instead of the swell he is down in the
city—on a regular wage and nothing to do for it only
dodge about the streets after horses with a small
shovel and a broom in his hand. But Brown was
always a lucky dog.
* * * *
A little further on the huntsmen sighted a mob
of kangaroos. Joy and excitement. A mob? it
was a swarm ! Away they hopped. Off scrambled
the dogs and off flew Paddy Maloney and Dave —
the rust followed anyhow and at various rates of
speed.
That all those dogs should have selected and fol-
lowed the same kangaroo was sad and humiliating,
And such a waif of a thing, too ! Still they stuck
to afc . For more than a mile, down a slope, the
weedy marsupial out-paced them, but when it
came to the hill the daylight between rapidly
began to lessen. A few seconds more and all
would have been over, but a straggling, stupid old
ewe belonging to an unneighborly squatter darted
II P f rom the shade of a tree right in the way of
Maloney’s Brindle, who was leading. Brindle
always preferred mutton to marsupial, so he let
the latter slide and secured the ewe. The death-
scene was most imposing. The ground around
became strewn with small tufts of white wool,
There was a complete circle of eager, wriggling
canines—all jammed together, heads down and
tails elevated. Not a scrap of the ewe was visible,
Paddy Maloney jumped down and proceeded
vigorously to batter the brutes with a waddy. As
tl ie others arrived, they joined him. The dogs
were hungry, and fought for every inch of the
sheep. Those not laid out were pulled away, and
when old Brown had dragged the last one off by
the hlnd le S s all that . was left of that ewe was
four feet and some skin.
* * * *
Dad shook his head and looked grave—so did
Anderson. After a short rest they decided to
divide into parties and “work” the ridges. A
start was made. Dad’s contingent—consisting of
himself and Joe, Paddy Maloney, Anderson, old
Brown, and several others—started a mob. This
time the dogs separated and scampered off in all
directions. In quick time Brown’s black slut
bailed up an “ old man,” full of fight,
Nothing was more desirable. He was a
monster, a king kangaroo, and as he raised
himself to his full height on his toes and tail
he looked a formidable fiend a grand and
majestic demon of the bush. The slut made no
attempt to tackle him ; she stood off with her
tongue out. Several small dogs belonging to
Anderson barked energetically at him, even ven-
turing occasionally to run behind and bite his tail,
But, further than grabbing them in his arms and
embracing them, he took no notice. There he
towered, his head back and chest well out, await-
ing the horsemen. They came, shouting and
hurraying. He faced them defiantly. Anderson,
aglow with excitement, dismounted and aimed a
lump of rock at his head, which laid out one of
the little dogs. They pelted him with sticks and
stones till their arms were tired, but they might
just as well have pelted a dead cow. Paddy
Maloney took out his stirrup. “Look out!” he
cried. They looked out. Then, galloping up, he
swung the iron at the marsupial and nearly
knocked his horse’s eye out.
T, j j- . j tt j t j.l. i j
Dad was disgusted. He and Joe then approached
the enemy on Farmer. Dad carried a short stick,
The “ old man ” looked him straight in the face,
Dad poked the stick at him. He promptly grabbed
hold of it and a piece of Dad’s hand as well.
Farmer had not been in many battles—no Defence-
force man ever owned him. He threw up his head
and snorted and commenced a retreat. The kan-
garoo followed him up and seized Dad by the shirt.
Joe evinced signs of timidity. He lost faith in
Dad, and half jumping, half falling, he landed on
the ground and set out speedily for a tree. Dad
lost the stick, and in attempting to brain
the brute with his fist he overbalanced and
fell out of the saddle. He struggled to his feet
and clutched his antagonist affectionately by both
paws—standing well away. Backwards and for-
wards and round and round they moved. “Use
your knife!” Anderson called out advisedly, get-
ting further away himself. But Dad dared not
relax his grip. Faddy Maloney ran behind the
brute several times to lay him out with a waddy,
but each time he turned and fled before striking
the blow. Dad now thought to force matters, and
began kicking his assailant vigorously in the
stomach. Such dull, heavy thuds ! The kangaroo
retaliated, putting Dad on the defensive. Dad
displayed remarkable suppleness about the hips.
At last the brute fixed his deadly toe in Dad’s
It was an anxious moment, but the belt broke,
and Dad breathed freely again. He now acted
entirely on the defensive, but an aw r ful consciousness
of impending misfortune assailed him. His belt
was gone and—his trousers began to slip—slip—
slip ! He called wildly to the others for God’s sake
to do something. They helped with advice. He
yelled “ Curs ! ” and “ Cowards ! ” back at them.
Still, as he danced around with his strange
and ungainly partner, his trousers kept
slipping—slipping! For the fiftieth time and
more he glanced eagerly over his shoulder for
some haven of safety. None was near. And
then-oh, horror !-down slid the unmention-
ables, calmly and noiselessly. Poor Dad! He
was now at a disadvantage ; his leg-work was
it
free of them altogether But he couldn t-his
er 6 large. He took a lesson from the foe and
jumped—jumped this way and that way, and
rolled offTm
rolled off him It was a grotesque scene. The
small dogs displayed renewed and ridiculous
ferocity, often mistaking Dad for the marsupial.
himOn“heSf—Z
Twice he tripped. Heetaggered again p dt,wn he
was going — down, down —and down he fell ! But
at the same moment, and as though they had
dropped from the clouds, Brindle and five or six
other canines pounced on the “old man.” The
rest may be imagined.
» * * *
Dad lay on the ground to recover his wind, and
when he mounted Farmer again and silently
turned for home Paddy Maloney was trium-
phantly seated on the carcass of the fallen fiend
exultingly explaining how he missed the brute’s
head with the stirrup-iron, and claiming the tail.

STEELE RUDD.
smellbourne’s latest anonymous letter-writer shows
some knowledge of this wicked world in his (or her) deal-
ings with newly-wedded ladies. The letter, in thes*
cases, threatens to disclose pre-nuptial philanderings be-
tween the young bride and a married man. There’s
wisdom in the “ a married man ” assumption. Spinsters
are 80 likely to have indulged at least one little weakness
that sort before finding a legitimate victim.
THE MARRIED MAN.
Miss Maudie, when she answers “ Yes ”
To cousin Dick, will freely own
That half-a-dozen boys, or less,
Were privileged her hand to press
Ere first she cared for him alone.
She ticks them off, nor dreads his blame,
Upon her fingers with her fan ;
Such “ sweethearts ” give no cause for shame-
Mere pastime, all girls do the same.
But one boy Maudie does not name-
The Married Man.
m, „ ■ , , .
Th ? man who gripped her tight
And left her lips inflamed and rough,
Who blurred toe line twixt ‘wrong and
