The Old to the New.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
[“ Australia can only boast of two or three cities on its
Seaboard, the great portion of its interior being desert.”
—English globe-trotter in an English paper.]
Thus the little English critic standing on his
island ledge,
Careful not to jostle others —they might shove him
off the edge !
Seeing all Australia’s backblocks from an Orient
liner’s deck,
Where they’d damn well dress for dinner in the
middle of a wreck.
But behind the streets of Melbourne, and behind
the Sydney squares,
Are the plains flow’r-ribbed with crimson, and
the waste that never spares.
We can show that critic wonders in this white
isle walled with seas—
Granaries for all the ages billowing on a million
leas.
I have seen the Gippsland jinkers hauling paves
for London streets,
Heard the clarion of the top-saw —tasbcd of that
life the sweets ;
Watched the blackwood blossom melting into
honey for the bee ;
Seen the Leeuwin light lay tracks on all the mys-
t’ry of the sea ;
And the sun’s broad, blood red footways on old
Bogong’s ancient snow,
And the eaglehawk go wheeling to the Loddon
vales below ;
Heard the bellbird strike her hammer deep from
endless dew and musk,
And the bells of all the “workers” seeking pas-
ture in the dusk ;
Heard the creaking of the saddle as the pommel
gripped the “ tree,”
When we left the stony uplands for the marshes
of the sea;
Seen “the Promontory ” fishers lifting Singapore
and past,
Heard the crying of the cordage, and the music
of the mast;
Seen the mighty mallee country drowned in curl-
ing waves of grain,
Heard the chirping of the brake-block on the
backward loading wain ;
Felt the whipping of the Rip tide on the steps of
Melbourne’s halls,
And the lashing of the Pole wind on the rocks of
Otway’s walls;
Seen the Old Man Plain brim level with a flood of
moving wool,
And with life the sub-soil teeming, and with
wealth Australia full;
Seen the Darling River steamer with her naked
stack ablaze,
Heard her helm-gear groan and squeal in all the
devious water-ways,
Watched her snag-poles walk, and felt her shoot
the races of the flood,
With the freshet’s rush behind her and “ Adven-
ture ” in her blood ;
Seen old Sydney Harbor blushing in the opening
rose of dawn—
Gathered daisies on the Cooper from a hundred
leagues of lawn ;
Caught the giant groper off the sugar-barges at
Wardell,
Seen the knife flash through the sweet cane, oozing
sugar as it fell;
Watched the pearlers north of “ Thursday ” shake
their sails out to the dawn —
Played the game for lazy women—with a diver’s
life as pawn !
Seen the fireman of the coaster, and the trimmer,
black and stark,
Groping for the final bearings in the sobbing, oily
dark ;
Rocked off Hell-gate for a week, and with the
Rocks of Death a-lee,
Beat sev’n thousand miles of current and the reefs
of Reminie ;
Seen Macquarie’s convict-hell become a treasure-
house of mines,
Guarded by the water-tangle and the wild coast’s
sentry-lines ;
Seen the bush-fires clearing Zeehan, breathed the
southern wind a-wing,
Eaten with the men of Lyell, and the piners of
the King;
Hunted rubies at the Peak-height, where
M‘Donnell’s ranges lift
Up the veil of clouds upon them, and the cairns
of Ruby Drift;
Seen an ocean-big lake flashing back the sun’s
defiant eye,
Shining round a sapphired arch which otherwhere
is but a sky.
Seen the beche-de-mer boats fishing in the sludge
of Flat-top Bay,
And the teamsters on the Roper cram a week into
a day ;
Watched the ocean-gates at Wyndliam whence t.lie
West cyclone comes forth
Raced the buffalo at Arnheim's with the hunters
of the north.
Poor old England ! with your petty sneering at
these ‘ ‘ colonies ”
“Shall Be’s ” greater far than “Has Been!’’ —
new wine better than old lees ;
You, who gave away your people, when you closed
the fields of grain—
You, who outward drove your yeomen, cannot
have their strength again !
As our land is so our strength is—wide as
planets—broad as skies —
Every acre is a manling with a new strong giant’s
eyes!
From the Leeuwin to the Norman —cape on cape
and plain On plain—
From Dirk Hartog’s to the Smoky and West to
the Bight again,
Every wild shall have its city- every scrub shall
have its men—
Wide-World’s cream have we creating new the
mountain and the fen !
That you gave us, that we hold to—merged in us
your Strong and Bold !
And their new cry is “ Defiance —from the New
unto the Old ! ”
I O U., Westralia.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
A compositor in Brisbane Govt. Printing-
office tallied up his dockets, the other Saturday,
and wrote “ Domino ” after the number (the first
time for 13 years). He then went out to a selection
and was struck dead by a falling-tree.
Brisbane hospital records show that the
mortality in typhoid has fallen from 14 per cent,
in female cases to 6 per cent, since the adoption of
the cold-bath treatment; and in male cases from
16 per cent, to 9 per cent. ; and at Glebe (Sydney)
children’s hospital the diphtheria mortality (taking
an experience of 600 cases) has been reduced from
52 to 18 per cent, by the anti-toxin treatment.
Kate: An Australian Girl .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Bulletin, — You told me once, years ago, to “ send
you more gospel from my heathen land.” The accom-
panying is gospel—it is a lot truer than most of the gospel
you hear preached nowadays.— Note from Author.
Up to 15 or thereabouts Kate was very-
like the average farm-bred Australian girl. She
would shin up a tree after a bird’s nest with as
much ease as a boy, and would ride a horse bare-
backed as fearlessly. She would walk over the
ploughed ground barefoot, planting potatoes all
day without a murmur ; and would sit up half the
night husking corn in the old draughty barn.
But as her skirts grew downwards Kate took
more interest in herself. Gradually she got freed
from the thraldom of outdoor work, and by-and-by
managed to coax her father into buying her a
side-saddle. After that she had some excuse every
fine day for riding into the nearest township ; and
from a riding-skirt she rose to a tall hat and veil.
Then she affected a lisp ; and if a young man were
near found it impossible to get on or off her horse
without assistance. She also developed a great
love for novels of the Young Ladies’ Jouknal
style, and could often be found lolling about
on the old husk-stuffed sofa at home en deshabille,
reading the literature that has ruined so many
Australian belles, while her mother was at the
wash-tub, or feeding the calves and pigs.
In spite of all this, and although she was red-
haired, and freckled, and vainly tried to hide the
freckles with paint and powder, Kate had found a
very soft part in my heart (or head), but luckily,
or unluckily, I was sent away from that district
before matters had gone too far.
* * * *
After 12 years’ absence I called to see Kate
the other day. She married the other fellow after
I left, and has four “kids,” as she calls them.
Her “ old man,” as she terms him, is a well-to-do
farmer, and owns four farms.
It was 10 a.m. ; Kate was in the kitchen. The kit-
chen is also the dining room, and the table was still
‘ ‘ set ” for breakfast, though that meal had been eaten
some hours before. The table-cloth had about two
dozen holes and rents in it, and was unspeakably
dirty. There wasn’t a whole cup, saucer, or plate
on the table; the cups that had handles had gaps,
and the cups that had no gaps were cracked.
Kate greeted me with “ a hundred thousand
welcomes ; ” wiped her sticky hands on her dress
to shake hands with me ; and bade me walk straight
in and take a chair. With the exception of two
long stools under the table, the only thing like a
chair I could see was a greasy block of wood about
a foot high placed alongside the fireplace. The
lisping, skittish Kitty I had known 12 years ago
was now dressed in an old wincey frock trimmed
with red braid ; no apron ; and a pair of boots from
which the soles were making great efforts to part
company—in the effort to do so displaying Kate’s
toes (she had no stockings on) as black as a coal-
miner’s.
Her hair was plastered with grease, and twisted
into a knot at the back of her head. Her dress,
especially the front from the level of the hips
down, was covered with grease and flour, but as it
was Kate’s towel and wash-basin combined that
was easily accounted for. Kate’s hands and arms
were covered with a mixture of flour, eggs, and
butter ; for she had just started to make a plum-
pudding for dinner and was not using a spoon to
stir the ingredients. She had a patch of yellow
on one side of her mouth and some greasy marks
on the other, showing that she had lately had a
meal of eggs and bacon.
♦ ♦ • *
I sat down, while Kate resumed her stirring of
the sticky mass that was destined to be a pudding •
but she had hardly got fairly started when the
knot at the back of her head uncoiled itself and
fell ker-flop over her shoulder, about six inches at
the end sticking into the pudding. Kate rubbed
her doughy fingers up and down once on the front
of her dress; rolled the disorderly hair up again
into the same old knot ; and resumed the pudding
operations. By-and-by she left the room for a
minute, and returned with two dirty pillow-slips
into one of which she scraped (with her fingers!
the pudding, and bundled it into the boiler. Then
she washed up the breakfast cups, &c., in the pud
ding-dish, which was still covered with the yellow
slimy dough; and wiped them dry on the other
pillow-slip.
I had promised to stay and have dinner but
long before dinner-time came—well, I didn’t feel
very well. About 12 o’clock, Joe, Kate’s husband
arrived ; and inquired “ Has my pretty Pollv
got the tucker ready yet?” Alas for the pa-
tience and resignation of Man—or had Kate
married a mere hog?) At dinner, Joe so per-
sistently begged me to “wire in,” and “buck
up -’ for some more, that, as I couldn’t eat anv
meat, vegetables, or pudding, I asked for some
bread and butter. Kate at once took the saucer
from under her cup and went to the butter-' 1 kag ”
in the corner, from which she scooped out with
her fingers a lump of butter, patting it smooth on
the saucer. Then she wiped her fingers on her
dress, and begged me to eat.
* * • •
When I was bidding them good-bye, Kate said
I must take a bottle of her honey. She said I
mustn’t notice a little dirt in it, for the kerosene-
tin in which it had been kept fell off the shelf on
to the floor the other day. I took the honey but
when crossing the river in the ferry-boat it some-
how slipped over the stern in mid-stream, where
it sank with all my old, fond recollections of
Kitty.
KATE'S CHAP.
From Nansen’s Arctic diary “ I have
weighed all the dogs, and have come to the con-
clusion that we can feed them on each other and
keep going for about 50 days, having, in addition
to this, dog provisions for about 30 days.”
It is stated now that Jamieson’s raid into
the Transvaal came to grief, like many other
schemes, through the insidious influences of
drink, Two men were detailed to cut the tele-
graph wires. One kept sober and did his job
effectually. The other, overcome by alcohol, sank
to the mental level of the beasts that per-r-rish
and thought he was cutting the main telegraph
line to Pretoria, while in reality he only hacked a
section out of a farmer’s wire fence. Consequently
news of the raid got through, and Oom Paul’s sol-
diers were lying in wait for Jameson at Door-
knop.
A Record for a Sovereign. —Hordern’s
in Pitt-street sovereign blankets break a record
for sterling value, and the fact that they are for-
warded without extra cost to any town in New
South Vales should induce residents in the
country districts of New South Wales to send for
a pair. Hordern Brothers, Government Con-
tractors, 203, 205, 207, 209, 211, Pitt-street (only)
Sydney. Telegraphic address simply ; “Hordern
Brothers.”


HOW TWO BEAUTIFUL CHILDREN
Were Rescued from Disease, and Restored to Health
and Happiness by Ayer s Sarsaparilla.
m,

S/ M
rU
Throughout the world Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is noted as a blood purifier
and a tonic and invigorator. Testimonials to its worth are to be numbered
by the thousands, and come from all the four quarters of the globe. One
of the latest is from Miss Mary Maguire, a well-known Philadelphia
business woman, who has two nephews, both aged five years, Elliott
Maguire and Walter Kester. Out of gratitude for the cure of their
diseases by Ayer’s Sarsaparilla she sends us their portraits, which we
reproduce above, and writes :
“ I can safely say that Ayer’s Sarsaparilla has saved those children much agony and
perhaps their lives. Since they were horn, not one dollar in doctor’s fees has been spent upon
them, and the only medicine they have taken has been the Sarsaparilla. They first began on
it two years and a half ago. They hadn’t been strong, but were never exactly sick till then.
It was early in the spring and both children, who are cousins of almost the same age, fell ill
at the same time. Elliott’s trouble was chiefly in his stomach at first. He had violent indi-
gestion, and at such an early age that was something alarming. Then eczema made its
appearance. These unhealthy conditions were largely hereditary. The little boy’s skin
began to blotch up and eruptions made their appearance. With Walter it was different. His
arms and the upper part of his legs were badly swollen and the veins seemed to stand out like
purple cords. He also had stomach trouble, which was inherited, and it is pretty hard to get
medicine to cure inherited diseases. However, I had seen Ayer’s Sarsaparilla advertised to
cure such cases and I got a bottle of it, and gave it to both boys. The first bottle produced
a really remarkable change. It was wonderful. Then I began a regular Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
treatment. I gave the medicine to them three times a day. The blood troubles disappeared
and their skin cleared up splendidly. Then their stomachs got into condition again. Since
that time I have given it to them regularly every spring. They have got so used to taking it in
that season that when I miss a dose they say: ‘Auntie, you forgot our Ayer’s.’ They have
never had any other medicine whatever, and that alone has cured them and made them well,
strong and hardy. I consider the Sarsaparilla a wonderful medicine.”
